The Third Proof: Infinite Regress of Individuation
Presents the author's argument against universals being individuated by an additional determination, leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third proof against universals being individuated by an additional determination, and why it leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Individuation cannot be added to a universal without causing infinite regress.

This lesson presents a key argument from the commentary on Ibn Sina's 'Uyun al-Hikma' against the view that universals become particular by an additional determination. The argument shows that if individuation were added to a universal essence, it would itself need individuation, leading to an infinite regress. This establishes a foundational point about the nature of universals and particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a reference to further objections in another book.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the conditional statement at the beginning: if humanity is a universal, then individuation is additional to the essence. Understand what this means.
The argument assumes a certain definition of universality.
3
Trace the reasoning to infinite regress
Follow the logic: because individuation itself would be a quality of a universal essence, it would need another individuation, and so on. This leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
The impossibility of infinite regress is a standard argument in Kalam.
4
Note the context and objections
The author mentions that there are many objections discussed in 'Al-Matalib al-Aliya'. Understand that this proof is part of a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد شرح المؤلف كتاب عيون الحكمة لابن سينا. وقال في شرحه في الفصل السابع ما نصه: الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت الإنسانية كلية لكان التعين زائدا على الماهية . وذلك محال . لأن التعين من حيث إنه تعين يكون أيضا صفة ماهية كلية، وكان يجب افتقاره الى تعين آخر. ولزم التسلسل وهنا وجوه كثيرة في الإشكالات ذكرناها في الكتاب المسمى : المطالب العالية إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن الشيخ - ابن سينا - شرع في هذا الموضع في بيان أن الصورة الذهنية، كيف تكون كلية؟ فذكر فيه وجهين . . . الخ (۱).
And the author commented on the book Uyun al-Hikma by Ibn Sina. He said in his commentary in Chapter Seven what follows: The Third Proof: If humanity were a universal, then individuation would be additional to the essence. And that is impossible. Because individuation, insofar as it is individuation, would also be a quality of a universal essence, and it would necessarily need another individuation. And an infinite regress would follow. And there are many aspects of objections that we have mentioned in the book called Al-Matalib al-Aliya. If you have understood this, we say: Indeed, the Sheikh - Ibn Sina - began in this place to explain how the mental form becomes universal. He mentioned two aspects... etc.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the view that universality is a property of the essence itself, taken from the author's commentary on Ibn Sina. The argument proceeds: If humanity (as a universal) were a universal, then the individuation of a particular human would be something added to the essence (mahiyya). But that is impossible because individuation itself, being a determination, would itself be a quality of a universal essence and would require another individuation, leading to an infinite regress. The author notes that many objections are detailed in his book Al-Matalib al-Aliya.

Key Terms
التعين
الماهية الكلية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this argument is about physical accidents rather than metaphysical individuation.
Correction: The argument concerns the metaphysical individuation (ta'ayyun) of a universal essence, not physical accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would be the consequence if humanity were a universal and individuation were additional to the essence?
A) Individuation would be unnecessary
B) Individuation would be impossible
C) It would lead to an infinite regress
D) Humanity would not be a universal

Key Takeaway
The third proof demonstrates that universality cannot be a property of the essence because it would necessitate an infinite regress of individuations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you consider essential to your identity—perhaps a role, a label, or a trait. According to this argument, if that identity is merely added to your essence, it would require something else to define it, leading to an infinite regress. Where does your chain end? Is your identity grounded in something that is not added but is truly your essence? How does this affect the way you view yourself before Allah?
?
The argument shows that adding individuation to a universal leads to impossibility. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes try to add deeds or accolades to define your faith, as if they were additional requirements for your identity as a believer? What would it mean to rest in the essence of being a servant of Allah, without adding extra conditions? Can you identify one 'added' thing you rely on that you now see as unnecessary?

Classification of Beings and Proof of the Necessary Existent's Attributes
The author classifies beings into four types and proves the Necessary Existent's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational classification of existing things into four categories and how the author proves that God alone is the Necessary Existent who is effective without being affected.

Core Idea
Existing things are rationally divided into four types; only God is purely effective.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification in Islamic theology: the rational division of all existing things based on whether they affect or are affected. The author then uses this classification to pinpoint the Necessary Existent (God) as the only being that affects without being affected, and provides a proof for His independence in all attributes. Understanding this classification is key to grasping the uniqueness of God's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of beings
The passage begins by stating that existing things are rationally divided into four categories. Read the classification carefully and note each category.
The four categories are based on the ability to affect (effect) and be affected.
2
Identify which category applies to God
The author identifies the first category—that which affects and is not affected—as God. Consider why this must be the case.
This is because God is the Necessary Existent, independent of all others.
3
Follow the proof for God's attributes
The passage then provides a proof that God's necessity implies He is necessary in all His attributes, both positive and negative. Trace the argument that if His essence were sufficient for an attribute, it would be eternal; if not, it would depend on another, contradicting necessity.
This proof establishes that God does not accept effect from anything else.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that God is unique and independent. Think about how this classification helps in understanding God's oneness and self-sufficiency.
This is a key argument in Islamic theology for tawheed (monotheism).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم ان الموجودات بالنسبه العقلية على اربعه اقسام لانها اما ان یکون موثرا ولا يتأثر بوجه من الوجوه واما ان يقبل الأثر ولم يؤثر واما ان يكون مؤثرا ومتأثرا واما ان لا يؤثر ولا يتأثر فهذه اقسام الأربعة لان معها ما لا يخلو الأول وما الذي يؤثر ولا اثر البتة؟ نهر اله سمند و تعالى والدليل عليه انه واجب الوجود لذاته وكل ما هو واجب الوجود لذاته فإنه واجب الوجود في جميع صفاته السلبية والثبوتية والدليل عليه أن ذاته إن كانت كافيه في حصول ذلك الإيجاب او ذلك السلب لزم ان يدوم ولزم الاعجاب و ذلك السلب بدوام دانه محسند منبع و تقوقع المعرفي مني من صفاته وان لم تكرذ انه كانيه في حصواغ لك الإيجاب و ذاك الله خان موقفه علی حصہ ان ایک الإيجاب وذلك السلات والموقوف على الفرع موقوف على الغير والموقوف على غير مارند: به نواجب الوجود البداية مكن الرجود الدانه مناخات مست از المية احاله دو دلداته وواحد الوعول في كل صفاته الإيجابية والسلسة وكل ما كان لذلك ببولا تقبل الإتو من الخير الله وأساسيان انه موتو في غيره مهرانه ثبت انان با عدا الموجود الواحد تاند ما لذاته و يلا مكه الذاته فانه لا يوجد لا با بمباران الاحب لانه اما برا مقدار بنر باسته است از المحنة و مالي برفی دال ما سران وانه لا يقبل الله الانوس في مواد فرو من حسینه انه مستایا وجود این جميع جثمانه الثبوتية والسلبية قاسم نفسه و شربت از کل ما سواه را با به بودن کارهه
Know that existing things, with respect to reason, are of four divisions: either they are effective and not affected in any way, or they receive effect and do not effect, or they are effective and affected, or they neither effect nor are affected. These are the four divisions because they include what is not free from the first. And what is that which effects and does not effect at all? He is God, the Exalted. The proof for it is that He is necessary of existence by Himself, and whatever is necessary of existence by Himself is necessary of existence in all His attributes, both negative and positive. The proof for that is that if His essence were sufficient for the occurrence of that affirmation or that negation, then it would necessarily be permanent, and that affirmation and that negation would be permanent with the permanence of His essence. . . . And if it were not that His essence is sufficient for the occurrence of that affirmation and that negation, then His essence would be dependent for the occurrence of that affirmation and that negation on another, and what is dependent on another is dependent on something else, and what is dependent on something else is not necessary of existence by itself. So it is established that His essence is necessary of existence by itself, and that it is impossible that it be dependent on another, and that He is one and unique in all His attributes, positive and negative. And everything that is like that does not accept effect from anything else. . . .
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. The author first divides existing things rationally into four categories: (1) that which affects and is not affected, (2) that which is affected but does not affect, (3) that which both affects and is affected, (4) that which neither affects nor is affected. He then identifies the first category as God, the Necessary Existent. The proof proceeds: God is necessary of existence by Himself, and whatever is necessary by Himself is necessary in all His attributes—both negative (like not being affected) and positive (like existence). The argument is that if His essence were sufficient for an attribute, that attribute would be eternal. If not sufficient, it would depend on another, contradicting necessity. Thus God is independent and does not accept effect from anything else. The passage ends with a statement that God is the source of all existence.

Key Terms
موجودات
موثر
متأثر
واجب الوجود
صفات سلبية
صفات ثبوتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the classification implies multiple independent agents or that other beings can also be unaffected.
Correction: The author proves that only God is truly effective without being affected, because only the Necessary Existent can be self-sufficient in all attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, into how many rational divisions are existing things classified?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
God alone is the Necessary Existent who is effective without being affected, and this is proven by His self-necessity implying independence in all attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God alone is effective and unaffected, would you still fear the opinions of others more than His judgment? What would change in your daily decisions if you lived with that conviction?
?
The proof shows that God's attributes are necessary and not dependent on anything. Is there any attribute you attribute to God—like mercy or justice—that you secretly doubt is always present? Why?
?
You claim God is the only true cause. Reflect on a recent moment when you felt completely in control. Did you act as if you were the independent cause? How can you reorient that belief today?

Bodily Pleasures Are Base, Spiritual Pleasures Are Superior
The author presents several proofs that spiritual happiness is superior to bodily happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural proofs that establish spiritual happiness as superior to bodily happiness.

Core Idea
Spiritual pleasures are enduring and noble; bodily pleasures are fleeting and base.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a series of compelling arguments for the superiority of spiritual happiness over bodily pleasures. The author uses reason, observation of human nature, and divine revelation to demonstrate that while bodily pleasures are shared with animals and quickly fade, spiritual joys elevate the soul and are eternal. Let us examine these proofs step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument about animal participation
Notice how the author uses induction to show that animals surpass humans in bodily pleasures, indicating baseness.
2
Find the second proof: intuitive and inductive evidence
The author argues that pursuing bodily pleasures pulls the soul from the spiritual realm to the bestial.
3
Consider the third contrast: perishable vs permanent
Bodily pleasures are quick to pass, while spiritual pleasures are secure from annihilation.
4
Examine the fourth proof from human disposition
The author notes that society honors those who are moderate in bodily desires and despises those who indulge excessively.
This leads to the conclusion that bodily happinesses are base.
5
Read the scriptural evidence and final conclusion
The author quotes the Quran and hadith to reinforce the superiority of spiritual happiness.
The lesson ends with the clear statement that spiritual happiness is superior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السعادات الجسمانية خسيسة وأقل ما فيها أن الحيوانات الخسيسة تشارك الإنسان فيها بل الاستقراء يدل على أن تلك الحيوانات الخسيسة أقوى وأكمل في جانب تلك اللذات من الإنسان. وأيضا فالحدس والاستقراء يدلان على أن الخوض في جلب تلك اللذات يجذب النفس من أعالي عالم الأرواح المقدسة إلى أسافل عالم البهيمية. وأيضا فهذه اللذات سريعة الانقضاء والانقراض، واللذات الروحانية آمنة من الزوال مصونة عن الفناء. وأيضا فالاستكثار من اللذات الجسمانية مشهود عليه بفطرة جميع الخلق أنه خسيس فإن الانسان الذي يكون كل أوقاته مصروفا إلى الأكل والوقاع يكون محكوما عليه عند كل أحد بخساسة الذات ودناءة الهمة وعلى أنه بهيمة محضة. وأما الانسان الذي يعتقد فيه التقليل من هذه الأحوال فإن طبع كل عاقل يحمله على تعظيمه والاعتراف له بعلو الدرجة وكمال المنقبة. ولذلك فإن العوام من الخلق إذا اعتقدوا في إنسان قلة الرغبة في الأكل والشرب والنكاح اعتقلوا فيه كونه مستوجبـا للتعظيم والخدمة وعدوا أنفسهم بالنسبة إليه كالعبيد إلى الأرباب، وكل ذلك يدل على أن هذه السعادات الجسمانية خسيسة نازلة فأما السعادات الروحانية فإنها باقية دائمة عالية شريفة تجذب النفس من حضيض البهيمية إلى أوج الملكية، ومن ظلمات عالم الأجسام إلى أعالي عوالم المقدسات المطهرات. فلهذه البراهين القاهرة، قال في الكتاب الإلهي : والباقيات الصالحات خير عند ربك (۱) وقال صاحب الوحي والشريعة حكاية عن رب العزة تعالى: (أنا جليس من ذكرني) (۳) فقد ظهر بما ذكرناه أن السعادات الروحانية أفضل من السعادات الجسمانية.
Bodily happinesses are base, and the least of what is in them is that base animals share with man in them; rather, induction indicates that those base animals are stronger and more perfect in the domain of those pleasures than man. Also, intuition and induction indicate that engaging in bringing those pleasures pulls the soul from the heights of the world of holy spirits to the depths of the world of bestiality. Also, these pleasures are quick to pass and perish, while spiritual pleasures are secure from cessation and preserved from annihilation. Also, seeking abundance of bodily pleasures is witnessed by the innate disposition of all creation as base, for the man whose every time is spent in eating and copulation is judged by everyone as base in essence and low in aspiration and as a pure beast. And as for the man in whom one believes to be moderate in these states, the nature of every rational person carries him to venerate him and acknowledge his high rank and perfect merit. Therefore, the common people, if they believe of a man that he has little desire for eating, drinking, and marriage, they consider him deserving of veneration and service and count themselves in relation to him as slaves to lords. And all that indicates that these bodily happinesses are base and low. But as for spiritual happinesses, they are enduring, perpetual, high, noble, pulling the soul from the abyss of bestiality to the peak of angelic-ness, and from the darknesses of the world of bodies to the heights of the worlds of holy purified things. So for these overwhelming proofs, He said in the divine Book: 'But the enduring good deeds are better with your Lord' (18:46). And the possessor of revelation and law related from the Lord of Might: 'I am the companion of whoever remembers Me.' So it has become evident from what we have mentioned that spiritual happinesses are superior to bodily happinesses.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rational and scriptural proofs for the superiority of spiritual happiness over bodily happiness. The author begins by stating that bodily pleasures are base, and the least evidence is that base animals share them; indeed, animals often surpass humans in strength and completeness of these pleasures. Secondly, engaging in these pleasures drags the soul from the lofty spiritual realm down to the baseness of bestiality. Thirdly, bodily pleasures are fleeting and perishable, whereas spiritual pleasures are permanent and secure. Fourthly, human innate disposition witnesses that a person who indulges excessively in food and sex is considered base, while one who is moderate is venerated. The author then cites the Quranic verse 'but the enduring good deeds are better with your Lord' and the hadith 'I am the companion of whoever remembers Me' as divine confirmations. The conclusion is that spiritual happiness is superior to bodily happiness.

Key Terms
سعادات جسمانية
سعادات روحانية
لذات
بهيمية
ملكية
براهين قاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one indication that bodily pleasures are base?
A) They are shared with animals.
B) They are eternal.
C) They are spiritual.
D) They lead to angelic-ness.

Key Takeaway
Spiritual happiness is superior to bodily happiness because it is enduring, elevates the soul, and is recognized by human reason and divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have free time, do you instinctively seek a bodily pleasure (like food or entertainment) or a spiritual one (like prayer or knowledge)? What does your choice reveal about what you truly value?
?
The author says that people instinctively venerate someone moderate in bodily desires. Is your life such that others would see you as worthy of respect for your self-control, or do you indulge in ways that lower your standing? What specific change can you make this week?
?
If spiritual pleasures are secure from annihilation while bodily pleasures vanish, what investments are you making in the enduring pleasures that will last beyond this life? Name one action you will take today to prioritize spiritual over bodily happiness.

Inner Joy from Spiritual Knowledge
Author argues that spiritual knowledge increases inner joy and strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the experiential argument that spiritual knowledge increases inner joy and strength, and that all happiness is connected to it, as supported by the Quran.

Core Idea
Spiritual knowledge directly fuels inner joy and spiritual strength.

This lesson presents the third proof for the honor of spiritual knowledge. The author draws on direct human experience: the more deeply a person immerses in knowledge of God, the greater their inner delight and spiritual power. This observation is then reinforced by a Quranic verse that links remembrance of Allah to peace of heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the parallel structure: increasing immersion leads to increasing joy, and vice versa.
The author uses a clear cause-and-effect relationship.
2
Identify the key experiential claim
The author claims that the perfect human (al-insan al-kamil) observes within himself that greater immersion in spiritual knowledge brings greater delight, strength, and joy.
3
Contrast the opposite case
Note that when immersion is less, the spiritual states are the opposite. This reinforces the argument by showing a direct correlation.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
The author cites Quran 13:28 to show that this experiential truth aligns with divine revelation: remembrance of Allah brings rest to hearts.
This combines rational observation with scriptural confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث في بيان شرف هذا العلم : إن الانسان الكامل يجد من نفسه أنه كلما كان اشتغراق روحه في هذه المعارف أكمل، وكان خوضه فيها أعظم وانجذابه إليها أتم، وانقطاعه عما سواها أوفى كان ابتهاجه بذاته أفضل، وقوة روحه أكمل، وفرحه بذاته أوفى . وكلما كان الأمر بالعكس كانت الأحوال الروحانية والآثار النفسانية بالعكس مما ذكرناه وكل ذلك يدل على أن كل السعادات مربوطة بهذه العلة وكل الكمالات والخيرات طالعة من هذا الافق . كما قال في الكتاب الإلهي : ألا بذكر الله تطمئن القلوب) (۳)
And the third aspect in explaining the honor of this science: That the perfect human finds in himself that whenever his spirit's immersion in these knowledges is more complete, and his entering into them is greater, and his attraction to them is more perfect, and his detachment from other than them is more complete, then his delight in himself is better, and the strength of his spirit is more complete, and his joy in himself is more perfect. And whenever the matter is the opposite, the spiritual states and psychic effects are the opposite of what we mentioned. And all that indicates that all happinesses are tied to this cause, and all perfections and goods arise from this horizon. As He said in the divine Book: 'Verily, in the remembrance of Allah do hearts find rest' (13:28).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is the third proof for the honor of this science. The author describes an experiential observation: the perfect human (al-insan al-kamil) notices that the more his spirit is immersed in these knowledges (i.e., knowledge of God and spiritual matters), the greater his delight in himself, the stronger his spirit, and the more perfect his joy. Conversely, when immersion is less, the opposite occurs. This shows that all happiness is connected to this cause (i.e., spiritual knowledge) and that all perfections and goods emerge from this horizon. The author cites the Quranic verse 'Verily, in the remembrance of Allah do hearts find rest' as confirmation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between immersion in spiritual knowledge and inner joy?
A) They are unrelated.
B) Greater immersion leads to greater joy.
C) Greater immersion leads to less joy.
D) Joy is only from worldly things.

Key Takeaway
Deep immersion in spiritual knowledge directly increases inner joy and spiritual strength, and this is confirmed by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When did you last feel a genuine surge of inner joy during your spiritual practice? Was it when your immersion was deep or shallow? What prevents you from fully immersing in the remembrance of Allah?
?
Look at your daily routine: what takes up most of your mental energy? If all happiness is tied to this cause, what does your current state of joy say about your spiritual investment? Will you increase your share of spiritual knowledge from today?
?
You are the 'perfect human' in potential—do you experience the promised delight and strength? If not, why? Are you willing to detach from distractions and enter fully into the ocean of divine knowledge, knowing that your inner peace depends on it?

Proof that Self-Knowledge is the Clearest Knowledge
The author provides a proof that self-knowledge is the clearest knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why self-knowledge is considered the clearest and most fundamental knowledge, and how it precedes all other knowledge including self-evident truths.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is the precondition for all other knowledge, making it the clearest.

The author argues that before we can know anything else, we must know our own essence. This lesson presents the proof that self-knowledge is the foundation of all knowledge and therefore the clearest of all sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: the author begins with a conditional statement and then invokes a well-known principle.
2
Understand the key argument
The author argues that knowledge of anything is conditioned by knowledge of one's own essence. Identify the two premises: (a) every assent is preceded by a concept, and (b) the condition is prior in rank to the conditioned.
The conclusion that self-knowledge precedes even self-evident truths is derived from these premises.
3
Reflect on why self-knowledge is the clearest
Since self-knowledge is a condition for all other knowledge, including clear self-evident truths, it must itself be even clearer. Ask yourself: How does this align with your own experience of knowing?
This reflection helps internalize the philosophical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذلك فإنه يقول : أدركت هذا الشيء وعرفته، إلا أن علمه بكونه عالما بذلك الشيء، مسبوق بعلمه بذاته المخصوصة (لأن من لا يعلم ذاته، كيف يمكنه أن يحكم عليها بكونها عالمة بذلك المعلوم)؟ وكذلك فإنهم قالوا كل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور، ومن الظاهر أن الشرط سابق بالرتبة على المشروط وهذا يدل على أن علم كل أحد بأي معلوم كان مشروط بعلمه بذاته المخصوصة ومسبوق بعلمه بذاته المخصوصة فيثبت أن علم كل أحد بذاته سابق على علمه بكل ما يغاير ذاته سواء كان ذلك العلم من البديهيات الجليات، أو من الكسبيات والسابق على جملة الجليات أولى بكونه جليا بديهيا. فثبت بهذا البرهان أن علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة، أجلى العلوم وأجلها وأظهرها وأقواها .
Thus he says: I have perceived this thing and known it, except that his knowledge of his being knowledgeable of that thing is preceded by his knowledge of his specific essence (because he who does not know his own essence, how can he judge that it is knowledgeable of that known thing)? Likewise, they said: Every assent is preceded by a concept, and it is apparent that the condition is prior in rank to the conditioned. This indicates that every person's knowledge of any known thing is conditioned by his knowledge of his specific essence and preceded by his knowledge of his specific essence. Thus it is established that every person's knowledge of his own essence is prior to his knowledge of everything other than his essence, whether that knowledge is from the self-evident clear matters or from acquired matters, and that which is prior to the totality of clear matters is more deserving of being clear and self-evident. Thus it is established by this proof that every person's knowledge of his specific essence is the clearest of sciences, the greatest, the most apparent, and the strongest.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof that self-knowledge is the clearest of all knowledge. The author begins by explaining that when someone knows a thing, their knowledge that they know that thing depends on prior knowledge of their own essence. This is because one cannot attribute knowledge to oneself without knowing one's own self. The author then cites the rule that all assent (tasdiq) is preceded by a concept (tasawwur), and the condition is prior to the conditioned in rank. This means that since knowledge of any object requires self-knowledge as a condition, self-knowledge must logically precede all other knowledge. If self-knowledge precedes even the self-evident clear truths (al-badihiyyat al-jaliyyat), then it is even more deserving of being called clear and self-evident. The proof concludes that self-knowledge is the clearest, greatest, most apparent, and strongest of sciences.

Key Terms
علم الذات
بديهيات جليات
تصديق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge is merely empirical or reflective knowledge gained through introspection.
Correction: Self-knowledge here means the direct, immediate awareness of one's own essence, which is a necessary condition for any knowledge at all, not a product of reflection.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that self-knowledge is the clearest of sciences?
A) Because it is acquired through sense perception
B) Because it is a condition for all other knowledge and precedes even the self-evident truths
C) Because it is taught by all prophets
D) Because it is universally agreed upon

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge is the clearest because it is the necessary condition for all other knowledge, including self-evident truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know yourself, but do you truly recognize that your knowledge of yourself is the basis for every single thing you know? When was the last time you paused to reflect on your own existence before seeking knowledge of the world? Commit to starting your day with a moment of self-awareness.
?
If self-knowledge is the clearest knowledge, why do you often neglect it in favor of external knowledge? What habit can you change today to prioritize knowing your own soul over acquiring information?
?
The proof shows that you cannot even judge that you know something without first knowing yourself. Are there areas in your life where you have made judgments about others or about truth while remaining ignorant of your own inner state? Seek to know yourself first before seeking to correct others.

The Inductive Proof: Reason's Perplexity with Self and Time-Place
Induction of self and place/time leading to bewilderment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how induction (istiqra') demonstrates that even the most obvious human knowledge—self-knowledge and awareness of place and time—leads to philosophical bewilderment.

Core Idea
Our basic self-awareness and sense of time and place defy complete rational explanation.

The author invites us to examine three areas that everyone thinks they know well: themselves, their location, and their moment in time. Through a quick induction, he shows that philosophers have disagreed profoundly about each one. This lesson focuses on the first two examples, setting the stage for a powerful argument about the limits of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the author's calm, persuasive tone as he builds the argument.
Pay attention to the words 'الاستقراء' and 'تحير'.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage presents two of three examples: self-knowledge and knowledge of place and time. Notice how each is introduced and what the author says about the perplexity they cause.
The first example (self) is very brief; the second (place/time) is detailed with philosophical disagreements.
3
Reflect on the implication
The author concludes this part by saying that even the most apparent things lead to perplexity. Ask yourself: why does he emphasize this before moving to the third example?
This sets up the a fortiori argument about God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن لتقرير هذه الحجة شرحاً آخر وهو: أن الاستقراء يدل على أن أظهر المعلومات عند الخلق أشياء معدودة مثل علم كل أحد بنفسه، ومثل علمه بزمانه ومكانه ومثل علمه بجسميته. ثم إن العقل إذا خاص في معرفة النفس والجسم ومعرفة المكان والزمان تحير ولم يقدر على الخلاص، فإذا كان حاله في معرفة أظهر الأشياء كذلك، فكيف يكون حاله في معرفة أخفى الأشياء. ولنبين صحة ما ذكرناه فنقول : أولها : ذاته المخصوصة وقد كشفنا حقيقة الحال فيه وثانيها علمه بالمكان والزمان فإن كل أحد يحكم ببديهة عقله أنه كان في ذلك المكان وانتقل منه إلى مكان آخر، وبقي في ذلك المكان الأول، والعلم بالمكان جزء من أجزاء ذلك العلم. وأيضاً كل أحد يحكم ببديهة عقله أن هذا الوقت الخاص وقت كذا، ثم بعده يقول : إنه مضى ذلك الوقت، وحضر وقت آخر والعلم بحقيقة الوقت والمدة جزء من العلم بأنه مضى الوقت الأول، وحضر الوقت الثاني. ثم إن العقلاء دارت رؤوسهم وحارت عقولهم في معرفة حقيقة المكان والزمان. أما المكان فأصحاب أفلاطون وكل من كان قبله من الحكماء المعتبرين: اتفقوا على أنه عبارة عن البعد الممتد . وأما اصحاب ارسطاطاليس فقد اتفقوا على أنه عبارة عن السطح المحيط، وأن القول بالبعد باطل. وصعوبة هذه المسألة تظهر في مباحث مسألة المكان. وأما الزمان فقد حارت العقول ودارت الرؤوس في معرفته، وإذا تأملت في مسألة الزمان وأحاط عقلك بما في تلك المسائل من الدقائق العميقة، والمباحث الدقيقة، علمت أن هذه المسألة قد بلغت إلى أقصى الغايات، وأبلغ النهايات في الصعوبة والخفاء . وثالثها: العلم بحقيقة الجسم، وقد حارت العقول أيضاً في أنه هل هو مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، أو ليس الأمر كذلك، بل هو قابل للقسمة إلى غير النهاية؟ ومن خاض في تلك المسألة وعرف قوة الدلائل من الجانبين، علم أنه لا حاصل عند العقل إلا الحيرة والدهشة والأخذ بالأولى والأخلق، فيثبت بهذا الاستقراء : أن حاصل العقل في معرفة أظهر المعلومات ليس إلا محض الحيرة والدهشة والأخذ بالأولى والأخلق فما ظنك بالعقل عند العروج إلى باب كبرياء الله تعالى وعندما يحاول الخوض في البحث عن كنه عزته وصمديته وصفات جلاله وإكرامه من علمه وقدرته وحكمته؟ فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب.
Know that for establishing this argument there is another explanation, which is: that induction indicates that the most apparent things in creation are limited things, such as each person's knowledge of himself, and his knowledge of his time and place, and his knowledge of his bodiliness. Then if the intellect, when it delves into the knowledge of the soul, the body, place, and time, becomes perplexed and unable to extricate itself—if its state in knowing the most apparent things is like this, how will its state be in knowing the most hidden things? And to clarify the correctness of what we have said, we say: The first of them: his specific self (dhatihi al-makhsusah) and we have already uncovered the reality of the matter concerning it. The second: his knowledge of place and time. For everyone judges by the intuition of his intellect that he was in that place and moved from it to another place, and remained in that first place, and knowledge of place is part of that knowledge. Also, everyone judges by the intuition of his intellect that this particular time is such and such, then afterwards he says: that time has passed and another time has come, and knowledge of the reality of time and duration is part of the knowledge that the first time passed and the second time came. Then the intelligent have had their heads spin and their intellects perplexed in knowing the reality of place and time. As for place: the followers of Plato and all the respected philosophers before him agreed that it is the extended dimension. As for the followers of Aristotle, they agreed that it is the encompassing surface and that the statement about dimension is false. The difficulty of this issue appears in the investigations of the issue of place. As for time: the intellects have been perplexed and heads have spun in knowing it. If you contemplate the issue of time and your intellect encompasses what is in those issues of profound subtleties and precise investigations, you will know that this issue has reached the utmost extremes and the highest limits in difficulty and concealment. The third: knowledge of the reality of the body. The intellects have also been perplexed: is it composed of indivisible parts, or is it not so but rather divisible infinitely? Whoever delves into this issue and knows the strength of the proofs from both sides knows that the outcome for the intellect is nothing but perplexity, astonishment, and adopting the worthier and more appropriate. Thus it is established by this induction that the outcome of the intellect in knowing the most apparent things is nothing but pure perplexity, astonishment, and adopting the worthier and more appropriate. So what do you think of the intellect when it ascends to the door of God's majesty and when it attempts to delve into the investigation of the essence of His might, His self-sufficiency, and His attributes of majesty and generosity—His knowledge, power, and wisdom? This is the summary of the speech in this section.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is an inductive proof (istiqra') for the incomprehensibility of God. The author begins by listing three apparently obvious things: (1) each person's knowledge of himself, (2) knowledge of place and time, (3) knowledge of body. He argues that upon deeper scrutiny, each of these leads to perplexity. For self-knowledge, he says it has been discussed elsewhere. For place and time, he explains that people intuitively know they are in a place and time, but philosophers dispute the nature of place (Platonists vs. Aristotelians) and time, which is extremely difficult. For the body, philosophers dispute whether it is composed of indivisible particles (atoms) or is infinitely divisible. Induction shows that reason's outcome is only bewilderment regarding even the most apparent things. Therefore, it is even more bewildered when trying to know God's majesty and attributes. The argument thus reinforces the point that human intellects can only rely on the via eminentiae (al-akhdh bil-awla wal-akhlaq).

Key Terms
الاستقراء
الحيرة
الدهشة
الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It gains clear understanding
B) It becomes perplexed and unable to extricate itself
C) It discovers the true nature of both
D) It relies on revelation

Key Takeaway
Even our most basic self-awareness and sense of time and place lead to profound philosophical perplexity, underscoring the limits of human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust your own reasoning daily. This passage confesses that even your awareness of yourself, time, and place defies full understanding. When have you felt that your intellect hit a wall? Will you admit your dependence on divine guidance, or will you continue to rely solely on your own mind? What one step can you take this week to humble your intellect before the Creator?
?
The philosophers argued over the nature of place—dimension or surface. You may never have thought about it, but you act as if you know what 'here' means. How does the depth of your ignorance about the most basic realities affect your confidence in your opinions about God? Will you now hold your theological views with humility and a readiness to learn, or will you cling to certainty?
?
You have a sense of time passing—past, future. But this passage tells you that even the greatest minds are perplexed by it. In what ways do you waste time as if it is trivial, when it is a profound sign of Allah's creativity? How will you treat your time differently today, knowing it is a mystery that points to the Eternal?

The Body and the Conclusion: From Perplexity to Divine Transcendence
Body example and final conclusion: via eminentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the third example (the nature of the body) and the overall induction lead to the conclusion that reason can only rely on the via eminentiae (al-akhdh bil-awla) when approaching God's essence and attributes.

Core Idea
If reason is bewildered by physical bodies, how can it comprehend the divine essence?

The author now turns to the third supposedly obvious thing—the human body. Philosophers have bitterly disputed whether it is made of indivisible atoms or infinitely divisible. This deadlock leads to the same result: perplexity. The author then drives home the point: if even the most familiar things baffle reason, then reason must adopt awe and the 'more appropriate' approach when speaking about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the body
Read the Arabic and translation for the third example. Notice the two opposing views: indivisible parts vs. infinite divisibility.
The author presents both as having strong proofs.
2
Recognize the outcome of the induction
After all three examples, the author states that the intellect's outcome is pure perplexity, astonishment, and 'adopting the worthier and more appropriate'. Understand what 'al-akhdh bil-awla wal-akhlaq' means.
This is the via eminentiae.
3
Connect to God
The author concludes with a rhetorical question: if this is the state with manifest things, what about when ascending to God's majesty? Reflect on the implication for theological language.
The answer is that we must speak analogically and with awe.

Geometry as a Measure of Intellectual Difficulty
Author cites Euclid's geometry to illustrate increasing difficulty in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Euclid's geometry as an analogy for the increasing difficulty of rational demonstration, especially regarding the knowledge of Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
As polygons grow in sides, proofs require more premises — so does theological knowledge.

The author strengthens his argument by appealing to geometry, considered the most rigorous rational science. He references Euclid's Elements to show that proving more complex shapes requires additional steps. This analogy prepares the reader for the increasing challenge of proving divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Note that the text is incomplete but captures the beginning of the analogy.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the key idea is clear.
2
Identify the key analogy
The author says polygons start from the triangle and increase infinitely. For each new polygon, Euclid needed an extra premise. This mirrors the increasing difficulty in theology.
The intended point is that knowing Allah's attributes is more difficult than simpler truths.
3
Reflect on the implication
Though cut off, the author likely argues that as the subject becomes more complex, more preparatory work is needed. Apply this to understanding God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يزيد هذا الكلام تقريراً : أن أقوى المباحث العقلية باتفاق جمهور العقلاء المباحث الهندسية، فليتأمل في كتاب اقليدس، يقول: إن أقسام المضلعات تبتدىء من المثلث وتمر إلى غير النهاية. ثم إن إقليدس أقام الحجة على إثبات المثلث والمربع في المقالة الأولى ولما احتاج إلى اثبات المخمس احتاج إلى تقديم مقدمة عليه، وهو أنه عمل مثلثاً يكون كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين
And what increases this speech in confirmation is that the strongest rational investigations, by agreement of the majority of rational beings, are the geometrical investigations. So let one reflect on the book of Euclid. He says: The divisions of polygons begin from the triangle and continue infinitely. Then Euclid established the proof for triangle and square in the first article. And when he needed to prove the pentagon, he needed to present a premise before it, namely that he made a triangle such that each of the two angles which...

Commentary
This passage is an additional argument to reinforce the previous point. The author claims that geometry is the strongest rational discipline. He then references Euclid's Elements, noting that polygons increase in complexity starting from the triangle. Euclid could prove the triangle and square in the first book, but to prove the pentagon he needed an additional premise. The text breaks off, but the intended point is likely that as the figures become more complex, the proofs require more steps, analogously to the increasing difficulty in knowing God's attributes. This is an incomplete lesson due to the cut-off.

The Intellect's Limits: Space, Time, and Body
Author argues by induction that human intellect ends in bewilderment regarding space, time, and body, then applies to divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses induction from three fundamental concepts (space, time, body) to demonstrate that human reason ends in bewilderment, and the implication for understanding Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
Reason reaches its limit even with the most obvious things; how can it grasp the divine?

This passage presents a powerful argument about the boundaries of human intellect. The author takes three seemingly straightforward concepts—space, time, and the nature of body—and shows that even here, reason cannot achieve certainty. If our minds are bewildered by these, what hope do they have in understanding the essence of Allah? Let's follow the author's induction step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the flow from one example to the next and the strong concluding rhetorical question.
Pay attention to the words 'حارت العقول' (intellects bewildered).
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three examples the author uses: space (place), time, and the nature of body. For each, note why the author says reason is bewildered.
3
Understand the method of induction
The author uses استقراء (induction): from several cases, he derives a general conclusion. See how the three examples lead to the statement that the outcome of intellect is bewilderment.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and its application
The conclusion is that even for the most apparent matters, the intellect ends in bewilderment. The author then applies this to divine matters: if reason fails here, how can it grasp Allah's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالبعد باطل. وصعوبة هذه المسألة تظهر في مباحث مسألة المكان. وأما الزمان فقد حارت العقول ودارت الرؤوس في معرفته، وإذا تأملت في مسألة الزمان وأحاط عقلك بما في تلك المسائل من الدقائق العميقة، والمباحث الدقيقة، علمت أن هذه المسألة قد بلغت إلى أقصى الغايات، وأبلغ النهايات في الصعوبة والخفاء. وثالثها: العلم بحقيقة الجسم، وقد حارت العقول أيضا في أنه هل هو مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، أو ليس الأمر كذلك، بل هو قابل للقسمة إلى غير النهاية؟ ومن خاض في تلك المسألة وعرف قوة الدلائل من الجانبين، علم أنه لا حاصل عند العقل إلا الحيرة والدهشة والأخذ بالأولى والأخلق، فيثبت بهذا الاستقراء: أن حاصل العقل في معرفة أظهر المعلومات ليس إلا محض الحيرة والدهشة والأخذ بالأولى والأخلق فما ظنك بالعقل عند العروج إلى باب كبرياء الله تعالى وعندما يحاول الخوض في البحث عن كنه عزته وصمديته وصفات جلاله وإكرامه من علمه وقدرته وحكمته؟ فهذا جملة الكلام في هذا الباب.
By distance is false. And the difficulty of this issue appears in the investigations of the issue of place. As for time, intellects have been bewildered and heads have spun in its knowledge. And if you reflect on the issue of time and your mind encompasses what is in those issues of profound subtleties and precise investigations, you know that this issue has reached the utmost limits and the most extreme ends in difficulty and concealment. And third of them: knowledge of the reality of body, and intellects have also been bewildered whether it is composed of indivisible parts, or not, but rather it is divisible to infinity. And whoever delves into that issue and knows the strength of the proofs from both sides knows that there is no outcome for the intellect except bewilderment, astonishment, and taking what is more fitting and worthier. So by this induction it is established that the outcome of the intellect in knowing the most apparent of known things is nothing but pure bewilderment, astonishment, and taking what is more fitting and worthier. So what do you think of the intellect when ascending to the door of the majesty of God Almighty and when it attempts to delve into research about the essence of His might, His aseity, and His attributes of majesty and honor: His knowledge, power, and wisdom? This is the sum of the discourse in this chapter.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an argument by induction (istiqra') demonstrating the limits of human intellect. It begins by stating that the difficulty of the issue of space (place) is evident. Then it moves to time, noting that minds are bewildered and heads spin in trying to understand it; a deep reflection reveals it reaches extreme difficulty and concealment. Thirdly, it discusses the nature of body: whether it is composed of indivisible atoms (the atomist view) or infinitely divisible. Both sides have strong proofs, and the intellect is left only with bewilderment, astonishment, and accepting what is most fitting (al-awla wa al-akhlaq) without certainty. Through this induction of three examples—space, time, and body—the author concludes that the intellect's result even for the most evident matters is pure confusion. He then applies this to divine matters: if reason fails here, how can it grasp the essence of God's attributes? The passage ends by summarizing that this is the gist of the discussion.

Geometrical Example of Intellect's Limit: Euclid's Polygons
Author uses Euclid's geometry to show human intellect's incapacity even in mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's geometrical constructions illustrate the limits of human intellect even in the strongest rational science.

Core Idea
Even geometry, the strongest rational science, has limits that the intellect cannot overcome without advanced tools.

This passage deepens the argument that human intellect is limited by examining the most rigorous science: geometry. By walking through Euclid's constructions—and his failures—the author shows that even in mathematics, the intellect cannot reach all truths without additional aids. This sets the stage for understanding why divine matters require revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the author's argument unfold step by step.
The passage is dense with geometrical details; focus on the pattern of success and failure.
2
Observe Euclid's successes
Notice which polygons Euclid could construct: triangle, square, pentagon, hexagon, and fifteen-sided polygon. Understand the methods used.
The pentagon required a special triangle construction; the hexagon used the relation of radius to chord.
3
Note Euclid's failures
Identify the polygons Euclid could not construct: heptagon, nonagon, 11-gon, 13-gon. Recognize why—they require conic sections or angle trisection.
The author emphasizes that Euclid 'was incapable' and left these constructions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the overall point: even in geometry, the strongest rational science, the human intellect is limited. This supports the need for revelation in divine matters.
The passage is not a critique of Euclid but an illustration of intellectual boundaries.

ومما يزيد هذا الكلام تقريرا: أن أقوى المباحث العقلية باتفاق جمهور العقلاء المباحث الهندسية، فليتأمل في كتاب اقليدس، يقول: إن أقسام المضلعات تبتدىء من المثلث وتمر إلى غير النهاية. ثم إن إقليدس أقام الحجة على إثبات المثلث والمربع في المقالة الأولى ولما احتاج إلى اثبات المخمس احتاج إلى تقديم مقدمة عليه، وهو أنه عمل مثلثا يكون كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة منعطفا إلى الزاوية الفوقانية ثم قسم كل واحدة من الزاويتين إلى نصفين، وبهذه الطريقة قدر على عمل المخمس، ثم لما أقام البرهان على أن نصف قطر كل دائرة يساوى وتر سدس تلك الدائرة، لا جرم قدر بهذا الطريق على عمل المسدس ثم إنه طفر منه إلى شكل ذي خمسة عشر ضلعا، والسبب في هذه الطفرة أنه احتاج في عمل المسبع إلى تقديم مثلث يكون كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة ثلاثة أمثال الزاوية الفوقانية . وأعمال كتاب أقليدس لا تفي بإقامة البرهان على هذا المطلوب، بل لا يمكن إثبات هذا المطلوب إلا بقطوع المخروطات، فلا جرم عجز عنه وتركه . وأيضا لا يمكنه إثبات عمل المتسع إلا بطريقين: أحدهما: أن يعمل مثلثا متساوي الأضلاع، ثم يقسم كل واحدة من زواياه الثلاث ثلاثة أقسام متساوية. وقد بينا أن هذا لا يتم إلا بقطوع المخروطات. والطريق الثاني: أن يعمل مثلثا يكون كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة أربعة أمثال الزاوية الفوقانية وهو عاجز عنه . وأما الشكل الذي يحيط به أحد عشر ضلعا، فهو إنما يتم بتقديم عمل مثلث، تساوي كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة لخمس مرات للزاوية الفوقانية، وهو عاجز عنه، وكذا القول في مضلع محيط به ثلاثة عشر مضلعا، فإنه إنما يتم بعمل مثلث يساوي كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة للزاوية الفوقانية ست مرات. وهو عاجز عنه. وأما المضلع الذي يحيط به خمسة عشر مضلعا فإنه قدر على عمله بمقدمات أثبتها في كتابه، وذلك أنه أوقع في الدائرة مثلثا متساوي الأضلاع فانقسمت الدائرة ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، ثم أوقع
And what increases this discourse in confirmation: that the strongest of rational investigations according to the consensus of the majority of rational people are the geometrical investigations. So let one reflect on the book of Euclid. He says: The divisions of polygons begin from the triangle and pass to infinity. Then Euclid established proof for the triangle and square in the first treatise. And when he needed to prove the pentagon, he needed to present a premise for it, namely that he made a triangle in which each of the two angles at the base is inclined to the apex angle, then he divided each of the two angles into two halves, and by this method he was able to construct the pentagon. Then when he established proof that the radius of every circle equals the chord of one-sixth of that circle, he was able by this method to construct the hexagon. Then he leaped from it to the fifteen-sided figure. The reason for this leap is that in constructing the heptagon he needed to present a triangle in which each of the two angles at the base is three times the apex angle. And the works of Euclid's book are not sufficient to establish proof of this desired [construction]; rather, this desired [construction] cannot be proven except by conic sections. So he was incapable of it and left it. Also he cannot prove the construction of the nonagon except by two methods: one of them is to make an equilateral triangle, then divide each of its three angles into three equal parts. And we have shown that this is not completed except by conic sections. The second method: to make a triangle in which each of the two angles at the base is four times the apex angle, and he is incapable of it. As for the shape enclosed by eleven sides, it is completed only by presenting a triangle in which each of the two angles at the base equals five times the apex angle, and he is incapable of it. And similarly the statement for a polygon enclosed by thirteen sides: it is completed only by making a triangle in which each of the two angles at the base equals six times the apex angle, and he is incapable of it. As for the polygon enclosed by fifteen sides, he was able to construct it by premises he proved in his book, namely that he placed an equilateral triangle in a circle, so the circle was divided into three equal parts, then he placed...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a further confirmation of the previous argument by example from geometry. It begins by stating that geometry is the strongest rational science according to most rational people. Then it recounts how Euclid in his 'Elements' was able to construct regular polygons: triangle, square easily; pentagon by a special construction; hexagon using the relation of radius to chord of 60°. But for the heptagon, Euclid needed a triangle with base angles three times the apex, which he could not prove without conic sections, so he left it. Similarly, the nonagon requires trisecting angles or a triangle with base angles four times the apex, both beyond Euclidean methods. The 11-gon and 13-gon require base angles five and six times the apex respectively, and Euclid is incapable. Only the 15-gon he could construct using an equilateral triangle inscribed in a circle. The author uses this to show that even the most rigorous rational science (geometry) has limits, and the intellect cannot reach all truths without advanced tools (conic sections), reinforcing the idea of intellectual limitation before divine matters.

Correction: The author uses Euclid's limitations to illustrate that even the strongest rational science has boundaries, not to criticize Euclid. Euclid's work is presented as the pinnacle of geometry.

Euclid's Polygons and the Limits of Reason
The author demonstrates the limitation of human reason through Euclid's inability to construct most regular polygons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Euclid's geometric limitations to prove the insufficiency of human reason.

Core Idea
Even the greatest geometer could only construct five polygons, showing reason's limits.

This lesson presents a striking argument from the history of mathematics. The author takes the example of Euclid, the father of geometry, to show that human reason can only achieve a tiny fraction of knowledge. By detailing Euclid's successes and failures in constructing regular polygons, the author builds a case for the inherent deficiency of the human intellect, especially when faced with divine truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how the author builds his argument from specific geometric details to a general conclusion.
The passage contains many geometric terms; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify Euclid's successful polygons
The author lists five polygons that Euclid was able to construct: triangle, square, pentagon, hexagon, and fifteen-sided figure. Find where these are mentioned.
The pentagon construction uses angle bisection, while the hexagon uses the fact that radius equals chord of one-sixth.
3
Examine the failed polygons
Look for the polygons Euclid could not construct: heptagon, nonagon, eleven-sided, and thirteen-sided. Note the reasons given, such as needing conic sections or impossible angle divisions.
The author explains why each fails, linking to triangle angle requirements.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read the final paragraph where the author concludes that human intellects are deficient, unable to perceive realities except a little. Consider the rhetorical question at the end.
This conclusion applies the geometric example to the broader limitation of reason.

Source Text

فوق القاعدة منعطفا إلى الزاوية الفوقانية ثم قسم كل واحدة من الزاويتين إلى نصفين، وبهذه الطريقة قدر على عمل المخمس، ثم لما أقام البرهان على أن نصف قطر كل دائرة يساوى وتر سدس تلك الدائرة، لا جرم قدر الفوقانية . وأعمال كتاب أقليدس لا تفي بإقامة البرهان على هذا المطلوب، بل لا يمكن إثبات هذا المطلوب إلا بقطوع المخروطات، فلا جرم عجز عنه وتركه . وأيضا لا يمكنه إثبات عمل المتسع إلا بطريقين: أحدهما: أن القاعدة أربعة أمثال الزاوية الفوقانية وهو عاجز عنه . وأما الشكل الذي يحيط به أحد عشر ضلعا، فهو إنما يتم بتقديم عمل مثلث، تساوي كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة لخمس مرات للزاوية الفوقانية، وهو عاجز عنه، وكذا القول في مضلع محيط به ثلاثة عشر مضلعا، فإنه إنما يتم بعمل مثلث يساوي كل واحدة من الزاويتين اللتين فوق القاعدة للزاوية الفوقانية ست مرات. وهو عاجز عنه. وأما المضلع الذي يحيط به خمسة عشر مضلعا فإنه قدر على عمله بمقدمات أثبتها في كتابه، وذلك أنه أوقع في الدائرة مثلثا متساوي الأضلاع فانقسمت الدائرة ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، ثم أوقع في القسم الواحد منها نصف قطر الدائرة، وهو وتر المسدس، ثم قسم ما بقي نصفين، فبهذا الطريق قسم كل قوتين بخمسة أقسام متساوية، فخرج له مضلع أحاط به خمسة عشر ضلعا وعند هذا وقف عمله، ولم يقدر على الزيادة . فالحاصل أن أقليدس قدر على إقامة البرهان على إثبات خمسة أنواع من المضلعات المثلث والمربع والمخمس والمسدس وذو خمسة عشر ضلعا . وأما بقية الأقسام التي لا نهاية لها فقوانينه قاصرة عن إثباتها، ومقدماته غير وافية بتقريرها . وأما أصحاب علوم المخروطات، فقد تكلفوا طريقة في إثبات المسبع والمتسع وأما البقية فقد بقيت في موقف العجز والقصور فقد ظهر بما ذكرنا : أن العقول البشرية قاصرة والأفهام الإنسانية غير وافية بإدراك حقائق الأشياء إلا في القليل القليل من الكثير الكثير في معرفة هذه المحسوسات فما ظنك بالعقل عند طلوع نور الالهية وسطوع الأضواء الصمدية؟
Above the base, turning to the superior angle, then he divided each of the two angles into halves, and in this way he was able to construct the pentagon. Then when he established the proof that the radius of every circle equals the chord of its sixth, inevitably he was able by this method to construct the hexagon. Then he leaped from it to a shape having fifteen sides. The reason for this leap is that he needed in constructing the heptagon to present a triangle in which each of the two angles above the base is three times the superior angle. The works of Euclid's book do not suffice to establish a proof for this requirement; rather, this requirement cannot be proven except by conic sections. So inevitably he was unable to do it and left it. Also, he cannot prove the construction of the nonagon except by two methods: one: to construct an equilateral triangle, then divide each of its three angles into three equal parts. We have explained that this cannot be done except by conic sections. The second method: to construct a triangle in which each of the two angles above the base is four times the superior angle, and he is unable to do it. As for the shape bounded by eleven sides, it is only completed by presenting the construction of a triangle where each of the two angles above the base equals five times the superior angle, and he is unable to do it. Similarly for a polygon bounded by thirteen sides, for it is only completed by constructing a triangle where each of the two angles above the base equals six times the superior angle, and he is unable to do it. As for the polygon bounded by fifteen sides, he was able to construct it through premises he established in his book. That is, he inscribed an equilateral triangle in the circle, so the circle was divided into three equal parts. Then he inscribed in one of those parts the radius of the circle, which is the chord of the hexagon, then he divided the remainder into halves. In this way he divided each of the two powers into five equal parts, and a polygon bounded by fifteen sides resulted. At this point his work stopped, and he could not add more. The result is that Euclid was able to establish proof for five types of polygons: the triangle, square, pentagon, hexagon, and fifteen-sided figure. As for the remaining infinite divisions, his principles are insufficient to prove them, and his premises are inadequate for their determination. As for the practitioners of conic sections, they undertook a method for proving the heptagon and the nonagon, but the rest remained in a position of incapacity and deficiency. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that human intellects are deficient and human understandings are insufficient to perceive the realities of things except for the very little out of the very much in knowing these sensible things. So what do you think of the intellect at the rising of the light of divinity and the shining of the eternal lights?
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is the first proof in a chapter demonstrating the limitation of human reason. The author uses the example of Euclid, the greatest geometer, to show that even with rigorous methods, human reason can achieve only a tiny fraction of knowledge. The author details Euclid's construction of regular polygons: the triangle, square, pentagon, hexagon, and fifteen-sided figure. For each, he explains the geometric reasoning. He then shows that Euclid failed to construct the heptagon, nonagon, eleven-sided, and thirteen-sided figures because they require conic sections, which Euclid did not have. The author emphasizes that Euclid's methods only suffice for five polygons; the rest remain beyond reach. Even later mathematicians using conic sections could only add the heptagon and nonagon, leaving the rest unsolved. The conclusion is that human intellects are inherently deficient, unable to grasp realities except for a tiny fraction. This point is driven home by a rhetorical question: if reason is so limited even with sensible things, how can it hope to comprehend divine light and eternal truths? The author follows a pattern of enumerating cases to support his argument.

Analogy Between Sight and Insight
The author compares sight and insight, listing two of three states of visible objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses an analogy between physical sight and intellectual insight to argue that both have limitations at extremes.

Core Idea
Just as sight fails at extremes, so does insight.

In this passage, the author draws a powerful analogy between the power of sight (basar) and the power of insight (basira). He notes that visible objects come in three states, and sight fails at both extremes. This sets up an argument that our intellectual capacity also has limits when it comes to knowing the Divine. Although the passage breaks off mid-sentence, the classification is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the analogy between basar and basira and the mention of three states of visible objects.
The passage cuts off before the third state and conclusion.
2
Identify the two states given
Find the two states described: weak objects (atoms, motes) and strong objects (sun disk). See how sight fails in both.
3
Reflect on the implied third state
Consider that the missing third state is likely objects of moderate strength that sight can perceive normally. Parallel this with insight: we can grasp moderate truths but not extremes.
Do not invent details beyond the text.

The second state: the strong, overpowering, dominant visible objects like the disk of the sun at

Commentary
This passage introduces the second proof, which draws an analogy between the physical power of sight (basar) and the intellectual power of insight (basira). The author states they are equal. Then he presents a three-part classification of visible objects, though only two are given in this window. The first state includes objects that are too weak or subtle to be seen, like atoms and motes. Sight is incapable of perceiving them. The second state includes objects that are too strong and overwhelming, like the sun's disk. The implication is that just as sight fails at extremes, so insight fails at extremes of knowledge—whether too subtle or too sublime. The missing third state would presumably be objects of moderate strength that sight can perceive normally. The author uses this to show the limitations of human reason.

Human Conceptions and Assents Limited to Four Categories
The author concludes that human intellect cannot know God's essence in detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human intellect cannot know God's essence in detail, and how we can only know Him through general attributes of perfection.

Core Idea
Our minds are boxed into four categories; God's reality escapes them all.

This passage brings together two key ideas: first, that human conceptions and assents are limited to just four categories; second, that God's essence is completely unlike anything within those categories. Consequently, we cannot know God in detail, but only through the concept of perfection, stripped of all imperfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Notice how the author builds from the premise of limited categories to the conclusion about God. The passage is a conclusion of a previous discussion.
Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Understand the link between conceptions and assents
The author asserts that assents depend on conceptions, so if conceptions are limited to four categories, assents are also limited.
3
See the argument for God's transcendence
The author argues that God is different from all conceivable essences; if He shared any aspect, He'd be composite, which is impossible.
This is a key proof for divine simplicity.
4
Learn the indirect way of knowing God
We conceive perfection and imperfection in ourselves and affirm perfection for God while removing imperfections. This gives only general, not detailed, knowledge.
The absolute is part of the qualified.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Human intellects have only summary knowledge of God's majesty, not detailed understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بهذا البيان: أن تصورات العقول البشرية لا تخرج عن هذه الأقسام الأربعة، وإذا كانت التصورات الحاصلة عندنا محصورة في هذه الأقسام كانت التصديقات أيضا محصورة فيها، لما ثبت أن التصديقات مشروطة بالتصورات. فيثبت أن تصورات الخلق وتصديقاتهم محصورة في هذه الأقسام الأربعة. وإذا ظهرت هذه المقدمة فنقول: ثبت بالبرهان أن حقيقة الحق سبحانه وتعالى مخالفة لجملة هذه الماهيات التي هي محسوسة لنا، وحاضرة في عقولنا حضورا بالتفصيل، وأنه سبحانه لا يناسب شيئا منها وهو مخالف لها بأسرها مخالفة من جميع الوجوه، فإنه لو شاركها من بعض الوجوه، وخالفها في سائر الوجوه، لكان ما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالطة فتكون حقيقته مركبة وذلك محال. وإذا كان كذلك وجب ألا تكون حقيقته متصورة للخلق بوجه من الوجوه. وإذا لم تكن حقيقته متصورة للخلق كان الحكم عليها بالسلب والإيجاب البسيطين أو المركبين ممتنعا، لما ثبت أن التصديق موقوف على التصور. فالعقول قاصرة عن معرفته والإدراكات غير منتهية إليه، وإنما الغاية القصوى أنا إذا تصورنا معنى الكمال والنقصان في حق أنفسنا بحسب ما يليق بنا وبمقدار ما يناسبنا وجب أن نفهم معنى الكمال والنقصان، لأن المطلق، جزء من ماهية المقيد، وبهذا الطريق يتصور معنى الكمال والنقصان، وإذا تصورنا هذا المعنى اعترفنا بإثبات مسمى الكمال له بشرط تنزه ذلك المسمى عن اللواحق اللاحقة له بسبب حصوله فينا، فليس عند جملة الخلق من معارف جلاله، إلا هذا القدر. فظهر بهذا البيان أن عقول الخلق لم يحصل عندها من المعارف الإلهية إلا هذه الأمور المجملة المشار إليها على سبيل الأولى والأخلق وأما على سبيل التفصيل فلا.
Thus it is established by this demonstration: that the conceptions of human intellects do not go beyond these four categories. And if the conceptions that occur to us are confined to these categories, then the assents are also confined to them, because it is established that assents are conditional upon conceptions. Thus it is established that the conceptions and assents of creatures are confined to these four categories. Once this premise is clear, we say: it is established by proof that the reality of the Truth, glorified and exalted, is different from all these essences that are perceived by us and present in our minds in detail, and that He, glorified, does not correspond to any of them, and He is contrary to them all in all respects. For if He shared with them in some respects and differed in others, then that by which He shares would be other than that by which He differs, so His reality would be composite, and that is impossible. Since this is so, it is necessary that His reality not be conceivable by creatures in any way. And if His reality is not conceivable by creatures, then making judgments about it by simple affirmation or negation or composite ones is impossible, because it is established that assent depends on conception. Thus intellects are deficient in knowing Him, and perceptions do not reach Him. Rather, the ultimate goal is that when we conceive the meaning of perfection and imperfection with respect to ourselves according to what befits us and to the extent that suits us, it becomes necessary that we understand the meaning of perfection and imperfection, because the absolute is part of the essence of the qualified, and in this way the meaning of perfection and imperfection is conceived. When we have conceived this meaning, we affirm the existence of that which is called perfection for Him, on condition that that which is called perfection is free from the attachments that adhere to it due to its occurrence in us. So all creatures have of the knowledge of His majesty only this much. Thus it appears by this demonstration that the intellects of creatures have not obtained from divine knowledge except these summary matters indicated in a primary and more fitting way, but not in detail.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage concludes a previous demonstration about the limitation of human conceptions and assents. The author states that human conceptions are confined to four categories (presumably established earlier), and since assents depend on conceptions, assents are also confined. Then he applies this to knowledge of God: God's reality is different from all the essences we perceive in detail. If He shared some aspects, His essence would be composite, which is impossible. Therefore His essence is not conceivable by creatures at all. Consequently, judgments about Him (affirmation or negation) are impossible because assent requires conception. However, there is an indirect way: we conceive perfection and imperfection in ourselves, and then affirm perfection for God while removing all imperfections that accompany it in us. So human knowledge of God is only general, not detailed. The author emphasizes that creatures have only this much knowledge of His majesty.

The Fourth Proof: Three Ways of Reasoning to Know God
Sanusi enumerates the three types of reasoning and rules out two for knowing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical ways to move from the known to the unknown and why only reasoning from effect to cause can lead to knowledge of God.

Core Idea
From effect to cause: the only path to the Creator.

This lesson presents the fourth proof for the impossibility of detailed knowledge of God. The author explains that transition from known to unknown can only occur through three types of reasoning. Since God has no cause and no equivalent, the first two ways are impossible, leaving only the ascent from creation to Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the three ways
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the three types of reasoning.
2
Identify each way
Find the three ways: cause to effect, equivalent to equivalent, effect to cause. Notice how the author defines each.
3
Understand why first two fail
The author states the first two are 'missing' regarding the Truth. Think about why God has no cause and no equal.
This is a key point in kalam.
4
Grasp the remaining path
The only valid way is from effect to cause, i.e., ascending from creation to the Creator. This is the foundation for the rest of the proof.
5
Reflect on the implication
This means detailed knowledge of God is impossible through direct reasoning; only general knowledge via effects is possible.
Connects to the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
