إلى الله تصير الأمور) وقال: هو الأول والآخر) فالحق هو الأول عند النزول من الحق إلى الخلق، والآخر عند الصعود من الخلق إلى الحق ولما كانت درجات الوسائط كثيرة، ومراتبها خفية عن العقول البشرية، وكانت أحوال تلك الوسائط مختلفة ومراتب أضوائها وقهرها وقوتها مختلفة، وكانت قوة النفس الناطقة البشرية عند الترقي في هذه المراتب ضعيفة، لا جرم بقيت أكثر النفوس البشرية في درجة من درجات هذه المتوسطات بل نقول : أكثر الخلق بقوا في حضيض عوالم المحسوسات والشاذ القليل منهم تخلص من عالم الحس، مترقيا عن عالم المحسوسات إلى عالم الخيالات، و القليل من أصحاب الخيالات انتقل إلى عالم المعقولات. ثم في عالم المعقولات مراتب الأرواح المقدسة كثيرة، فلا جرم أكثر العقول الفاضلة [الما؟] وصلت إلى عوالم أنوار المعقولات تلاشت وفنيت واضمحلت في أنوار تلك الأرواح المقدسة إلا من أيد بقوة قاهرة، ونفس إلهية تترقى من زنجبيل المريخ إلى سلسبيل المشتري ومنه إلى كافور زحل ثم استعمل على الكل وترقى على الكل ووصل الى الحضرة المقدسة، عن لواحق عالم الإمكان وغيار الحدوث، واستسعد بقوله : وسفاهم ربهم شرابا طهورا أي ذلك الشراب الطهور، يظهره عن علائق الإمكان والحدوث، ويجليه على عتبة الوجوب بالذات . وإذا عرفت ذلك ظهر أن القليل من الأرواح البشرية يستسعد لقبول ذرة من ذرات أنوار عالم الجلال. وهذه تلويحات وتنبيهات ذكرناها في مقدمة هذا العلم، ليعلم الإنسان أن القليل من مباحث هذه المعالم الشريفة كثير كثير بالنسبة إلى الأرواح. ولذلك قال في الكتاب الإلهي حكاية عن الحق سبحانه أن قال وقليل من عبادي الشكور وقال حكاية عن إبليس : ولا تجد أكثرهم شاكرين). ولنكتف بهذا القدر من البيان في هذا المقام فإنه بحر لا ساحل له .
Concerning the attachments that attach to Him (God) due to His occurrence in us, so the entirety of creation has no knowledge of His majesty except this amount. So it became clear by this exposition that the intellects of creation have obtained from divine knowledge only these summarized matters indicated in a primary and more appropriate way, but not in detail. The fourth proof for them in this chapter is that transition from the known to the unknown is not conceivable except by one of three ways: One of them: reasoning from cause to effect. The second: reasoning from equivalent to equivalent. The third: reasoning from effect to cause. The first two ways are lacking with respect to the Truth, so the third way remains, which is to ascend from the effect to the cause, and to move from the created to the Creator. And if you know this, we say: The human rational soul occupies the last rank among the pure, sanctified beings, as you will come to know the reality of this premise when you learn the degrees and ranks of the angels. If this is so, then this human soul ascends from its knowledge of itself to its knowledge of its cause, and from its knowledge of its cause to its knowledge of the cause of its cause, and so on ascending rank by rank until it finally reaches the presence of the Necessary of Existence in essence. As it is said in the Divine Book: 'And that to your Lord is the finality' (Al-Najm 42); and He said: 'Indeed, to Allah all matters return' (Al-Shura 53); and He said: 'He is the First and the Last' (Al-Hadid 3). So the Truth is the First in descent from the Truth to creation, and the Last in ascent from creation to the Truth. And since the degrees of intermediaries are many, and their ranks are hidden from human intellects, and the states of those intermediaries are different, and the ranks of their lights, compulsion, and strength are different, and the power of the human rational soul in ascending these ranks is weak, necessarily most human souls remain in some rank of these intermediate levels. Rather we say: Most creatures remain in the depths of the worlds of sensible things, and the rare few of them escape the world of sense, ascending from the world of sensible things to the world of imaginations, and the few of the possessors of imaginations transfer to the world of intelligibles. Then in the world of intelligibles the ranks of sanctified spirits are many, so necessarily most of the excellent intellects that [unclear] reached the worlds of the lights of intelligibles dissipated, withered, and dissolved in the lights of those sanctified spirits, except those who were supported by an overwhelming power and a divine soul that ascends from the ginger of Mars to the Salsabil of Jupiter and from it to the camphor of Saturn, then it dominated over all and ascended over all and reached the sanctified presence, free from the attachments of the world of possibility and the dust of temporality, and was blessed by His saying: 'And their Lord will give them a pure drink' (Al-Insan 21) meaning that pure drink purifies him from the ties of possibility and temporality and manifests him at the threshold of essential necessity. And if you know that, it becomes clear that few of the human souls are blessed to accept an atom of the atoms of the lights of the world of majesty. And these are hints and reminders that we have mentioned in the introduction to this science, so that man may know that the few of the investigations of these noble stations are very many in relation to the souls. Therefore it is said in the Divine Book in narration from the Truth Most High that He said: 'And few of My servants are grateful' (Saba' 13) and He said in narration from Iblis: 'And you will not find most of them grateful' (Al-A'raf 17). And let us suffice with this amount of exposition in this place, for it is a sea with no shore.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a definition of the limits of human knowledge of God, the presentation of the fourth proof for the impossibility of detailed knowledge, and an explanation of the rarity of those who attain true knowledge. The author begins by stating that all of creation has no knowledge of God's majesty except a general, summarized understanding. This is because we only know God through His effects in us, not in His essence or detailed attributes. Then the author presents the fourth proof: transition from the known to the unknown can only occur through three types of reasoning: (1) from cause to effect, (2) from equivalent to equivalent, and (3) from effect to cause. The first two are not applicable to God (since He has no cause and no equivalent), so the only valid path is from effect to cause—ascending from creation to the Creator. Applying this to the human rational soul, which is the lowest of the pure spiritual beings, we find that it can ascend by knowing itself, then its cause, then the cause of its cause, and so on until the Necessary of Existence. The author supports this with Quranic verses. However, because the levels of intermediaries (from the sensible world to the intelligible world to the divine presence) are many and hidden, and human power is weak, most souls remain at lower levels. Only a few escape the sensible world to the imaginal world, and fewer still reach the intelligible world, and among those, most are overwhelmed by the lights of the sanctified spirits unless supported by divine power. The author uses astrological metaphors (Mars, Jupiter, Saturn) to describe the spiritual ascent but these are likely symbolic. Ultimately, only an extremely rare soul reaches the sanctified presence. The author concludes that these are hints for the introduction to this science, and that even a little of these noble investigations is much for souls, quoting Quranic verses about gratitude. He ends by stating that this topic is a boundless sea.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالعلة على المعلول (reasoning from cause to effect)
الاستدلال بالمساوى على المساوى (reasoning from equivalent to equivalent)
الاستدلال بالمعلول على العلة (reasoning from effect to cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that we can reason from cause to effect for God, since He is the cause of everything.
Correction: Reasoning from cause to effect requires knowing the cause first. Since we do not have direct knowledge of God's essence, this path is impossible. The only available path is from the known effect (creation) to the unknown cause (God).

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, which of the three ways of reasoning is valid for knowing God?
A) Reasoning from cause to effect
B) Reasoning from equivalent to equivalent
C) Reasoning from effect to cause
D) All three are valid

Key Takeaway
The only rational way to know God is to ascend from creation to Creator, as reasoning from cause or equivalent is impossible.

كم مرة في يومك تتأمل في مخلوق واحد وتصعد به إلى خالقه، كما يعلمك هذا الدرس؟ ما الذي يمنعك الآن من فعل ذلك؟
?
أي من الطريقتين المرفوضتين تميل إليه أنت أكثر: محاولة فهم الله كسبب أو تشبيهه بشيء تعرفه؟ كيف أثر ذلك على إيمانك؟

The Ascent of the Soul and the Rarity of True Knowledge
Sanusi explains the ascent of the rational soul through intermediaries and the rarity of souls reaching divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the human rational soul can ascend through the ranks of existence to knowledge of God, and why only a very few souls succeed in this journey.

Core Idea
The soul's ascent to God is a rare and arduous journey through many worlds.

Building on the fourth proof, the author now describes the actual process of ascent from the human soul to the Creator. He explains that the soul begins from knowledge of itself and ascends through intermediate causes, but most souls are too weak to traverse all the levels. Only a few, with divine support, reach the divine presence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ascent
Read the Arabic passage and translation describing the ascent of the rational soul.
2
Trace the ascent steps
Note how the soul moves from knowledge of itself to its cause, then to the cause of its cause, and so on until the Necessary of Existence.
This is a chain of causality.
3
Understand the obstacles
The author mentions many intermediaries and hidden ranks, and the weakness of the human soul. Identify why most souls get stuck.
4
Classify the levels
Recognize the three worlds: sensible, imaginal, intelligible. Note the rarity of souls that progress from one to the next.
This hierarchy is key.
5
Appreciate the rarity
The author concludes that very few souls accept even an atom of divine light. Reflect on the Quranic verses about gratitude.

ولا تجد أكثرهم شاكرين). ولنكتف بهذا القدر من البيان في هذا المقام فإنه بحر لا ساحل له .
Concerning the attachments that attach to Him (God) due to His occurrence in us, so the entirety of creation has no knowledge of His majesty except this amount. So it became clear by this exposition that the intellects of creation have obtained from divine knowledge only these summarized matters indicated in a primary and more appropriate way, but not in detail. The fourth proof for them in this chapter is that transition from the known to the unknown is not conceivable except by one of three ways: One of them: reasoning from cause to effect. The second: reasoning from equivalent to equivalent. The third: reasoning from effect to cause. The first two ways are lacking with respect to the Truth, so the third way remains, which is to ascend from the effect to the cause, and to move from the created to the Creator. And if you know this, we say: The human rational soul occupies the last rank among the pure, sanctified beings, as you will come to know the reality of this premise when you learn the degrees and ranks of the angels. If this is so, then this human soul ascends from its knowledge of itself to its knowledge of its cause, and from its knowledge of its cause to its knowledge of the cause of its cause, and so on ascending rank by rank until it finally reaches the presence of the Necessary of Existence in essence. As it is said in the Divine Book: 'And that to your Lord is the finality' (Al-Najm 42); and He said: 'Indeed, to Allah all matters return' (Al-Shura 53); and He said: 'He is the First and the Last' (Al-Hadid 3). So the Truth is the First in descent from the Truth to creation, and the Last in ascent from creation to the Truth. And since the degrees of intermediaries are many, and their ranks are hidden from human intellects, and the states of those intermediaries are different, and the ranks of their lights, compulsion, and strength are different, and the power of the human rational soul in ascending these ranks is weak, necessarily most human souls remain in some rank of these intermediate levels. Rather we say: Most creatures remain in the depths of the worlds of sensible things, and the rare few of them escape the world of sense, ascending from the world of sensible things to the world of imaginations, and the few of the possessors of imaginations transfer to the world of intelligibles. Then in the world of intelligibles the ranks of sanctified spirits are many, so necessarily most of the excellent intellects that [unclear] reached the worlds of the lights of intelligibles dissipated, withered, and dissolved in the lights of those sanctified spirits, except those who were supported by an overwhelming power and a divine soul that ascends from the ginger of Mars to the Salsabil of Jupiter and from it to the camphor of Saturn, then it dominated over all and ascended over all and reached the sanctified presence, free from the attachments of the world of possibility and the dust of temporality, and was blessed by His saying: 'And their Lord will give them a pure drink' (Al-Insan 21) meaning that pure drink purifies him from the ties of possibility and temporality and manifests him at the threshold of essential necessity. And if you know that, it becomes clear that few of the human souls are blessed to accept an atom of the atoms of the lights of the world of majesty. And these are hints and reminders that we have mentioned in the introduction to this science, so that man may know that the few of the investigations of these noble stations are very many in relation to the souls. Therefore it is said in the Divine Book in narration from the Truth Most High that He said: 'And few of My servants are grateful' (Saba' 13) and He said in narration from Iblis: 'And you will not find most of them grateful' (Al-A'raf 17). And let us suffice with this amount of exposition in this place, for it is a sea with no shore.

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a definition of the limits of human knowledge of God, the presentation of the fourth proof for the impossibility of detailed knowledge, and an explanation of the rarity of those who attain true knowledge. The author begins by stating that all of creation has no knowledge of God's majesty except a general, summarized understanding. This is because we only know God through His effects in us, not in His essence or detailed attributes. Then the author presents the fourth proof: transition from the known to the unknown can only occur through three types of reasoning: (1) from cause to effect, (2) from equivalent to equivalent, and (3) from effect to cause. The first two are not applicable to God (since He has no cause and no equivalent), so the only valid path is from effect to cause—ascending from creation to the Creator. Applying this to the human rational soul, which is the lowest of the pure spiritual beings, we find that it can ascend by knowing itself, then its cause, then the cause of its cause, and so on until the Necessary of Existence. The author supports this with Quranic verses. However, because the levels of intermediaries (from the sensible world to the intelligible world to the divine presence) are many and hidden, and human power is weak, most souls remain at lower levels. Only a few escape the sensible world to the imaginal world, and fewer still reach the intelligible world, and among those, most are overwhelmed by the lights of the sanctified spirits unless supported by divine power. The author uses astrological metaphors (Mars, Jupiter, Saturn) to describe the spiritual ascent but these are likely symbolic. Ultimately, only an extremely rare soul reaches the sanctified presence. The author concludes that these are hints for the introduction to this science, and that even a little of these noble investigations is much for souls, quoting Quranic verses about gratitude. He ends by stating that this topic is a boundless sea.

عالم المحسوسات (world of sensible things)
عالم الخيالات (world of imaginations)
عالم المعقولات (world of intelligibles)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that everyone can achieve detailed knowledge of God through spiritual effort.
Correction: The author argues that only a very few souls, with divine support, can reach even a glimmer of divine light. Most are stuck in lower worlds.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The ascent to detailed knowledge of God is extremely rare, with most souls remaining in lower worlds; only a few, divinely supported, reach the divine presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If most souls remain in the world of senses, where do you honestly find yourself right now? What are the top three sensory attachments that keep you from ascending? Will you begin detaching from at least one of them today?

The passage says the soul ascends 'from knowledge of itself to knowledge of its cause.' Do you truly know your own soul? When was the last time you spent an hour in honest self-reflection, not distracted by your phone or worldly noise? If not, what does that say about your desire to know God?

Rarity is emphasized: 'the few of My servants are grateful.' Examine your own gratitude: list five blessings you have not thanked God for today in a manner that changed your heart. If gratitude is the opening of ascent, are you among the few or the many? Commit to one specific act of deep gratitude before you sleep.

Two Ways to Know God and the First Way: Contingency Argument
Author enumerates two ways and details the first rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two general ways to knowledge of God and the structure of the classical contingency argument.

Core Idea
All contingent things point to a Necessary Existent.

The author begins by asking whether there is one or multiple paths to the knowledge of God. He reveals that people of insight recognize two fundamental ways: one of rational reflection and one of spiritual discipline. The first way, which he elaborates here, is the rational proof from contingency—a cornerstone of Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ways
Read the first part to see how the author distinguishes between the way of reflection and the way of spiritual discipline. Notice the terminology.
The two ways are not mutually exclusive but complementary.
2
Understand the four premises
The contingency argument rests on four key premises: contingency of the world, need for a preponderator, need for an originator, and impossibility of regress/circularity. List them in order.
These premises are established before the conclusion.
3
Trace the conclusion
See how the premises force the mind to accept a Necessary Existent. The author states it as a necessary termination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في أن تحصيل هذه المعارف المقدمة، هل الطريقة الواحد ، أم أكثر من واحد ؟ اعلم أنه قد انكشف لأرباب البصائر : أن الطريق إليه من وجهين : أحدهما : طريق أصحاب النظر والاستدلال. والثاني : طريق أصحاب الرياضة والمجاهدة. أما الطريق الأول، وهو طريق الحكماء الإلهيين: فهو الاستدلال بأحوال الممكنات على اثبات موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك لأنه لما ثبت أن هذه الموجودات المحسوسات ممكنة ومحدثة، وثبت أن الممكن محتاج إلى المرجح، وثبت أن المحدث محتاج إلى المحدث، وثبت أن التسلسل والدور محالان، فحينئذ يجب انتهاء هذه الموجودات إلى موجود قديم ازلي واجب الوجود لذاته. واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس أبا علي بن سينا ذكر في كتاب الإرشادات: أن ههنا طريقا يدل على إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، بحسب اعتبار حال الوجود من حيث إنه وجود قال: «ولا حاجة فيه إلى اعتبار حال وجود غيره فإنا نقول لا شك أن في الوجود موجودا. فنقول ذلك الموجود إن كان واجبا لذاته فهو المقصود، وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فلا بد له من الواجب لذاته فثبت أن اعتبار حال الموجود من حيث إنه وجود يشهد بوجود موجود هو واجب الوجود لذاته فهذا ما قاله ثم رجح هذا الطريق على الطريق الذي يستدل فيه بإمكان ما سواه على وجوده.
The third chapter on whether the method for acquiring these preliminary knowledges is one or more than one. Know that it has been revealed to people of insight that the way to Him is from two aspects: one is the way of the people of reflection and inference, and the second is the way of the people of spiritual discipline and struggle. As for the first way, which is the way of the divine philosophers, it is inference from the states of contingent things to prove an existent that is necessary of existence in itself. This is because when it is established that these sensible existents are contingent and originated, and it is established that the contingent needs a preponderator, and it is established that the originated needs an originator, and it is established that regress and circularity are impossible, then it necessarily follows that these existents terminate at an eternal existent, necessary of existence in itself. And know that the Chief Master Abu Ali ibn Sina mentioned in the Book of Indications that there is a way here that indicates the proof of the Necessary of Existence in itself, by considering the state of existence insofar as it is existence. He said: 'There is no need in it to consider the state of existence of something else, for we say: there is no doubt that in existence there is an existent. Then we say: that existent, if it is necessary in itself, then it is the intended; and if it is contingent in itself, then it must have the necessary in itself. So it is established that considering the state of the existent insofar as it is existence testifies to the existence of an existent that is necessary of existence in itself.' That is what he said. Then he preferred this way over the way that infers from the possibility of what is other than Him to His existence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage begins the third chapter by asking whether the method for obtaining the preliminary knowledges (of God) is one or multiple. The author states that two ways are clear to those with insight: the way of rational reflection and inference (nazar wa istidlal), and the way of spiritual discipline and struggle (riyada wa mujahada). He then expounds the first way, that of the divine philosophers (hukama' ilahiyyun). This way infers from the states (ahwal) of contingent things (mumkinat) to the existence of a Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). The argument proceeds by establishing four premises: (1) sensible existents are contingent and originated; (2) the contingent needs a preponderator (murajjih); (3) the originated needs an originator (muhdith); (4) infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr) are impossible. From these, it necessarily follows that these existents must terminate at an eternal, necessary existent. The author then cites Ibn Sina's alternative argument from the Book of Indications (Kitab al-Isharat). Ibn Sina argues that by considering existence as such, without needing to examine other existents, we can say: existence contains an existent; if it is necessary in itself, that is the goal; if it is contingent, it must depend on the necessary. Thus, existence itself testifies to a Necessary Existent. Ibn Sina preferred this argument to the one that uses the possibility of other things. The passage thus presents two formulations of the rational way.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
مرجح
تسلسل
دور
حكماء إلهيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many premises does the author list for the first way (contingency argument)?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The rational path to God builds from the contingency of the world through a chain of premises to the necessity of a First Cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that you are a contingent being, created after non-existence. Yet do you live each day as if you are self-sufficient? Where in your life today will you remind yourself that your very existence hangs by a thread sustained by the Necessary Existent?
?
The argument forces you to confront the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes. Have you been putting off the ultimate question of your purpose, hoping that one more achievement or possession will be the final answer? What would it mean to stop that regress today and submit to the Necessary Existent?
?
The rational path requires you to be a 'person of reflection and inference' (nazar wa istidlal). Reflect on whether you truly engage your intellect to know God, or do you rely only on spiritual feelings? What step of this contingency argument challenges your current understanding of God's existence, and how will you let it reshape your conviction?

Ibn Sina's Argument from Existence Itself
Ibn Sina presents a proof from existence as such.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's alternative proof for the Necessary Existent that relies only on the concept of existence, without needing to consider other existents.

Core Idea
Existence itself, when considered, reveals the Necessary Existent.

After presenting the classical contingency argument, the author cites Ibn Sina's more refined approach. Ibn Sina argues that we can prove the Necessary Existent simply by analyzing what it means for something to exist, without needing to look at the world's contingency. His argument is more a priori and elegant.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's reasoning
Read the passage from Ibn Sina carefully. He begins by noting that there is an existent. Then he divides it into two cases: necessary or contingent.
2
Follow the conditional logic
If the existent is necessary, we are done. If it is contingent, it requires a necessary cause. Either way, a Necessary Existent is established.
This is a form of the argument from the impossibility of a contingent regress without a necessary foundation.
3
Compare with the first way
Notice that Ibn Sina's argument does not rely on the four premises about the sensible world. It works from the mere fact that something exists.
This is considered a more fundamental proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في أن تحصيل هذه المعارف المقدمة، هل الطريقة الواحد ، أم أكثر من واحد ؟ اعلم أنه قد انكشف لأرباب البصائر : أن الطريق إليه من وجهين : أحدهما : طريق أصحاب النظر والاستدلال. والثاني : طريق أصحاب الرياضة والمجاهدة. أما الطريق الأول، وهو طريق الحكماء الإلهيين: فهو الاستدلال بأحوال الممكنات على اثبات موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك لأنه لما ثبت أن هذه الموجودات المحسوسات ممكنة ومحدثة، وثبت أن الممكن محتاج إلى المرجح، وثبت أن المحدث محتاج إلى المحدث، وثبت أن التسلسل والدور محالان، فحينئذ يجب انتهاء هذه الموجودات إلى موجود قديم ازلي واجب الوجود لذاته. واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس أبا علي بن سينا ذكر في كتاب الإرشادات: أن ههنا طريقا يدل على إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، بحسب اعتبار حال الوجود من حيث إنه وجود قال: «ولا حاجة فيه إلى اعتبار حال وجود غيره فإنا نقول لا شك أن في الوجود موجودا. فنقول ذلك الموجود إن كان واجبا لذاته فهو المقصود، وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فلا بد له من الواجب لذاته فثبت أن اعتبار حال الموجود من حيث إنه وجود يشهد بوجود موجود هو واجب الوجود لذاته فهذا ما قاله ثم رجح هذا الطريق على الطريق الذي يستدل فيه بإمكان ما سواه على وجوده.
The third chapter on whether the method for acquiring these preliminary knowledges is one or more than one. Know that it has been revealed to people of insight that the way to Him is from two aspects: one is the way of the people of reflection and inference, and the second is the way of the people of spiritual discipline and struggle. As for the first way, which is the way of the divine philosophers, it is inference from the states of contingent things to prove an existent that is necessary of existence in itself. This is because when it is established that these sensible existents are contingent and originated, and it is established that the contingent needs a preponderator, and it is established that the originated needs an originator, and it is established that regress and circularity are impossible, then it necessarily follows that these existents terminate at an eternal existent, necessary of existence in itself. And know that the Chief Master Abu Ali ibn Sina mentioned in the Book of Indications that there is a way here that indicates the proof of the Necessary of Existence in itself, by considering the state of existence insofar as it is existence. He said: 'There is no need in it to consider the state of existence of something else, for we say: there is no doubt that in existence there is an existent. Then we say: that existent, if it is necessary in itself, then it is the intended; and if it is contingent in itself, then it must have the necessary in itself. So it is established that considering the state of the existent insofar as it is existence testifies to the existence of an existent that is necessary of existence in itself.' That is what he said. Then he preferred this way over the way that infers from the possibility of what is other than Him to His existence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage begins the third chapter by asking whether the method for obtaining the preliminary knowledges (of God) is one or multiple. The author states that two ways are clear to those with insight: the way of rational reflection and inference (nazar wa istidlal), and the way of spiritual discipline and struggle (riyada wa mujahada). He then expounds the first way, that of the divine philosophers (hukama' ilahiyyun). This way infers from the states (ahwal) of contingent things (mumkinat) to the existence of a Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). The argument proceeds by establishing four premises: (1) sensible existents are contingent and originated; (2) the contingent needs a preponderator (murajjih); (3) the originated needs an originator (muhdith); (4) infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr) are impossible. From these, it necessarily follows that these existents must terminate at an eternal, necessary existent. The author then cites Ibn Sina's alternative argument from the Book of Indications (Kitab al-Isharat). Ibn Sina argues that by considering existence as such, without needing to examine other existents, we can say: existence contains an existent; if it is necessary in itself, that is the goal; if it is contingent, it must depend on the necessary. Thus, existence itself testifies to a Necessary Existent. Ibn Sina preferred this argument to the one that uses the possibility of other things. The passage thus presents two formulations of the rational way.

Key Terms

ممكن
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's argument in the passage, what is the basis for proving the Necessary Existent?
A) The contingency of the world
B) The concept of existence itself
C) The need for a preponderator
D) Spiritual discipline

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument shows that even without observing the world's contingency, the mere fact that something exists proves a Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist right now. Are you necessary or contingent? If contingent, then your existence at every moment depends on the Necessary Existent. Have you felt that dependence today? What would change if you lived with constant awareness that you are nothing without Him?
?
Ibn Sina says the proof is immediate from the mere fact of existence. When you look at your own life—your breath, your heartbeat—do you see them as signs pointing to the One who sustains them? Or do you see only the things themselves? What keeps you from seeing the Necessary Existent behind every existent?
?
If the Necessary Existent is the ground of all existence, then your very being is borrowed from Him. What would it mean to return that loan? How would you live differently if you truly believed that you exist only because He keeps you in existence?

Why Ibn Sina's Argument Falls Short: The Necessity of Contingency
Critique of Ibn Sina's proof and restatement of the contingency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina's alternative proof is insufficient and how the standard contingency proof remains the only valid way to establish a Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's argument still relies on the very premise it tries to avoid: contingency needs a preponderator.

The author has just presented the classic contingency argument. Now he turns to critique a rival proof attributed to Ibn Sina. While Ibn Sina attempted to prove a Necessary Existent directly from the division of existence, the author shows that this move secretly depends on the same contingency premise. Moreover, it fails to distinguish the Necessary Existent from physical bodies. This lesson unpacks that critique and restates the sound method.

Guided Path
1
Understand the critique's starting point
Read the passage carefully. Note that the author first states that Ibn Sina's statement is 'not strong.' Then he explains why: the division of existence into necessary and contingent already presupposes the need for a preponderator.
Watch out for the logical move.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage contains two objections: (1) Ibn Sina's argument is itself an inference from contingency, so it doesn't avoid that premise. (2) Even if it proved a Necessary Existent, it doesn't show that the Necessary Existent is not the physical world itself.
3
Trace the correct method
The author then lays out the correct method: prove contingency of sensible things, then need for a preponderator, then impossibility of regress and circularity. Only then can we conclude a Necessary Existent.
This is the standard three-step proof.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Revisit the core idea: Ibn Sina's argument is not self-sufficient. The contingency premise is indispensable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم ان البحث المستقصى يدل على أن هذا الكلام ليس بقوي وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا : الموجود إما واجب لذاته، أو ممكن لذاته، فإن كان ممكنا لذاته، امتنع رجحان وجوده على عدمه إلا المرجح، فهذا استدلال بوجود الممكن على وجود الواجب، فثبت أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات واجب الوجود إلا بهذه المقدمة. وأيضا فهب أن الطريق الذي ذكره يدل على إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته، إلا أنه يبقى الشك في أن ذلك الموجود الواجب لذاته، هل هو هذه الأجسام أو غيرها؟ فما لم يقم البرهان على أن هذه الأجسام ممكنة لذواتها، لم يقدر على الحكم باحتياجها في وجودها إلى المؤثر والمرجح . فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن معرفة واجب الوجود لذاته، لا تحصل إلا إذا اعتبرنا أحوال وجود هذه المحسوسات، فإنا إذا بينا أنها ممكنة لذواتها، ثم بينا أن الممكن لذاته لا بد له من المرجح ، ثم بينا أن التسلسل والدور باطلان، فعند ذلك يمكننا الجزم بإثبات موجود واجب الوجود لذاته. فهذا حاصل الكلام في الاستدلال بوجود غير الله على وجود الله تعالى .
And know that comprehensive investigation indicates that this statement is not strong. This is because if we say: the existent is either necessary in itself or contingent in itself. If it is contingent in itself, it is impossible that its existence be preponderant over its non-existence except by a preponderator, so this is an inference from the existence of the contingent to the existence of the Necessary. So it is established that there is no way to prove the Necessary of Existence except by this premise. Also, suppose that the way he mentioned indicates the proof of the Necessary of Existence in itself, but the doubt remains whether that existent necessary in itself is these bodies or something else. So as long as the proof has not been established that these bodies are contingent in themselves, one cannot judge their need for an effect and preponderator in their existence. So it is established by what we have mentioned that knowledge of the Necessary of Existence in itself is not obtained unless we consider the states of existence of these sensible things. For if we show that they are contingent in themselves, then we show that the contingent in itself must have a preponderator, then we show that regress and circularity are false, then at that point we can certainly establish an existent necessary of existence in itself. This is the sum of the discourse on inferring from the existence of other than God to the existence of God, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Ibn Sina's alternative argument and a reaffirmation of the standard contingency proof. The author argues that Ibn Sina's statement is not strong (qawi). The reason: when we say 'the existent is either necessary or contingent,' if we posit a contingent existent, we immediately need a preponderator (murajjih) for its existence over non-existence. This itself is an inference from the existence of the contingent to the Necessary, which relies on the same premise about contingency. Thus, Ibn Sina's argument does not dispense with that premise. Moreover, even if his argument proves a Necessary Existent, it leaves unresolved whether that Necessary Existent is the physical bodies themselves or something else. To resolve this, one must first prove that bodies are contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi) by a separate proof. Therefore, the correct method is as follows: first, prove that sensible things are contingent; second, prove that the contingent needs a preponderator; third, prove that infinite regress and circularity are impossible; then, and only then, can we conclusively establish a Necessary Existent. This is the complete inference from the existence of other than God to God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ibn Sina's argument is a standalone proof that avoids the need to examine contingent beings.
Correction: The author shows that Ibn Sina's argument still depends on the premise that contingent beings need a cause, and it does not identify the Necessary Existent as distinct from the physical world.

Key Takeaway
The contingency argument is indispensable; any proof of God that claims to bypass it still relies on it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the proof of God requires you to genuinely see the world around you as contingent — not self-sufficient — where in your life do you still act as if things exist by themselves? What would it take to truly internalize that everything you rely on needs Allah at every moment?
?
The passage warns that a flawed proof leaves doubt about whether the Necessary Existent is the physical world or something else. Do you have any subtle belief that the universe can function without its Creator? How would proving bodies are contingent change the way you view your own existence?
?
This lesson shows that even great philosophers like Ibn Sina cannot skip the fundamental premise of contingency. Where are you tempted to take shortcuts in your faith — relying on arguments or feelings that haven't been grounded in the reality of your own dependence on Allah? What step will you take today to trace your existence back to its true Source?

The Second Premise: Intuitive or Demonstrative?
Razi presents the second premise and argues for its intuitive nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise of kalam (the possible needs a preponderant) and the argument that it is intuitive based on universal human behavior, including that of children.

Core Idea
Even children instinctively know that events need causes—this premise is innate.

Razi now turns to the second foundational premise: that every possible thing requires a preponderant cause. He notes that rational people disagree on whether this premise is intuitive or requires proof. He then presents the case for it being intuitive, drawing on the universal human tendency to seek causes, even in children.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opinions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi first states the two opinions: (1) the premise is intuitive, (2) it is demonstrative. Then he focuses on the first opinion.
2
Analyze the evidence for the intuitive view
Razi provides evidence: adults instinctively seek causes for new events, and even children display this when they question who took or placed something. Reflect on why this behavior supports the intuition claim.
The child example is particularly strong because it shows the premise is ingrained before rational maturity.
3
Connect to the broader theme
Consider why the intuitive nature of this premise matters for later arguments about God's existence. If the premise is intuitive, it needs no proof, making the entire framework more immediate.
This premise is a pillar of the cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية: بيان أن الممكن لا بد له من مرجح. اعلم أن العقلاء لهم في هذا الموقف قولان: الأول: أن هذه المقدمة بديهية، والثاني: أنها برهانية. أما القائلون بالقول الأول فقد احتجوا على صحة مذهبهم بأن قالوا: إنا رأينا جمهور العقلاء مطبقين على أنهم إذا أحسوا بحدوث حادث طلبوا له سبباً، وإذا سمعوا صوت إنسان اضطروا إلى العلم بحضور ذلك الإنسان، وإذا رأوا حدوث بناء قطعوا بوجود باني، بل نزيد ونقول: إن هذا العلم حاصل في نفوس الأطفال الذين لم يبلغوا إلى كمال العقل، وذلك لأن الطفل إذا كان له مكان وموضع يختص هو به بالتصرف فيه، فإذا وجد فيه طعاماً لم يضعه فيه، أو غاب عنه شيء وضعه فيه، فإنه يصيح، ويقول: من الذي أخذه؟ ومن الذي وضعه؟ وذلك يدل على أن فطرة ذلك الطفل تشهد بأن الممكن لا بد له من مرجح، والحادث لا بد له من محدث. وإذا كان هذا العلم مركوزاً في غريزة نفس ذلك الطفل، علمنا أنه أقوى العلوم البديهية.
The second premise: an explanation that the possible needs a preponderant. Know that the rational people hold two opinions in this situation: the first is that this premise is intuitive, and the second is that it is demonstrative. As for those who hold the first opinion, they argued for the correctness of their view by saying: We see the majority of rational people unanimously agree that when they sense a new event, they seek a cause for it; and when they hear the voice of a person, they are compelled to know the presence of that person; and when they see the occurrence of a building, they are certain of the existence of a builder. Moreover, we say: This knowledge exists in the souls of children who have not reached full reason. That is because a child, if he has a place which he specifically controls and in which he acts, and he finds food there that he did not put there, or something of his that he placed there goes missing, he will shout and say, 'Who took it?' and 'Who put it?' This indicates that the child's innate disposition bears witness that the possible must have a preponderant, and the originated must have an originator. And if this knowledge is ingrained in the nature of that child's soul, we know that it is among the strongest of intuitive sciences.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage begins the detailed discussion of the second premise: that the possible needs a preponderant. The author states that rational people have two opinions on this premise: one holds it is intuitive (بديهية), the other that it is demonstrative (برهانية). He then presents the argument of the first group. They base their claim on universal human practice: people instinctively seek causes for events. Even children display this awareness when they question who took something or who placed something. The author argues that this shows the premise is ingrained in human nature, making it one of the strongest intuitive truths.

Misconception: The need for a cause is a conclusion reached by reasoning, not an innate knowledge.
Correction: Razi argues it is intuitive, as shown by children's immediate reactions without prior reasoning, indicating it is ingrained in human nature.

The Proof That a Possible Can Occur Without a Preponderant
The author argues that a possible can occur without a preponderant by analyzing essence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a possible entity can occur without a preponderant, based on the conception of essences and the impossibility of circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Distinction among essences cannot be caused without infinite regress, so some possibilities need no preponderant.

In this passage, the author presents a sophisticated argument to challenge the assumption that every possible requires a preponderant. By analyzing how essences are distinguished from one another, he shows that the distinguishing factor itself cannot have a cause, thus proving that a possible can exist without a preponderant. Follow along as we unpack this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical flow from the nature of essences to the conclusion.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms: ذوات (dhāt, essences), واجب (wājib, necessary), ممكن (mumkin, possible), امتياز (imtiyāz, distinction), مرجح (murajjih, preponderant), and بديهة (badīhah, self-evidence).
These terms anchor the argument.
3
Understand the initial claim about essences
The author first establishes that essences share the attribute of being a dhāt, common to necessary, possible, material, and abstract. Notice how this sets up the problem of distinction.
4
Analyze the argument about distinction
Follow the reasoning: each essence is distinguished by a restriction. If that restriction were caused by another matter, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress, which are impossible. Therefore, the distinction occurs without a cause or preponderant.
Focus on the logical necessity: the specification must be uncaused.
5
Grasp the conclusion and its implication
The conclusion is that a possible can occur without a preponderant. The author also notes that this view is held by many rational thinkers, refuting the claim that the need for a preponderant is self-evident.
This directly challenges a common assumption in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنا إذا عقلنا الذوات . فإن عقلنا بعد ذلك : كونه واجباً لذاته ، بقي المعتقد الأول . وإن اعتقدنا كونه ممكناً [ لذاته ] زال اعتقاد كونه واجباً لذاته ، ولكن لا يزول اعتقاد كونه ذاتاً [ فثبت أن كونه ذاتاً ] قدر مشترك بين الواجب والممكن والمادي والمجرد ، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا شك أن ذات واجب الوجود ممتازة عن سائر الذوات بقيد من القيود ، [ وكذلك ذات العقل ممتازة عن ذات الجسم بقيد من القيود ] إذ لو لم يحصل أمر من الأمور، به يحصل الامتياز ، لما حصل الامتياز البتة ، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ثبت أن الذوات من حيث إنها ذوات أمور متساوية ، ثم إن كل واحد منها اختص بأمر لأجله امتاز عن غيره ، فاختصاص كل واحد منها بذلك المميز ، إن كان لأمر آخر لزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل وهما محالان ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص حاصلاً ، لا لأمر ولا لمرجح ، فثبت بهذا الدليل : وقوع الممكن لا عن مرجح ، وأيضاً : فهو قول قال به طائفة عظيمة من العقلاء . وكل ذلك . يبطل القول بأن افتقار الممكن إلى المرجح مقدمة معلومة بالبديهة
Second: When we conceive essences, then if we afterward conceive it as necessary in itself, the first belief remains. And if we believe it as possible [in itself], the belief that it is necessary in itself ceases, but the belief that it is an essence does not cease. [Thus it is established that its being an essence] is a common factor between the necessary, the possible, the material, and the abstract. When this is established, we say: There is no doubt that the essence of the Necessary Existence is distinguished from other essences by some restriction, [and likewise the essence of the intellect is distinguished from the essence of the body by some restriction]. For if no matter occurred by which distinction occurs, distinction would not occur at all. When this is established, we say: It is established that essences, insofar as they are essences, are equal matters. Then each one of them is specified by a matter by which it is distinguished from another. So the specification of each one of them by that distinguisher, if it were for another matter, would necessitate either circularity or infinite regress, both of which are impossible. Therefore that specification must occur not for a matter and not for a preponderant. Thus it is established by this proof: the occurrence of the possible not from a preponderant. Moreover, it is a statement held by a great group of rational people. And all of that invalidates the statement that the need of the possible for a preponderant is a self-evident premise.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a possible entity (mumkin) can occur without a preponderant (murajjih), which refutes the claim that the need for a preponderant is self-evident. The author begins by noting that when we conceive essences (dhāt), we can conceive them as necessary, possible, etc. The attribute of being an essence is common to all categories. Then he argues that each essence is distinguished from others by a restriction (qayd). If this restriction itself were caused by something else, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, the distinction must occur without any cause or preponderant. From this, he concludes that a possible can occur without a preponderant. He also notes that this view is held by a large group of rational thinkers, and this undermines the claim that the need for a preponderant is a self-evident premise.

Key Terms
ذوات (dhāt, essences)
واجب (wājib, necessary)
ممكن (mumkin, possible)
امتياز (imtiyāz, distinction)
مرجح (murajjih, preponderant)
بديهة (badīhah, self-evidence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need of a possible for a preponderant is a self-evident (badīhah) premise.
Correction: The author shows that a possible can occur without a preponderant through rational argument, and notes that many rational thinkers hold this view, thereby invalidating the claim that it is self-evident.

Checkpoint

A) All possibles require a preponderant.
B) A possible can occur without a preponderant.
C) The necessary being is not distinguished from possibles.
D) Essences are all equal and have no distinctions.

Key Takeaway
The specification of a possible essence's distinguishing feature cannot be caused, proving that a possible can occur without a preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that some realities are simply distinct without any cause, does it challenge your assumption that everything must have a reason? How does that reshape your trust in God's absolute sovereignty over creation?
?
The proof hinges on the necessity of avoiding infinite regress. In what areas of your own life do you assume an infinite chain of justifications? Are you willing to stop at God as the ultimate ground without needing further justification?
?
If the need for a preponderant is not self-evident, then your certainty about causality might be less firm than you thought. What would it mean for your daily reliance on cause and effect if you truly accepted that some things occur without a preponderant? How would that change your reliance on God?

The Original Disposition Does Not Prove Necessity
Refutation of using original disposition to prove necessity of cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the original disposition cannot be used as a proof for the necessity of a cause, and how the author defends this by clarifying the criterion for sound intuitive knowledge.

Core Idea
The original disposition only serves as a proof when free from sophistry and disputation.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that seeks to differentiate between two types of causal necessity: the building needing a builder versus needing prior material. The objector claims that only the first is universally agreed upon. The author argues that this distinction fails because the criterion for sound intuitive knowledge must be the disposition free from sophistry, and in that case both propositions are equally evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage and identify the objection: the objector says people universally agree that a building needs a builder, but not that it needs prior material, because theologians disagree.
The objection is in the part starting with 'فإن قالوا'.
2
Understand the author's reply
Notice how the author responds by defining the criterion for distinguishing intuitive propositions: the judgment of the original disposition that is not accustomed to sophistry and debate.
The author emphasizes that the majority of people judge both propositions similarly.
3
Examine the symmetry argument
The author points out that if the existence of a small opposing group damages self-evidence, then both propositions are equally damaged. If such opposition is irrelevant, then both rely on the same sound disposition.
This shows the objection cannot differentiate between the two cases.
4
Conclude the argument
The conclusion is clear: there is no way to differentiate between the two propositions based on original disposition.
The passage ends with 'فظهر أنه لا سبيل إلى الفرق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفطرة الأصلية لا يدل على كون ذلك الحكم صحيحاً . فإن قالوا إنهم مطبقون بحكم الفطرة الأصلية على افتقار البناء إلى الباني ، وليسوا مطبقين على افتقار البناء إلى المادة السابقة بدليل أن المتكلمين يخالفونهم في صحة هذه المقدمة فنقول في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : إنا إذا أردنا تمييز القضايا الفطرية عن غيرها ، فالمعتبر في ذلك التمييز حكم الفطر الأصلية التي لم تتعود المشاغبات والمجادلات ولم تألف التزام المكابرات ، ومن المعلوم أن حكم جمهور الخلق في إحدى القضيتين مثل حكمهم في القضية الأخرى . بقي أن يقال : إن افتقار البناء إلى المادة السابقة يخالف فيه طائفة من المتكلمين . فيقال : وافتقار البناء إلى الباني يخالف فيه قوم آخرون ، فإن كان ظهور المخالفة من الجمع القليل قادحاً في كون القضية بديهية ، فهذا المعنى حاصل في الطرفين، وإن كان لا عبرة بموافقة أهل الجدل والشغب وبمخالفتهم ، وإنما العبرة بحكم الفطرة الأصلية والنفوس الخالية عن النقوش الباطلة ، فهذا المعنى حاصل في البابين فظهر أنه لا سبيل إلى الفرق .
The original disposition does not indicate that that judgment is correct. If they say: 'They are agreed by the judgment of the original disposition that the building needs a builder, and they are not agreed that the building needs prior material, as evidenced by the theologians opposing them on the validity of this premise,' we say in answer to this question: When we wish to distinguish original intuitive propositions from others, the criterion in that distinction is the judgment of the original dispositions that are not accustomed to sophistries and debates and have not familiarized themselves with adhering to stubbornness. And it is known that the judgment of the majority of people in one of the two propositions is like their judgment in the other proposition. It remains to be said: 'A group of theologians oppose the building's need for prior material.' It is said: 'And other groups oppose the building's need for a builder.' If the appearance of opposition from a small group is detrimental to the proposition being self-evident, this meaning is present in both sides. And if there is no consideration given to the agreement of the people of disputation and sophistry or to their opposition, and the only consideration is the judgment of the original disposition and souls free from false imprints, this meaning is present in both cases. Thus it is clear that there is no way to differentiate.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The objector argues that while people universally agree that a building needs a builder, they do not universally agree that a building needs prior material, because some theologians disagree. The author counters by clarifying what 'original disposition' means: it must be free from the influence of sophistry and contentious argument. The majority of people judge both propositions similarly. The objector points out that some theologians deny the need for prior material, but the author retorts that some also deny the need for a builder. If the existence of a small opposing group damages self-evidence, then both propositions are equally damaged. If the opinions of contentious people are irrelevant, then both cases are equally based on the sound disposition. Therefore, no distinction can be made between the two propositions.

Key Terms
الفطرة الأصلية
القضايا الفطرية
البديهية
المكابرة
الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The original disposition always gives correct judgment regardless of external influences.
Correction: The author clarifies that the original disposition only yields valid intuitive knowledge when it is not habituated to sophistry, debate, and stubbornness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for distinguishing genuine intuitive propositions from others?
A) The agreement of all theologians
B) The judgment of the original disposition free from sophistry and debate
C) The opinion of the majority of people regardless of their disposition
D) The absence of any opposing views

Key Takeaway
The original disposition cannot be used to prove a proposition if the disposition is tainted by sophistry and disputation; both propositions about necessity are equally intuitive when the disposition is sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong belief, do you ever examine whether it comes from a sound, untrained fitra or from years of hearing only one side of an argument? Are you willing to face that honestly?
?
The passage says the majority's fitra judges both propositions similarly. But your own heart might lean towards one as 'obvious' and reject the other. Could that imbalance be a sign that your disposition has been shaped by sophistry rather than truth? What would it cost you to submit both judgments to the same criterion?
?
Imagine you are debating someone who denies something you consider self-evident. Before you dismiss them, ask yourself: is your own position free from the imprints of false arguments and stubbornness? If not, how can you claim to follow the sound fitra?

Second Argument: Parity Between Cause and Time/Place
Imam Raghib argues that the universal agreement on the need for time/place parallels the agreement on the need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Raghib al-Isfahani uses the agreement of rational people on the need for time and place to argue for the necessity of a cause, and why the opponent cannot consistently deny one while accepting the other.

Core Idea
The certainty of needing a cause is no stronger than needing a time and place.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful parity argument. He observes that rational people universally agree that every event occurs in a specific time and place. Theologians even use this to argue that time and place must be eternal. But the opponent thinks that the need for a cause is more certain. The author shows that the agreement on the need for an agent is not stronger than the agreement on the need for time and place. If the opponent denies one, he must deny the other; if he accepts one, he must accept the other. This reveals the inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms: العقلاء, الزمان, المكان, الفاعل, الحادث. Notice the structure of the argument: it starts by stating the agreement, then the theologians' claim, then the parity argument.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Understand the parity argument
Identify the two agreements: (1) events need specific time/place, (2) events need an agent. The author says neither is stronger than the other. So they must be treated consistently.
This is a logical argument, not a denial of cause.
3
Reflect on the opponent's inconsistency
Ask yourself: Why would the opponent accept the need for time/place but deny the need for a cause? The author shows that this is inconsistent. The argument uses a principle of parity.
This is a common theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنا نرى العقلاء مطبقين على أن الشيء لا يحدث إلا في زمان معين ومكان معين ثم إن المتكلمين زعموا أن حدوث الشيء يتوقف على سبق زمان معين ومكان معين، وإلا لزم القول بقدم الزمان والمكان ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إطباق العقلاء على افتقار الحادث إلى الفاعل ليس أقوى عند العقل الأول من إطباقهم على افتقار الحادث إلى الزمان والمكان، فإن جاز التكذيب في أحد البابين جاز في الباب الثاني ، وإن وجب التصديق في أحد البابين ، وجب في الباب الثاني فثبت أن الفرق باطل .
And the second: We see rational people agreed that a thing does not occur except in a specific time and specific place. Then the theologians claimed that the occurrence of a thing depends on the precedence of a specific time and specific place, otherwise it would necessitate saying that time and place are eternal. When this is established, we say: The agreement of rational people that a contingent event needs an agent is not stronger, to the first intellect, than their agreement that a contingent event needs time and place. For if denial is allowed in one of the two cases, it is allowed in the second case; and if affirmation is required in one of the two cases, it is required in the second case. Thus it is established that the distinction is invalid.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is the second argument against the opponent's claim. The author points out that rational people universally agree that every event occurs at a specific time and place. Theologians themselves hold this, as they say that change depends on time and place. The opponent thinks that the need for a cause is more certain than the need for time and place. But the author argues that the agreement on the need for an agent is not stronger than the agreement on the need for time and place. If denial of the need for time and place is possible, then denial of the need for a cause is also possible. Conversely, if affirmation is required for one, it is required for the other. Since the opponent cannot prove a distinction, his argument fails.

Key Terms
العقلاء
الزمان
المكان
الفاعل
الحادث
الإطباق
الافتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies that events need a cause.
Correction: The author only argues that the opponent is inconsistent; he does not deny causation but uses parity to show the opponent's reasoning is flawed.

Checkpoint
What does the author use the example of time and place to show?
A) That time and place are eternal
B) That the need for an agent is more certain than the need for time and place
C) That agreement on the need for an agent is not stronger than agreement on the need for time and place
D) That theologians deny the need for time and place

Key Takeaway
The agreement on the need for a cause is as certain as the agreement on the need for time and place; therefore, one cannot accept one and deny the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself doubting the need for a Creator because you cannot see Him, do you also doubt the need for time and place because you cannot see them? What does this lesson reveal about your inconsistency?
?
If you accept without question that every event requires a specific time and place, why do you hesitate to accept that every event requires a specific agent? Where in your life are you making this distinction, and what does that say about your trust in your own intellect?
?
The first intellect recognizes the need for a cause as clearly as it recognizes the need for time and place. Which of these two truths do you find harder to live by, and why? How will you close that gap starting today?

The Fourth Objection: First Proof
Objection and first proof: comparing necessity of cause to arithmetic certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that knowledge of the need for a cause is not necessary knowledge, and how the debate hinges on comparing certainty between propositions.

Core Idea
Certainty varies; causality is less certain than basic arithmetic.

This passage presents the first proof that knowledge of the need for a cause is not necessary. The objector compares it with the self-evident truth that one is half of two, arguing that the difference in strength shows causality is only probable. The discussion explores whether the difference is in the assent itself or merely in the clarity of the concepts involved, and the author defends the self-evident nature of the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first proof
Start by reading the fourth objection and the first proof carefully. Notice how the objector sets up a comparison between two propositions.
2
Understand the comparison
Focus on the comparison between 'one is half of two' and 'the possible needs a preponderator'. The objector argues that the first is clearer, implying the second is not certain.
The key term here is 'مرجوح' (weaker) and 'ظني' (probable).
3
Examine the counter-objection
Read the counter-objection that the difference might be due to ease of conception, not the strength of assent itself. This is a subtle but important distinction.
The objector concedes difference but attributes it to 'تصور' (conception) rather than 'تصديق' (assent).
4
Grasp the author's final answer
The author replies that the difference is self-evident and known by intellectual intuition. Denying it is mere obstinacy.
The passage ends with a principle about distinguishing necessary from non-necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : أن نقول : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن العلم بافتقار الممكن والمحدث إلى المؤثر علم ضروري، فههنا وجوه تدل على أن هذا العلم ليس بضروري وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا عرضنا على عقولنا : أن الواحد نصف الاثنين ، وعرضنا أيضاً على عقولنا : أن الممكن لا بد له من مرجح ، وجدنا حكم العقول بالقضية الأولى أظهر من حكمها بالثانية والتفاوت بين الحكمين في القوة يدل على أن احتمال النقيض حاصل في المرجوحية ، وذلك يدل على أن هذه المرجوحية ظنية لا يقينية فإن قيل : لا نسلم وقوع التفاوت في جزم العقل بهاتين القضيتين ، فما الدليل عليه ؟ سلمنا أنه قد يقع التفاوت بين هذين الحكمين في بعض الأوقات ، إلا أنه قد يحصل التساوي بينهما في القوة في بعض أوقات ، فإن من سمع صوت إنسان ، فإنه يعلم بالضرورة حضور ذلك الإنسان ، ويكون هذا العلم مساوياً في القوة للعلم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين . سلمنا حصول التفاوت مطلقاً ولكن لا نسلم أن ذلك التفاوت عائد إلى التصديق ، بل هو عائد إلى التصور وبيانه : وهو أن التصديق يتوقف على تصور طرفي القضية ، وتصور أن قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين : تصورات جلية غنية عن التعريف لأنه ليس فيها إلا تصور الواحد والاثنين والنصف، وهذه التصورات جلية غنية عن التعريف بخلاف قولنا : الممكن يفتقر إلى المؤثر ، فإن هذا التصديق يتوقف على تصور ماهية الممكن وعلى تصور ماهية الافتقار ، وعلى تصور ماهية المؤثر ، وهذه الأمور الثلاثة تصوراتها تصورات غامضة خفية ، فالتفاوت الحاصل في عقول الخلق من هذين الحكمين، إنما وقع بسبب حصول التفاوت بين تصوراتها ، فأما أن يقال : إن ذلك التفاوت وقع في نفس تلك التصديقات ، فهذا ممنوع . فما الدليل عليه ؟ سلمنا حصول التفاوت بين التصديقات . فلم قلتم : إنه لما كان أحد التصديقين أقوى من الثاني لزم كون التصديق المرجوح ظنيا ويمتنع كونه يقينياً ، وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ والجواب : - أما قوله : لا تسلم وقوع التفاوت بين حكمنا بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين وبين حكمنا بأن الممكن لا يترجع أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجع ) . فنقول : هذا التفاوت معلوم ببديهية العقل، فإنكاره إنكار للبديهي ، ولو جاز لكم أن تصروا على هذا الإنكار، على سبيل المكابرة ، جاز لغيركم أيضاً أن يقول : إني لا أجد من نفسي ، جزم العقل بأن المحدث ، لا بد له من محدث وبالجملة : فالأصل المعتبر في الفرق بين البديهيات وبين غيرها : ما يجده
The fourth question: We say: If what you have mentioned indicates that the knowledge that the possible (mumkin) and the originated (muḥdath) are in need of an efficient cause (muʼaththir) is necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī), then here are arguments indicating that this knowledge is not necessary. Its explanation is from several aspects: The first: If we present to our minds that one is half of two, and also present to our minds that the possible must have a preponderator (murajjiḥ), we find the judgment of the minds regarding the first proposition is more evident than their judgment regarding the second. The difference between the two judgments in strength indicates that the possibility of the opposite exists in the weaker one, and that indicates that this weaker judgment is probable (ẓannī) and not certain (yaqīnī). If it is said: We do not concede that there is a difference in the mind's certainty regarding these two propositions; what is the proof of it? We concede that a difference might occur between these two judgments at some times, but it might happen that equality between them in strength occurs at some times. For if a person hears the sound of a human, he knows necessarily the presence of that human, and this knowledge equals in strength the knowledge that one is half of two. We concede the occurrence of difference absolutely, but we do not concede that this difference goes back to the assent (taṣdīq); rather it goes back to the conception (taṣawwur). Its explanation: Assent depends on conceiving the two terms of the proposition. The conception of our saying 'one is half of two' is clear conceptions that need no definition [because it only contains the conception of one, two, and half, and these conceptions are clear and need no definition], unlike our saying 'the possible is in need of an efficient cause', for this assent depends on conceiving the quiddity of the possible, and on conceiving the quiddity of need, and on conceiving the quiddity of the efficient cause. These three things — their conceptions are obscure and hidden. So the difference that occurs in the minds of creatures regarding these two judgments occurs only due to the difference between their conceptions. As for saying that this difference occurs in the assents themselves, this is denied. So what is the proof of it? We concede the occurrence of difference between the assents. So why do you say that when one of the two assents is stronger than the second, it necessitates that the weaker assent is probable and it is impossible for it to be certain? And what is the proof that it is so? The answer: As for his saying: 'You do not concede the occurrence of difference between our judgment that one is half of two and our judgment that the possible does not preponderate one of its two sides without a preponderator,' we say: This difference is known by intellectual intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql); denying it is a denial of the self-evident. And if it is permissible for you to persist in this denial by way of obstinacy, it would be permissible for others to say: 'I do not find in myself the mind's certainty that the originated must have an originator.' In summary: The reliable principle in distinguishing between self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt) and others is what one finds...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is the fourth objection against the claim that knowledge of the need for a cause is necessary. The author proposes to show proofs that it is not necessary. The first proof compares two propositions: 'one is half of two' (clear) and 'the possible needs a preponderator' (less clear). The difference in strength indicates the latter is probable, not certain. An objection follows: perhaps there is no difference, or the difference is due to ease of conception, not the assent itself. The author answers by insisting that the difference is intuitive and self-evident; denying it is obstinacy. The passage ends with a general principle about distinguishing necessary from non-necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
مرجح
تصديق
تصور
بديهي
ظني
يقيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a judgment is less clear than another, it is automatically not certain, but the author argues that the difference might be due to conception, not the certainty of the assent itself.
Correction: The passage shows that difference in strength between judgments does not necessarily mean the weaker is not certain; it could be due to the obscurity of concepts. However, in this case, the author insists the difference is self-evident and the weaker is indeed probable.

Checkpoint
In the first proof of the fourth objection, what does the difference in strength between the two judgments indicate?
A) That the weaker judgment is false.
B) That the weaker judgment is probable (ظني) and not certain.
C) That the weaker judgment is equally certain.
D) That the weaker judgment is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof that knowledge of causality is not necessary rests on comparing it with a self-evident truth; the difference in clarity shows it is only probable, not certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your belief that everything that begins to exist has a cause, do you find it as certain as the fact that one is half of two? If not, what does that tell you about the strength of your conviction? Are you willing to let this uncertainty shake your reliance on rational arguments for God's existence, or does it challenge you to seek deeper certainty? What will you do about it today?
?
The objector suggests that the difference in certainty might be due to the difficulty of concepts, not the truth itself. Have you ever dismissed a deep theological truth because it was hard to understand, while accepting easier ones? Is it possible that you have remained comfortably uncertain about fundamental beliefs because you never wrestled with the concepts? Will you commit to studying the difficult concepts until they become clear?
?
The author insists that the difference in certainty between the two propositions is self-evident and that denying it is obstinacy. Is there any area of your faith where you are being obstinate, refusing to see clear evidence because it demands a change in your life or worldview? Are you truly open to the truth even if it is uncomfortable? What specific step can you take today to humble your heart before the intellect that God gave you?
