The Objection: Certainty Difference Due to Conception
Objector argues that certainty difference arises from conception clarity, not assent strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that the difference in certainty between two judgments is due to the clarity of underlying conceptions, not the nature of assent itself.

Core Idea
Certainty varies because some concepts are clear and others obscure, not because assent itself is stronger or weaker.

The discussion begins with an objection: while it is conceded that there is a difference between judgments like 'one is half of two' and 'the contingent needs a cause,' the objector denies that this difference lies in the assent itself. Instead, he argues it is due to the conceptions required for each judgment. This lesson unpacks that objection and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, focusing on the objector's main claim and the examples he uses.
2
Identify the key claim
The objector states that the difference is in the conception (tasawwur), not in the assent (tasdiq). Pinpoint where he makes this claim.
The phrase 'بل هو عائد إلى التصور' is the pivot.
3
Understand the explanation with examples
The objector explains that assent depends on conception. He contrasts 'one is half of two' (clear concepts) with 'the contingent needs a cause' (obscure concepts). Notice why the latter requires deeper understanding.
4
Examine the second objection
The objector then asks: even if we grant a difference in assents, why does the weaker assent become mere opinion? This sets up the author's response.
This second question challenges the assumption that weaker assent cannot be certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا حصول التفاوت مطلقا ولكن لا نسلم أن ذلك التفاوت عائد إلى التصديق، بل هو عائد إلى التصور وبيانه : وهو أن التصديق يتوقف على تصور طرفي القضية، وتصور أن قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين : تصورات جلية غنية عن التعريف لأنه ليس فيها إلا تصور الواحد والاثنين والنصف، وهذه التصورات جلية غنية عن التعريف بخلاف قولنا : الممكن يفتقر إلى المؤثر ، فإن هذا التصديق يتوقف على تصور ماهية الممكن وعلى تصور ماهية الافتقار ، وعلى تصور ماهية المؤثر ، وهذه الأمور الثلاثة تصوراتها تصورات غامضة خفية ، فالتفاوت الحاصل في عقول الخلق من هذين الحكمين، إنما وقع بسبب حصول التفاوت بين تصوراتها ، فأما أن يقال : إن ذلك التفاوت وقع في نفس تلك التصديقات ، فهذا ممنوع . فما الدليل عليه ؟ سلمنا حصول التفاوت بين التصديقات . فلم قلتم : إنه لما كان أحد التصديقين أقوى من الثاني لزم كون التصديق المرجوح ظنيا ويمتنع كونه يقينياً ، وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ والجواب : - أما قوله : لا تسلم وقوع التفاوت بين حكمنا بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين وبين حكمنا بأن الممكن لا يترجع أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجع . فنقول : هذا التفاوت معلوم ببديهية العقل، فإنكاره إنكار للبديهي ، ولو جاز لكم أن تصروا على هذا الإنكار، على سبيل المكابرة ، جاز لغيركم أيضاً أن يقول : إني لا أجد من نفسي ، جزم العقل بأن المحدث ، لا بد له من محدث وبالجملة : فالأصل المعتبر في الفرق بين البديهيات وبين غيرها : ما يجده العقلاء من نفوسهم وعقولهم ، وجدانا ظاهرياً جلياً . فإن فتحتم باب الإنكار ، على سبيل المكابرة ، انفتحت تلك الأبواب في الكل . وحينئذ يفسد الكل .
We concede that there is a difference absolutely, but we do not concede that that difference goes back to the assent; rather it goes back to the conception. Its explanation: Assent depends on conceiving the two terms of the proposition. The conception of our statement “The one is half of two” is clear conceptions rich from definition because it only includes the conception of one, two, and half, and these conceptions are clear and rich from definition, unlike our statement “The contingent is in need of an efficient cause”; for this assent depends on conceiving the quiddity of the contingent, and on conceiving the quiddity of need, and on conceiving the quiddity of the efficient cause. These three matters, their conceptions are obscure and hidden. So the difference occurring in the minds of people between these two judgments only occurs due to the occurrence of a difference between their conceptions. As for its being said that that difference occurs in the assents themselves, this is prohibited. So what is the proof for it? We concede that there is a difference between assents. Then why do you say: Since one of the two assents is stronger than the second, it follows that the weaker assent is opinion and it is impossible for it to be certain? And what is the proof that it is so? The answer: As for his statement: “You do not concede the occurrence of a difference between our judgment that one is half of two and our judgment that the contingent does not prefer one of its two sides over the other except due to a referent,” we say: This difference is known by the intuition of the intellect; its denial is a denial of the intuitive. And if it were permissible for you to insist on this denial by way of obstinacy, it would be permissible for others also to say: I do not find in myself the intellect's certainty that the originated necessarily has an originator. In sum: The principle considered in the distinction between the intuitive propositions and others is what rational people find in themselves and their intellects, a manifest apparent finding. If you open the door of denial by way of obstinacy, those doors are opened in everything, and then everything is corrupted.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector concedes that there is a difference between two judgments (e.g., “one is half of two” and “the contingent needs a cause”) but denies that the difference lies in the assent itself, claiming it lies in the conception. The objector explains that assent to the first requires only simple, clear concepts, while assent to the second requires obscure concepts like quiddity of the contingent, need, and efficient cause; thus the difference is in the clarity of conception, not in the nature of assent. The objector then asks: If we grant a difference in assents, why does the weaker assent become mere opinion rather than certainty? The author responds by asserting that the difference between these two judgments is known by intellectual intuition (badahi), and denying it is a denial of the self-evident. If one obstinately denies this, then one could also deny any necessary truth, such as that an originated thing must have an originator, leading to total skepticism and ruin of all knowledge.

Key Terms
التصديق
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference in certainty between two judgments is due to the assent itself being stronger or weaker.
Correction: The objector argues it is due to the clarity of conceptions, not the nature of assent.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what is the real cause of the difference between the two judgments?
A) The assent itself is weaker in one case.
B) The conceptions (tasawwurat) are clearer in one case.
C) The efficient cause is unknown.
D) The intellect is incapable.

Key Takeaway
The objector establishes that the difference in certainty between judgments can be traced to the clarity of the underlying concepts, not to a difference in the assent faculty itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have certainty about Allah's existence, but has that certainty come from a clear conceptual grasp of the arguments, or from habit and upbringing? If the concepts of contingency and need were genuinely obscure to you, would your certainty waver? Take a moment to identify one concept in your aqeedah that feels vague—commit to clarifying it this week.
?
When you face a difficult theological question, does your certainty drop? Have you ever mistaken a lack of conceptual clarity for weakness of faith? This lesson teaches that certainty can vary simply because some ideas are harder to grasp. What is the most obscure concept you have about Allah's attributes, and what concrete step will you take to make it clearer in your mind?
?
The objector forces you to ask: is your certainty about fundamental beliefs really based on the strength of your assent, or on how well you understand the terms? If your 'certainty' is actually just familiarity with clear concepts, then what happens when you encounter deeper mysteries of the faith that are inherently obscure? Are you willing to hold firm even when the concepts are difficult, or will you retreat to comfortable generalizations? Decide today to seek deeper understanding of one challenging doctrine.

The Reply: Certainty Difference is Intuitive and Denial Leads to Skepticism
Author responds that certainty difference is intuitive and obstinate denial destroys all knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response that the difference between intuitive and non-intuitive judgments is known by intellectual intuition, and that obstinate denial leads to total skepticism.

Core Idea
The difference in certainty is self-evident; denying it opens the door to rejecting all necessary truths.

In response to the objection, the author argues that the difference between judgments like 'one is half of two' and 'the contingent needs a cause' is known by the intuition of the intellect. To deny it is to engage in obstinate denial (mukabara), which would allow one to reject even the most basic necessary truths, leading to the corruption of all knowledge. This lesson examines that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the author's response, noting how he addresses the objector's claim.
2
Identify the core assertion
The author asserts that the difference between the two judgments is known by badihi (intuition). Find where he says this.
Look for 'معلوم ببديهية العقل'.
3
Understand the warning about obstinacy
The author warns that denying this intuitive knowledge is obstinate (mukabara) and would allow one to deny even the necessity of a cause for originated things, leading to total skepticism.
4
Grasp the general principle
The author concludes by stating the criterion for intuitive propositions: what rational people find in themselves, a manifest finding. Denial by obstinacy would corrupt all knowledge.
This principle upholds the reliability of intuitive knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا حصول التفاوت مطلقا ولكن لا نسلم أن ذلك التفاوت عائد إلى التصديق، بل هو عائد إلى التصور وبيانه : وهو أن التصديق يتوقف على تصور طرفي القضية، وتصور أن قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين : تصورات جلية غنية عن التعريف لأنه ليس فيها إلا تصور الواحد والاثنين والنصف، وهذه التصورات جلية غنية عن التعريف بخلاف قولنا : الممكن يفتقر إلى المؤثر ، فإن هذا التصديق يتوقف على تصور ماهية الممكن وعلى تصور ماهية الافتقار ، وعلى تصور ماهية المؤثر ، وهذه الأمور الثلاثة تصوراتها تصورات غامضة خفية ، فالتفاوت الحاصل في عقول الخلق من هذين الحكمين، إنما وقع بسبب حصول التفاوت بين تصوراتها ، فأما أن يقال : إن ذلك التفاوت وقع في نفس تلك التصديقات ، فهذا ممنوع . فما الدليل عليه ؟ سلمنا حصول التفاوت بين التصديقات . فلم قلتم : إنه لما كان أحد التصديقين أقوى من الثاني لزم كون التصديق المرجوح ظنيا ويمتنع كونه يقينياً ، وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ والجواب : - أما قوله : لا تسلم وقوع التفاوت بين حكمنا بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين وبين حكمنا بأن الممكن لا يترجع أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجع . فنقول : هذا التفاوت معلوم ببديهية العقل، فإنكاره إنكار للبديهي ، ولو جاز لكم أن تصروا على هذا الإنكار، على سبيل المكابرة ، جاز لغيركم أيضاً أن يقول : إني لا أجد من نفسي ، جزم العقل بأن المحدث ، لا بد له من محدث وبالجملة : فالأصل المعتبر في الفرق بين البديهيات وبين غيرها : ما يجده العقلاء من نفوسهم وعقولهم ، وجدانا ظاهرياً جلياً . فإن فتحتم باب الإنكار ، على سبيل المكابرة ، انفتحت تلك الأبواب في الكل . وحينئذ يفسد الكل .
We concede that there is a difference absolutely, but we do not concede that that difference goes back to the assent; rather it goes back to the conception. Its explanation: Assent depends on conceiving the two terms of the proposition. The conception of our statement “The one is half of two” is clear conceptions rich from definition because it only includes the conception of one, two, and half, and these conceptions are clear and rich from definition, unlike our statement “The contingent is in need of an efficient cause”; for this assent depends on conceiving the quiddity of the contingent, and on conceiving the quiddity of need, and on conceiving the quiddity of the efficient cause. These three matters, their conceptions are obscure and hidden. So the difference occurring in the minds of people between these two judgments only occurs due to the occurrence of a difference between their conceptions. As for its being said that that difference occurs in the assents themselves, this is prohibited. So what is the proof for it? We concede that there is a difference between assents. And if it were permissible for you to insist on this denial by way of obstinacy, it would be permissible for others also to say: I do not find in myself the intellect's certainty that the originated necessarily has an originator. In sum: The principle considered in the distinction between the intuitive propositions and others is what rational people find in themselves and their intellects, a manifest apparent finding. If you open the door of denial by way of obstinacy, those doors are opened in everything, and then everything is corrupted.

This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Badihi Proposition and the Argument from One Counterexample
Response to objection with definition and modus tollens argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single counterexample disproves a proposition's intuitive status using modus tollens, and why many positive instances do not prove intuitiveness.

Core Idea
One counterexample is enough; many confirmations prove nothing.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: even if judgments differ in strength at times, they may be equal at other times. The response is sharp: a single case where the predicated certainty fails is sufficient to refute the claim that the proposition is intuitive (badihi). The author defines an intuitive proposition and then uses a conditional argument to show that one counterexample, via modus tollens, disproves the claim, while countless positive instances do not help because affirming the consequent is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and response
Read the opening: the objector says equality in strength occurs often. The author replies that a single difference suffices for refutation. Notice the shift: the objector focuses on quantity, the author on logical necessity.
The response is not empirical but logical.
2
Define the badihi proposition
Find the definition: an intuitive proposition is one where mere conception of subject and predicate yields certainty. This is the standard definition used in Islamic logic.
The definition is key to the argument.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The author sets up a conditional: if the proposition were intuitive, then conceiving subject and predicate always yields certainty. He then points to a case where conception occurs but certainty does not. This is modus tollens: denying the consequent negates the antecedent.
The argument uses 'istithna' nafid al-tali' (exclusion of the negation of the consequent).
4
Contrast with many positive instances
The author notes that even if certainty occurred a million times, it proves nothing because 'istithna' 'ayn al-tali' (affirming the consequent) is logically invalid.
This highlights the asymmetry between proof and disproof.
5
Reflect on the logical principle
Ask yourself: why is a single counterexample logically decisive? Because the claim is universal. One exception falsifies it. This is a foundational principle in both logic and theology.
This principle appears often in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثاني: وهو أن العقل، وإن حكم بحصول التفاوت في بعض الأوقات، فقد يحكم بينها بالاستواء في سائر الأوقات. فنقول: إن وقوع التفاوت في بعض الصور يكفي في القدح، وأما حصول المساواة في الصور الكثيرة فإنه لا يفيد الصحة. وبيانه: وهو أن القضية البديهية هي التي يكون مجرد تصور موضوعها، ومحمولها كافيا في جزم الذهن بنسبة أحدهما إلى الآخر، إما بالنفي أو الإثبات. فنقول: لو كانت هذه القضية بديهية لكان تصور موضوعها ومحمولها كافيا في إيقاع ذلك التصديق، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لامتنع خلو حصول هذين التصورين عن حصول ذلك الجزم في التصديق، وحيث خلا هذان التصوران عن الجزم الحاصل في التصديق ولو في صورة واحدة، كفي ذلك في العلم بأن هذين التصورين لا يوجبان ذلك التصديق، فإن استثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم، أما لو حصل هذا الجزم في ألف ألف صورة، فإنه لا يفيد البتة شيئا لأن استثناء عين التالي لا ينتج شيئا البتة.
As for the answer to the second question—which is that the intellect, although it judges the occurrence of a difference in some times, may judge between them equality in the rest of the times—we say: The occurrence of a difference in some cases suffices for refutation; as for the occurrence of equality in many cases, it does not benefit correctness. Its explanation: The intuitive proposition is that whose mere conception of its subject and predicate is sufficient for the mind's certainty of the relation of one to the other, either by negation or affirmation. We say: If this proposition were intuitive, then conceiving its subject and predicate would be sufficient for producing that assent; and if it were so, it would be impossible for these two conceptions to be devoid of that certainty arising in the assent. And since these two conceptions have been devoid of the certainty arising in the assent even in one case, that suffices for knowing that these two conceptions do not necessitate that assent. For the exclusion of the negation of the consequent yields the negation of the antecedent; but if this certainty occurred in a thousand thousand cases, it would not benefit anything at all, because the exclusion of the identical consequent does not produce anything at all.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second objection. The objector says that even if there is a difference in strength at some times, at other times the two judgments are equal in strength. The author responds that a single case of difference is enough to refute the claim that the proposition is equally certain in all cases; many cases of equality do not prove correctness. He then defines an intuitive (badihi) proposition: one where merely conceiving the subject and predicate is sufficient to produce certainty in the mind about the relation between them. The author then provides a logical argument: If this proposition were intuitive, then conceiving its subject and predicate would always produce assent. But we observe cases where these conceptions occur yet certainty does not follow. This single case disproves the conditional using modus tollens (exclusion of the negation of the consequent yields negation of the antecedent). In contrast, even if certainty occurred in countless cases, that would not prove anything because affirming the consequent is invalid—it does not entail the antecedent.

Key Terms
القضية البديهية
استثناء نقيض التالي
استثناء عين التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If many people assent to a proposition with certainty in many cases, it must be intuitive.
Correction: No, because a single counterexample—a case where conception occurs without certainty—disproves intuitiveness, regardless of many positive instances.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what suffices to disprove that a proposition is intuitive (badihi)?
A) The majority of people do not assent to it.
B) There exists even one case where conceiving subject and predicate does not produce certainty.
C) The proposition is not found in the Qur'an.
D) The proposition is complex.

Key Takeaway
A single counterexample disproves a universal claim like intuitiveness, but no number of confirmations can prove it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold with certainty—perhaps about your own abilities, a person you trust, or a religious conviction. Can you recall even one instance that contradicts that certainty? How do you respond to that counterexample? Do you dismiss it, explain it away, or allow it to shake your certainty? What would it take for you to admit that your belief might not be as certain as you thought?
?
You have likely affirmed many things in your life based on repeated positive experiences—like trusting someone because they've been reliable many times. But what if a single betrayal occurred? Would you still trust them? Are you treating your religious convictions the same way—relying on many confirmations while ignoring a counterexample that challenges their foundation? Examine your heart: are you afraid to face that counterexample because it might unravel a comfortable certainty?
?
The author says that even a thousand thousand confirmations prove nothing if a single counterexample exists. Is there an area of your life where you've been accumulating evidence for something but avoiding the one fact that contradicts it? What is that fact? And are you willing to follow the logic of modus tollens—allowing that one truth to negate your antecedent assumption—even if it means losing a cherished belief?

Disproving Self-Evidence with One Counterexample
Response to objection, defining self-evident proposition with logical criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single counterexample disproves the self-evidence of a proposition, and the logical structure of the argument using denial of the consequent.

Core Idea
One counterexample is enough to disprove self-evidence; many confirmations prove nothing.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the intellect sometimes judges equality between cases. He argues that a single case of difference is sufficient to refute a claim of self-evidence, no matter how many cases appear to confirm it. He then defines a self-evident proposition and uses a conditional argument to show why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
The passage uses technical terms like 'قضية بديهية' (self-evident proposition) and 'نقيض التالي' (negation of consequent).
2
Identify the definition of a self-evident proposition
Locate the sentence defining 'badīhiyya'. Note that it requires mere conceptualization of subject and predicate to produce certainty.
This definition is crucial for the logical argument that follows.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The author argues: if the proposition were self-evident, then conceptualization would always produce certainty. Since there is a case where conceptualization occurs without certainty, the proposition is not self-evident.
This is a modus tollens argument: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
4
Understand the logical principle
The author explicitly states that negating the consequent (the absence of certainty) yields the negation of the antecedent (the proposition is not self-evident). Affirming the consequent (many cases of certainty) proves nothing.
This principle is key: a million confirmations do not outweigh one counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثاني : وهو أن العقل ، وإن حكم بحصول التفاوت في بعض الأوقات ، فقد يحكم بينها بالاستواء في سائر الأوقات . فنقول : إن وقوع التفاوت في بعض الصور يكفي في القدح ، وأما حصول المساواة في الصور الكثيرة فإنه لا يفيد الصحة. وبيانه : وهو أن القضية البديهية هي التي يكون مجرد تصور موضوعها ، ومحمولها كافياً في جزم الذهن بنسبة أحدهما إلى الآخر ، إما بالنفي أو الإثبات . فنقول : لو كانت هذه القضية بديهية لكان تصور موضوعها ومحمولها كافياً في إيقاع ذلك التصديق ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لامتنع خلو حصول هذين التصورين عن حصول ذلك الجزم في التصديق، وحيث خلا هذان التصوران عن الجزم الحاصل في التصديق ولو في صورة واحدة ، كفي ذلك في العلم بأن هذين التصورين لا يوجبان ذلك التصديق ، فإن استثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم ، أما لو حصل هذا الجزم في ألف ألف صورة ، فإنه لا يفيد البتة شيئاً لأن استثناء عين التالي لا ينتج شيئاً البتة .
As for the answer to the second question—that the intellect, although it judges that difference occurs at some times, may judge equality at other times—we say: The occurrence of difference in some cases is sufficient for objection; as for equality occurring in many cases, it does not benefit correctness. Its clarification: A self-evident (badīhiyya) proposition is that for which the mere conceptualization (taṣawwur) of its subject and predicate is sufficient for the mind's certainty (jazm) in the relation of one to the other, either negatively or affirmatively. We say: If this proposition were self-evident, then conceptualizing its subject and predicate would be sufficient to produce that assent (taṣdīq). If that were the case, it would be impossible for the two conceptualizations to occur without that certainty in assent. Since the two conceptualizations occur without the certainty in assent in even one case, that is sufficient for the knowledge that these two conceptualizations do not necessitate that assent; for negating the consequent (naqīḍ al-tālī) yields the negation of the antecedent (naqīḍ al-muqaddam). Even if this certainty occurs in a million cases, it does not benefit anything at all, because affirming the consequent (naqīḍ al-tālī?) does not yield anything at all.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a response to the second objection against the proof that the possible requires a determinant. The objector argued that the intellect sometimes judges equality between cases, not always difference. The author answers that a single case of difference suffices to refute the claim that the premise is self-evident; many cases of equality do not prove it correct. He then defines a self-evident proposition (qadiyya badīhiyya): one whose mere conceptualization (taṣawwur) of its subject and predicate compels the mind's certainty (jazm) of their relation, whether affirmative or negative. Using a conditional argument (sharṭiyya): if the proposition were self-evident, the two concepts would always produce that certainty. Since they do not in even one case (i.e., the objector's case of equality), the proposition is not self-evident. He explains the logic: denying the consequent (the absence of certainty from the concepts) entails denying the antecedent (that the proposition is self-evident). He notes that even if certainty occurs in many cases, that does not prove it; affirming the consequent in a conditional proves nothing.

Key Terms
قضية بديهية
تصور
تصديق
جزم
نقيض التالي
نقيض المقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many instances of certainty (affirming the consequent) prove a proposition is self-evident.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that even a million cases of certainty do not prove anything; only a single counterexample suffices to disprove self-evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of a self-evident (badīhiyya) proposition?
A) A proposition that is known through reasoning.
B) A proposition whose mere conceptualization of subject and predicate suffices for the mind's certainty of their relation.
C) A proposition that is accepted by many people.
D) A proposition that cannot be doubted.

Key Takeaway
A single counterexample suffices to disprove a claim of self-evidence, regardless of how many confirming cases exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold that you consider 'self-evident'—perhaps about yourself, others, or your deen—that you have never genuinely tested? What would happen if you encountered just one clear counterexample? Would you abandon the belief, or would you rationalize it away? This lesson teaches that one exception is enough to disprove self-evidence. Are you brave enough to look for that exception?
?
The author shows that repeated confirmation (even a thousand cases) proves nothing. In your life, how many times have you felt certain about something simply because it 'always happens'? Are you ignoring a rare but real contradiction that could shatter that false certainty? What is that contradiction, and will you face it now?
?
This passage distinguishes between logical necessity (self-evidence) and widespread agreement. Think of an Islamic truth you hold—like the obligation of prayer or the existence of Allah. Is your certainty based on rational proof that withstands a single counterexample, or on accumulated repetition? If someone sincerely claimed they saw no certainty from the same premises, would you be shaken or confident? This lesson demands you examine the foundation of your convictions. What will you do with the uncomfortable doubts this question raises?

Criterion for Probable vs Certain Judgment
Response to fourth objection establishing criterion for probable vs certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a weaker judgment is classified as probable rather than certain, based on the criterion of preventing the contradictory.

Core Idea
Certainty requires absolute prevention of the contrary; any possibility of the opposite makes it only probable.

This lesson unpacks the fourth objection: why should a weaker judgment be probable and not certain? The author lays out a clear criterion: if the intellect absolutely prevents the contradictory, certainty is achieved; if not, even a remote possibility of the opposite makes the judgment only probable. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating theological arguments that rely on probability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two scenarios the author presents.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: one where the intellect absolutely prevents the contradictory, and another where it does not. Find where each is discussed.
The key Arabic terms are منع كلي (absolute prevention) and احتمال النقيض (possibility of the contradictory).
3
Understand the implication
If absolute prevention is present, certainty results. If not, even a remote possibility makes the judgment probable. Ask yourself: why does any possibility of the opposite negate certainty?
This clarifies that probability is not merely low certainty but a different epistemic category.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Reflect on how this criterion affects the earlier proof that the possible needs a determinant. The proof yields only probability, not certainty, because the possibility of a different cause remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : و ما الدليل على أن أحد الجزئين ، لما كان أقوى من الثاني كان المرجوح ظنياً لا يقينياً ؟ . فنقول : الدليل على أنه يجب كون القضية المرجوحة ظنية لا يقينية : هو أن جزم العقل بهذه القضية المرجوحة ، إما أن يكون مع المنع من نقيضها منعاً كلياً ، وإما أن يقال : المنع من النقيض على سبيل الجزم غير حاصل ، فإن كان المنع الجازم من النقيض حاصلا امتنع وقوع التفاوت . وإن كان المنع الجازم من النقيض غير حاصل بل كان احتمال النقيض حاصلاً من بعض الوجوه سواء كان ذلك الاحتمال قريباً أو بعيداً ، كان ذلك التصديق ظناً غالباً ، فإن احتمال النقيض يوجب هذا المعنى ، وحينئذ لا يكون ذلك الجزم علماً ويقيناً ، بل يكون ظناً . فهذا جملة الكلام في الجواب عن السؤالات التي أوردوها على هذا الدليل .
As for the answer to the fourth question—which is his statement: 'What is the proof that when one of the two parts is stronger than the other, the weaker (marjūḥ) is probable (ẓannī) and not certain (yaqīnī)?' We say: The proof that the weaker proposition must be probable and not certain is that the intellect's certainty (jazm) regarding this weaker proposition is either accompanied by an absolute prevention (manʿ kullī) of its contradictory (naqīḍ), or it is said that preventive certainty of the contradictory is not obtained. If the preventive certainty of the contradictory is obtained, then difference would be impossible. If the preventive certainty of the contradictory is not obtained, rather the possibility (iḥtimāl) of the contradictory is obtained in some respect—whether that possibility is near or far—then that assent (taṣdīq) is a preponderant probability (ẓann ghālib). For the possibility of the contradictory necessitates this meaning. In that case, that certainty is not knowledge and certainty (ʿilm wa yaqīn), but rather probability (ẓann). This is the summary of the answer to the objections raised against this proof.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the fourth objection, which asks: why should a weaker judgment be probable rather than certain? The author explains the criterion. The intellect's certainty about a weaker proposition can be of two types: either it absolutely prevents the contradictory (naqīḍ), meaning it rules out its opposite completely—in which case there is no difference in strength, and it would be certain. Or it does not absolutely prevent the contradictory, meaning some possibility of the opposite remains, even if remote. In that case, the assent is a strong probability (ẓann ghālib) but not certain knowledge (yaqīn). Thus, the weaker proposition is not certain because its negation is not absolutely impossible. This criterion clarifies why the earlier argument about the possible needing a determinant yields only probability, not certainty.

جزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A high probability is the same as certainty.
Correction: The passage clarifies that any possibility of the contradictory, even remote, precludes certainty. Certainty requires absolute prevention of the opposite, not just high likelihood.

Checkpoint
What makes a judgment certain (yaqīnī) according to the passage?
A) It is widespread among scholars.
B) The intellect absolutely prevents its contradictory from being possible.
C) It is supported by many proofs.
D) It has no counterexamples.

Key Takeaway
For a judgment to be certain, the intellect must absolutely prevent the contradictory; otherwise, it is merely probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold as absolutely certain. Have you completely prevented its opposite? If not, will you admit it is merely probable?
?
The passage says that even a remote possibility of the opposite prevents certainty. What if your most deeply held convictions leave room for any doubt, however tiny? Are you willing to reclassify them as probable?
?
Do you ever call something 'certain' simply because it seems overwhelmingly likely? How does this passage confront your intellectual honesty today? What will you change in how you speak about your beliefs?

Why the Weaker Judgment is Only Probable
Razi answers the objection that the weaker judgment must be certain, proving it is only probable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a weaker rational judgment is only probable (ظن) and not certain (يقين), based on the necessity of preventing its opposite with certainty.

Core Idea
A judgment is certain only when its opposite is impossible; otherwise, it remains probable.

This passage addresses a key objection: if one judgment is stronger than another, why is the weaker only probable? Razi provides a clear logical criterion: certainty requires total prevention of the opposite. Since the weaker judgment lacks that, it remains probable. Let's work through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the objection
The objector asks: what is the proof that the weaker judgment is probable, not certain? Note how the question is framed.
3
Understand the two possibilities
The answer presents two cases: either the mind's certainty excludes the opposite totally, or it does not. Consider what each entails.
If the opposite is totally excluded, both judgments would be equally certain – no difference.
4
Trace the reasoning to probability
If the opposite is not totally excluded, possibility remains, and the assent becomes probable, not certain. Note the role of 'possibility of the opposite'.
5
Conclude the answer
Read the final sentence summarising the answer. This completes the response to all objections against the proof.
The passage ends by tying this answer to the earlier objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : وما الدليل على أن أحد الجزئين ، لما كان أقوى من الثاني كان المرجوح ظنياً لا يقينياً ؟ فنقول : الدليل على أنه يجب كون القضية المرجوحة ظنية لا يقينية : هو أن جزم العقل بهذه القضية المرجوحة ، إما أن يكون مع المنع من نقيضها منعاً كلياً ، وإما أن يقال : المنع من النقيض على سبيل الجزم غير حاصل ، فإن كان المنع الجازم من النقيض حاصلا امتنع وقوع التفاوت . وإن كان المنع الجازم من النقيض غير حاصل بل كان احتمال النقيض حاصلا من بعض الوجوه سواء كان ذلك الاحتمال قريباً أو بعيداً ، كان ذلك التصديق ظناً غالباً ، فإن احتمال النقيض يوجب هذا المعنى ، وحينئذ لا يكون ذلك الجزم علماً ويقيناً ، بل يكون ظناً . فهذا جملة الكلام في الجواب عن السؤالات التي أوردوها على هذا الدليل.
As for the answer to the fourth question, which is his statement: 'What is the proof that when one of the two parts is stronger than the other, the weaker is probable and not certain?' We say: The proof that it is necessary that the weaker proposition be probable and not certain is that the mind's certainty regarding this weaker proposition is either with a total prevention of its opposite, or it is said that the prevention of the opposite is not obtained with certainty. If the certain prevention of the opposite is obtained, then difference becomes impossible. And if the certain prevention of the opposite is not obtained, but rather the possibility of the opposite is obtained from some aspects, whether that possibility is near or far, then that assent is a predominant probability, for the possibility of the opposite necessitates this meaning, and then that certainty is not knowledge and certainty, but rather probability. So this is the summary of the answer to the questions that they raised against this proof.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the fourth objection. The objector asked: what proof is there that when one of two judgments is stronger than the other, the weaker is merely probable (ظن) and not certain (يقين)? The author replies that the certainty of the mind about the weaker judgment is either with a total prevention of its opposite, in which case there would be no difference in strength between the two judgments (i.e., both would be certain), or the prevention of the opposite is not certain, so there remains some possibility of the opposite, and that makes the assent probable rather than certain. Thus the weaker judgment is not certain knowledge but only probable. This completes the answer to the objections raised against this proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any stronger judgment is automatically certain.
Correction: The author clarifies that only when there is no possibility of the opposite does certainty obtain; otherwise, even a strong judgment is only probable.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what makes a weaker judgment merely probable rather than certain?
A) The judgment is not strong enough
B) The prevention of the opposite is not certain and possibility of the opposite remains
C) The two judgments are of equal strength
D) The mind cannot decide between them

Key Takeaway
A judgment is certain only if its opposite is absolutely impossible; otherwise, it remains probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider one of your most firmly held beliefs about God. Now ask yourself: have you truly prevented its opposite with total certainty, or is there some remote possibility? If there is any possibility, then by Razi's reasoning, your belief is only probable. Does this disturb you? How would you seek to eliminate that possibility?
?
Razi argues that if the opposite is prevented absolutely, then difference in strength is impossible. In your own reasoning, have you ever assumed that a stronger argument gives certainty? Reflect on a conviction you once thought certain but later realized there was a possibility of error. What did that teach you about the nature of certainty?
?
You are about to make a decision based on a probable judgment. According to this passage, even if the probability is overwhelming, you do not have certainty. Does that change how seriously you take that decision? What can you do to act with confidence despite probability?

Why the Premise is Not Self-Evident
Razi argues that the premise of needing a preponderator is not self-evident because many rational people deny it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the need of the contingent for a preponderator is not a self-evident premise based on the fact that many rational people deny it.

Core Idea
If many rational people deny a claim, it cannot be self-evident.

Razi presents his second argument, which challenges the assumption that the principle of needing a preponderator is self-evident. He points to the historical fact that many rational thinkers have rejected this principle, arguing that a truly self-evident truth would be impossible to deny. This lesson examines his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms like بديهية (self-evident) and the structure of the argument.
The passage is a concise proof.
2
Identify the premise in question
The premise is that contingent beings need a preponderator. Razi argues this premise is not self-evident.
Self-evident means known without proof.
3
Understand Razi's argument
Razi uses the fact that many rational people have denied this premise. If it were self-evident, such denial would be impossible. Since denial occurs, the premise is not self-evident.
This is an argument from consensus of rational people.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what makes a proposition self-evident. Razi's argument shows that even widely held beliefs may not be self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : في بيان أن افتقار الممكن إلى المرجح ليست مقدمة بديهية ، إنا قد ذكرنا أن جماعة عظيمة من العقلاء ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يجوز رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، ولو كانت هذه القضية بديهية لامتنع إطباق الطوائف العظيمة على إنكارها .
The second proof: In demonstrating that the need of the contingent for a preponderating factor is not a self-evident premise. We have mentioned that a great group of rational people held that it is not permissible for one of the two sides of the contingent to be preponderated over the other except due to a preponderating factor. If this proposition were self-evident, it would be impossible for great groups to agree upon denying it.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof by consensus. The author argues that the proposition that contingent beings need a preponderating factor cannot be self-evident (بديهية) because many rational people have denied it. If it were truly self-evident, it would be impossible for large groups of rational individuals to uniformly reject it. Since they do reject it, the premise is not self-evident but rather requires proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident truths are universally accepted by all rational people.
Correction: Razi shows that denial by large groups of rational people indicates a proposition is not self-evident; self-evident truth would be impossible to deny.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a complex idea
B) Because many rational people have denied it
C) Because it was never stated in the Quran
D) Because it leads to contradictions

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the premise that contingent beings need a preponderator is not self-evident because many rational people have denied it, which would be impossible for a self-evident truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Reflect on a time when you dismissed a view because it was held by a minority. How does Razi's method of using the denial by rational people to question self-evidence force you to reconsider the way you evaluate truth?

Third Argument: Persisting Contingent Needs Cause
Razi argues by implication that the premise is not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the impossibility of 'تحصيل الحاصل' to argue that the premise 'every contingent needs a cause' is not self-evident.

Core Idea
Denial of a specific implication shows the general principle is not self-evident.

Razi presents a third argument against those who claim that the need of a contingent for a cause is self-evident. He shows that if it were self-evident, then a particular case—the need of a persisting contingent—would also be self-evident. Yet most rational thinkers reject that, viewing it as impossible (تحصيل الحاصل). This demonstrates that the original premise is not self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the logical flow of Razi's argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'If... then... but not...'.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Notice that Razi sets up a conditional: if the premise were self-evident, then the specific case of the persisting contingent would also be self-evident. Identify the antecedent and consequent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum strategy.
3
Understand 'تحصيل الحاصل'
The term 'تحصيل الحاصل' means 'achieving what is already obtained'. Most rational people consider this impossible in self-evident reasoning. Understand why this is used to reject the consequent.
This concept is key to the argument against the need for a cause during persistence.
4
Trace the conclusion
Since the consequent is rejected (the specific case is not self-evident), the antecedent (the general premise) cannot be self-evident. This is the conclusion Razi draws.
Razi concludes that knowledge of the need of the contingent for an effect is not a self-evident judgment.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some think that if a principle applies to all contingents, it must apply to a persisting one. Razi uses the denial of the specific case to disprove the universality of the principle's self-evidence.
This argument does not deny that contingents need a cause, only that the statement is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت هذه المقدمة بديهية ، لكان العلم بافتقار الممكن الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر علماً بديهياً لأن الإمكان حاصل فيه ، لكن الأمر ليس كذلك ، فإن أكثر العقلاء يقولون ببطلانه ، ويقولون : إن تحصيل الحاصل محال في بديهة العقول، فعلمنا : أن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر ليس حكماً بديهياً .
The third proof: If this premise were self-evident, then knowledge of the need of the persisting contingent during its persistence for an effect would be self-evident knowledge, because possibility (إمكان) is present in it. But the matter is not so, for most rational people say it is false, and say that achieving what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل) is impossible in the self-evident judgment of minds. So we know that knowledge of the need of the contingent for an effect is not a self-evident judgment.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is the third argument against the self-evidence of the premise. The author argues by implication: if the original premise (that every contingent needs a cause) were self-evident, then a specific case—the persisting contingent (الممكن الباقي) needing a cause to continue existing—would also be self-evident. But most rational people reject this, claiming that it would entail 'تحصيل الحاصل' (renewing what already exists), which they consider impossible in the self-evident judgment of minds. Therefore, the original premise is not self-evident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a principle applies to all contingents, it must apply to a persisting one, so the premise is self-evident.
Correction: Razi uses the denial of the specific case (the persisting contingent needing a cause) to disprove the universality of the principle's self-evidence. The argument shows that the premise is not self-evident, not that it is false.

Checkpoint

B) Then the need of a persisting contingent for a cause would also be self-evident
C) Then all rational people would accept it
D) Then there would be no free will

Key Takeaway
Razi's third argument shows that the premise 'every contingent needs a cause' is not self-evident because its application to persisting contingents is rejected as impossible (تحصيل الحاصل) by rational thinkers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you truly believe that you need Allah's continuous creation at every moment, or do you subconsciously assume that once created, you are self-sufficient? How does Razi's argument force you to reexamine that assumption?
?
In your daily life, do you act as if the laws of nature are self-sufficient, or do you recognize that they are contingent on Allah's will at every instant? What would change if you truly internalized this lesson?
?
Razi shows that a commonly accepted principle is not self-evident. What other deeply held beliefs do you hold that you have never critically examined? Are you willing to test them, even if it challenges your comfort?

Answer to the First Objection: First Way (Partial)
Razi presents the first way of answering the first objection, arguing that possibility is the source of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first way of answering the objection that only originated things need a cause, and how he argues that possibility is the true source of need.

Core Idea
Possibility, not origination, is the real basis for needing a cause.

The opponent argues that only originated things are known to need a cause, not all possible things. Razi counters by first establishing that the need of the originated is self-evident, then deducing that possibility itself must be the cause of need. This lesson walks through the thought experiment and the elimination of origination as the cause, though the argument is incomplete here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection, the two ways, and the beginning of the first way.
The text ends abruptly; the full argument continues on the next page.
2
Identify the thought experiment
Razi asks you to imagine an eternal being that is necessary in itself. Ask yourself: can you conceive of such a being needing a cause? That shows the source of need must be either origination or possibility.
3
Understand why origination is eliminated
Razi argues that origination cannot be the cause of need because it is a temporal attribute that itself comes to be. The passage ends before completing the proof, but the direction is clear.
The logical elimination of origination leaves possibility as the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تمام الكلام في طرق السؤال والاعتراض. والجواب: قوله في السؤال الأول: إن الشبهات التي ذكرتموها، إنما تدل على أن العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر علم ضروري، ولا تدل على أن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر ضروري. فنقول: لنا في الجواب عنه طريقان: الطريق الأول: أن نقول: إنه لما ثبت بالوجوه المذكورة أن العلم باحتياج المحدث إلى المؤثر أمر ضروري بديهي، فنقول: إنه يلزم منه كون الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر، والدليل عليه: أنا إذا فرضنا الشيء قديماً أزلياً ومع كونه كذلك، فإذا فرضناه واجب الوجود لذاته، فههنا إذا حصل اعتقاد كونه أزلياً وحصل أيضاً اعتقاد كونه واجب الوجود لذاته، فمع حصول هذين الاعتقادين، يستحيل منا أن نعتقد فيه كونه محتاجاً في وجوده إلى مرجح ومؤثر، وهذا يدل على أن منشأ الحاجة إما الحدوث وإما الإمكان، لأنا عند فرض زوال هذين المفهومين لما امتنع الحكم علينا بالحاجة، ثبت أن المقتضي للحاجة إما مجموع هذين القيدين أو أحدهما، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الحدوث يمتنع أن يكون علة للحاجة، أو أن يكون جزء العلة، أو أن يكون شرط العلة، وإذا سقط الحدوث عن درجة الاعتبار، بقي
So this is the complete discussion of the ways of questioning and objection. And the answer: His statement in the first question: 'The doubts you have mentioned only indicate that knowledge of the need of the originated for an effect is necessary knowledge, and do not indicate that knowledge of the need of the contingent for an effect is necessary.' We say: We have two ways in answering it: The first way: We say that when it has been established by the mentioned methods that knowledge of the need of the originated for an effect is necessary and self-evident, we say: It follows from that that possibility (الإمكان) is a cause of need for an effect. The proof of that is: If we assume a thing to be eternal and ancient, and with that we assume it to be necessary of existence in itself, then when there is belief that it is eternal and also belief that it is necessary of existence in itself, with the presence of these two beliefs, it is impossible for us to believe that it is in need in its existence of a preponderating factor and an effect. This indicates that the source of need is either origination (الحدوث) or possibility (الإمكان). Because when we assume the removal of these two concepts, it is not impossible for us to judge need, so it is established that the necessitator of need is either the combination of these two conditions or one of them. And when this is established, we say: Origination cannot be the cause of need, or a part of the cause, or a condition of the cause. And when origination falls from the degree of consideration, there remains
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the answer to the first objection. The opponent argued that the proofs only show that originated things (حادث) need a cause, not that all contingent things (ممكن) need a cause. The author states two ways of answering. Here he gives the first way: Since it is established that the need of the originated for an effect is self-evident, it follows that possibility itself is the cause of need. The proof is a thought experiment: if we consider an eternal being that is necessary in itself, we cannot conceive of it needing a cause. So the source of need is either origination or possibility. But origination cannot be the cause, because it is a temporal attribute that comes to be, and thus it cannot be the cause of a perpetual need. So possibility remains as the cause. However, the argument is cut off at 'بقي', indicating the continuation is on the next page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only originated things need a cause; contingent things that are not originated do not need a cause.
Correction: The author shows that the need for a cause stems from possibility (imkan), not from temporal origination. Hence even non-originated possible things would need a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the first way of answering the objection, what does the author argue is the source of need for a cause?
A) Origination (الحدوث)
B) Possibility (الإمكان)
C) Both origination and possibility
D) The preponderating factor itself

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that even if we only knew originated things need a cause, the same reasoning shows that possibility itself necessitates a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are not eternal, nor are you necessary in yourself. What does the argument of this passage say about your constant, ongoing dependence on a Creator—not just for your beginning, but for every moment of your existence? How does that sit with your everyday feeling of self-sufficiency?
?
Razi shows that possibility itself is the source of need. When you look at your own life, do you tend to think that God is only relevant for the 'big moments' (birth, death) or for the times you feel weak? How does this lesson force you to acknowledge that your very nature as contingent means you need God now, in this instant, for your very existence?
?
The proof assumes that if a thing were eternal and necessary, we would not conceive it needing a cause. What would it take for you to truly internalize that you are not that thing—that you are contingent through and through? What would change in your daily relationship with your Creator if you lived in the awareness that your need for Him is not just about your past origin but about your present possibility?

Third Argument: Need for Cause Not Self-Evident
Presents the third argument that the need for a cause is not intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need of a possible being for a cause is not a self-evident judgment according to this argument.

Core Idea
The need for a cause is not intuitive because even rational people disagree.

This lesson examines the third argument presented in Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which challenges the assumption that the need for a cause is self-evident. The author uses a conditional argument and points to widespread disagreement among rational people to prove his point. Understanding this argument sharpens our grasp of what counts as intuitive in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures a conditional argument.
The Arabic wording is concise; pay attention to 'لو' (if) and 'لكن' (but).
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the 'if...then' structure. The author says: if the premise were intuitive, then X would be intuitive. But X is not accepted by most rational people.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Understand the objection from 'tahsil al-hasil'
The denial by rational people is based on the principle that 'attainment of the attained is impossible.' Grasp why this challenges the intuitive nature of the need for a cause.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the original premise is not intuition. Consider the implications for the debate on causality.
This completes the section on objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت هذه المقدمة بديهية ، لكان العلم بافتقار الممكن الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر علماً بديهياً لأن الإمكان حاصل فيه ، لكن الأمر ليس كذلك ، فإن أكثر العقلاء يقولون ببطلانه ، ويقولون : إن تحصيل الحاصل محال في بديهة العقول، فعلمنا : أن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر ليس حكماً بديهياً . فهذا تمام الكلام في طرق السؤال والاعتراض
The third argument: If this premise were intuitive, then the knowledge that the enduring possible, during its persistence, is in need of the efficient cause would be intuitive because possibility is present in it. But the matter is not so, for most rational people deny it, saying: 'The attainment of the attained is impossible in the intuition of minds.' Thus we know that the knowledge of the possible's need for the efficient cause is not an intuitive judgment. This concludes the discussion on the methods of question and objection.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument that the need of the possible for a cause is not an intuitive (badihi) judgment. The author argues by conditional: if the premise (that the possible needs a cause) were intuitive, then it would be intuitive that the enduring possible (al-mumkin al-baqi) continues to need the cause during its persistence, because possibility (imkan) remains in it. But in reality, most rational people deny this, claiming that 'attainment of the attained' (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible—meaning that once something exists, it does not need a further cause to continue. Therefore, the original premise is not intuitive. This completes the discussion on the methods of questioning and objecting.

Key Terms
بديهية
الممكن الباقي
المؤثر
الإمكان
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the need for a cause is always intuitive.
Correction: The author shows this is not the case because most rational people deny that the enduring possible needs a cause during its persistence, citing the impossibility of tahsil al-hasil.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why is the need of the possible for a cause not a self-evident (badihi) judgment?
A) Because the possible is always changing.
B) Because most rational people deny that the enduring possible needs a cause during its persistence.
C) Because the cause is unknown.
D) Because the possible is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The need of a possible for a cause is not a self-evident judgment, as shown by rational disagreement and the principle of tahsil al-hasil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you have a 'tahsil al-hasil' moment in your own faith—an area where you think you have 'arrived' and no longer need to seek? Examine your heart: Is your conviction built on constant seeking or on comfortable assumption?
?
The argument forces you to ask: Are you willing to admit that some of your deepest beliefs are not self-evident? If the need for a cause is not intuitive, then your reliance on Allah is a matter of acquired knowledge, not mere intuition. How will you actively reinforce that knowledge today, so that it becomes a living certainty rather than a passive assumption?

Second Method: The Self-Evident Need of the Possible for a Cause
Second method: directly asserting the intuitive judgment that a possible needs a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second method for proving that a possible being necessarily requires a cause, and how this argument is based on a self-evident rational judgment.

Core Idea
The possible's need for a cause is self-evident, not merely proven by complex arguments.

This passage presents an alternative approach to proving that every possible being requires a cause. Instead of relying on elaborate philosophical puzzles, the author appeals directly to the definition of the possible and the clear judgment of reason. We will see how this simpler method grounds the fundamental principle of causality in the very nature of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a different approach than the first method.
2
Understand the definition of the possible
The possible is defined as that whose relation to existence is equal to its relation to non-existence. This means it has no intrinsic inclination towards either state.
This definition is key to the argument.
3
Grasp the rational judgment
Reason judges that if one side (existence or non-existence) becomes actual, it must be due to a separate cause. Without it, equality would persist.
This judgment is presented as clear and necessary.
4
Reflect on the self-evident nature
The author claims this knowledge is intuitive and necessary (badihi daruri). Even someone who verbally denies it still holds it in their conscience.
This shows the strength of the argument.
5
Note the conclusion
By relying on this method, there is no need for complex arguments or examples. This suffices as an answer to the first objection.
The method is presented as simpler and more direct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن نترك الوجه الذي ذكرناه ، ونقول إن الممكن هو الذي يكون نسبة الوجود إليه كنسبة العدم، ومتى اعتقدنا أن نسبة الوجود ونسبة العدم إليه على التساوي ، حكم صريح العقل بأنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر إلا لأمر منفصل ، والعلم بأن الأمر كذلك علم بديهي ضروري ، ومن أنكر ذلك فقد فارق مقتضى عقله لساناً ، ويعود إليه ضميراً ، وإذا عولنا على هذا الطريق فلا حاجة بنا إلى ذكر تلك الشبهات وتقرير تلك الأمثلة . فهذا هو الجواب عن السؤال الأول
The second: That we abandon the method we mentioned, and say: The possible is that whose relation to existence is like its relation to non-existence. Whenever we believe that the relation to existence and the relation to non-existence are equal, the clear intellect judges that it is impossible for one side to preponderate over the other except by a separate matter. And the knowledge that this is so is intuitive and necessary knowledge. Whoever denies that has verbally departed from the requirement of his intellect, but returns to it in his conscience. If we rely on this method, we have no need to mention those ambiguities and establish those examples. This is the answer to the first question.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the second method for answering the first objection. The author sets aside the previous argument and directly appeals to the definition of the possible (al-mumkin): it is that whose relation to existence is equal to its relation to non-existence. When we hold that belief, reason necessarily judges that preponderance of one side over the other cannot occur without a separate cause. This judgment is self-evident (badihi) and necessary (daruri). Even if someone verbally denies it, he still holds it internally. Therefore, this method avoids complex arguments and directly proves the need for a cause. This concludes the answer to the first question.

Key Terms
الممكن
نسبة الوجود
نسبة العدم
التساوي
أمر منفصل
بديهي ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the need for a cause requires elaborate proof.
Correction: The author shows it is self-evident from the nature of the possible; no elaborate arguments are needed.

Checkpoint

A) The consensus of philosophers
B) The definition of the possible as having equal relation to existence and non-existence
C) The impossibility of an infinite regress
D) The authority of revelation

Key Takeaway
The second method proves that a possible needs a cause by appealing to the self-evident rational judgment that equal possibility cannot tip without an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your very existence is as possible as your non-existence, do you truly feel your absolute dependence on a cause every moment, or have you become accustomed to taking existence for granted? What practice could help you internalize this dependence?

The author says that even the denier of this truth returns to it in his conscience. Is there any belief in your heart that you profess verbally but resist internally? What would it cost you to fully align your conscience with your faith?
?
If the need for a cause is self-evident, why do you still find yourself living as if you are self-sufficient? What specific situation today can you use as a reminder that you are contingent and in need of Allah's constant support?

Possibility as the True Cause of Need for a Cause
Sanusi argues that imkan is the cause of need for a mueaththir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkan), not origination (huduth), is the rational cause for the need of an effective cause (mueaththir).

Core Idea
Possibility, not origination, necessitates a cause.

In kalam, establishing the need for a Creator hinges on identifying what exactly makes something require a cause. Some look to origination (huduth), but Sanusi argues that the true cause is possibility (imkan). In this passage, he presents a concise proof and then a more elaborate argument from the definition of the possible. This distinction is foundational for later proofs of God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the translation. Notice that it mentions two arguments.
The passage begins by stating the conclusion, then gives a second argument.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two approaches: first, a brief statement that imkan is the cause; second, a detailed argument starting with 'The second [argument]'. Focus on the second argument's logical steps.
3
Understand the self-evident judgment
The second argument defines the mumkin and claims that the intellect necessarily judges that an external cause is required for preponderance. This judgment is described as badihi/daruri (self-evident).
Self-evident knowledge does not require proof; denying it contradicts reason.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi emphasize imkan over huduth? How does this shift the basis for needing a Creator?
This distinction avoids problems with the origination argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن المؤثر في هذه الحاجة ليس إلا الإمكان ، وبهذا الطريق يظهر لنا أن الإمكان علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر وأما بيان أن الحدوث يمتنع كونه معتبراً في هذا الباب فسياتي في تقريره فصل مفرد . والثاني : أن نترك الوجه الذي ذكرناه ، ونقول إن الممكن هو الذي يكون نسبة الوجود إليه كنسبة العدم، ومتى اعتقدنا أن نسبة الوجود ونسبة العدم إليه على التساوي ، حكم صريح العقل بأنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر إلا لأمر منفصل ، والعلم بأن الأمر كذلك علم بديهي ضروري ، ومن أنكر ذلك فقد فارق مقتضى عقله لساناً ، ويعود إليه ضميراً ، وإذا عولنا على هذا الطريق فلا حاجة بنا إلى ذكر تلك الشبهات وتقرير تلك الأمثلة . فهذا هو الجواب عن السؤال الأول
That the effective cause in this need is nothing but possibility (imkan), and by this way it becomes apparent to us that possibility is the cause of the need for an effective cause. As for the explanation that origination (huduth) is impossible to be considered in this topic, it will come in its own separate chapter. The second [argument]: that we abandon the aforementioned approach and say: the possible is that whose relation of existence to it is like the relation of non-existence, and when we believe that the relation of existence and the relation of non-existence to it are equal, the clear intellect judges that it is impossible for one of the two sides to preponderate over the other except by a separate matter, and the knowledge that the matter is so is a necessary self-evident knowledge (daruri). Whoever denies that has contradicted the requirement of his intellect in speech and returns to it in conscience. And if we rely on this way, we have no need to mention those doubts and establish those examples. This is the answer to the first question.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that the need for an effective cause is based on possibility (imkan) rather than origination (huduth). It begins by stating that the effective cause in this need is nothing but possibility, and then gives a second argument: the possible is defined as that whose relation to existence and non-existence is equal. When we consider this equality, the clear intellect necessarily judges that no preponderance of one side can occur without an external cause. This judgment is self-evident (badihi or daruri). If we adopt this argument, we do not need to enter into other debates. This serves as the answer to the first question.

مؤثر (mueaththir)
حدوث (huduth)
ممكن (mumkin)
بديهي (badihi / daruri)

Misconception: The need for a cause is based on origination (huduth) — that things come into existence at a time.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the need is based on possibility (imkan), not origination. Origination is not the cause; possibility is.

Checkpoint

A) Origination (huduth)
B) Possibility (imkan)
C) The intellect
D) The existence of the possible

Key Takeaway
The necessity of an effective cause arises from the possibility of the possible, not from its origination.

Sanusi's Defense of the Preponderance Principle Against Two Objections
Sanusi refutes two counterarguments on the preponderance of the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the rational necessity of a preponderant for the possible against two objections: one claiming unequal certainty, and another citing scholarly disagreement.

Core Idea
Proper reflection shows that preponderance without a cause is impossible, just as one is half of two.

Sanusi now addresses two challenges to the claim that every possible needs a determining cause. The first objection claims that the judgment is not as certain as basic mathematical truths; the second points to disagreement among thinkers. In his refutation, Sanusi reaffirms the self-evidence of the principle when properly understood, and challenges the premise that opponents genuinely allow preponderance without a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read the first objection: that the certainty of 'one is half of two' is stronger than the certainty that the possible needs a preponderant.
Notice the claim of unequal certainty.
2
Study Sanusi's first answer
Sanusi denies any disparity. He explains that when one properly considers the equal relation of existence and non-existence, the judgment becomes just as self-evident as the mathematical truth.
Key phrase: 'when the intellect considers this proposition in this way, no disparity remains'.
3
Read the second objection
The second objection: some rational thinkers allowed preponderance without a preponderant, implying the judgment is not self-evident.
4
Examine Sanusi's second response
Sanusi rejects the premise: he does not concede that those thinkers actually affirm preponderance without a cause. The response is cut off but the core denial is clear.
This shows how to handle disagreement about self-evident truths.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how both objections fail. The first misjudges the clarity of rational intuition; the second mistakes disagreement for genuine opposition to the principle.
The principle of causality remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في المعارضة الأولى : ( إن جزم العقل بقولنا : الواحد نصف الأثنين ، أقوى من جزمه بأن الممكن لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح . فالجواب الحق : أن هذا التفاوت ممنوع وذلك لأن من أزال عن عقله تعود الجدال والمنازعة في كل شيء ، واستحضر في عقله أن نسبة الوجود اليه كنسبة العدم إليه ، وأنه لا رجحان لأحد الجانبين على الآخر البتة ، جزم جزماً بديهيا : أنه ما دام يبقى هذا الأستواء فإنه يمتنع حصول الرجحان ، فإن حصل الرجحان فقد زال الاستواء وانضم إلى الطرف الراجح شيء آخر ، فإذا اعتبر العقل هذه القضية على هذا الوجه ، لم يبق بينها وبين القول الواحد نصف الأثنين تفاوت . وأما المعارضة الثانية وهي قوله : ( إن جمعا من العقلاء جوزوا رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، ولو كانت هذه القضية بديهية لما اختلفوا فيها .. فنقول : لا نسلم أنهم يلتزمون رجحان الممكن لا عن مرجح نعم ربما
As for his statement in the first objection: 'The certainty of the intellect in our saying 'one is half of two' is stronger than its certainty that the possible does not have one of its sides preponderate over the other except by a preponderant.' The correct answer: that this disparity is denied, because one who removes from his mind the habit of disputation and contention in everything, and presents in his mind that the relation of existence to it is like the relation of non-existence, and that there is no preponderance of one side over the other at all, will judge with a self-evident judgment: that as long as this equality remains, the occurrence of preponderance is impossible. If preponderance occurs, then equality has ceased and something else has been joined to the preponderant side. When the intellect considers this proposition in this way, no disparity remains between it and the statement 'one is half of two'. As for the second objection, his statement: 'A group of rational thinkers allowed the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderant, and if this proposition were self-evident, they would not have differed about it.' We say: we do not concede that they affirm preponderance of the possible without a preponderant. Indeed, perhaps...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage responds to two objections against the judgment that the possible needs a preponderant. The first objection claims that the certainty of 'one is half of two' is stronger. The answer denies any disparity: when one properly considers the equal relation of existence to non-existence, it is equally self-evident that any preponderance requires an external cause. Proper contemplation dissolves the supposed weakness. The second objection notes that some rational thinkers have allowed preponderance without a preponderant, implying the judgment is not self-evident. The author rejects this premise: he does not grant that they actually affirm preponderance without a cause. The response is cut off but makes the point by denying the claim.

Key Terms
بديهي (badihi)
رجحان (rujhan)
مرجح (murajjih)
معارضة (mu'arada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If some scholars disagree about a rational judgment, it cannot be self-evident.
Correction: Sanusi argues that disagreement does not disprove self-evidence; rather, he denies that the opponents truly affirm the counterposition.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi respond to the claim that the judgment of 'one is half of two' is stronger than the judgment that the possible needs a preponderant?
A) He accepts the disparity
B) He denies the disparity and shows that both are equally self-evident when properly considered
C) He says the judgment about the possible is stronger
D) He says the two are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The rational need for a preponderant is as self-evident as basic mathematical truths, and alleged disagreement does not undermine its certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
