Answer to Objection: Equal Certainty of the Causal Principle
Author rebuts the claim that the certainty of 'one is half of two' is stronger than the contingent's need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the equal certainty of the causal principle compared to a mathematical axiom.

Core Idea
Proper reflection reveals that the causal principle is as self-evident as mathematics.

The objection claims that the principle 'every possible needs a cause' is less certain than 'one is half of two'. The author challenges this by asking us to clear our minds of habits and consider the possible's equal relation to existence and non-existence. When we do, the causal principle becomes equally self-evident. This passage demonstrates a key method in kalam: defending foundational principles by showing their self-evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection is presented in the first sentence, and then the author gives his answer.
The objection uses a comparative claim about certainty.
2
Identify the core move
The author tells us to remove from our minds the habit of disputation and to bring to mind the equal relation of the possible to existence and non-existence. This is the key to the answer.
This mental exercise is what restores self-evidence.
3
Understand the argument
Once we recognize the equal relation, we judge with self-evidence that as long as equality remains, inclination is impossible. If inclination occurs, equality must have been broken by something else (the determinant).
The logic is: equality → no inclination; inclination → equality broken → a determinant.
4
Conclude the rebuttal
The author concludes that when the mind considers this proposition in this way, there is no disparity between its certainty and that of 'one is half of two'. The objection is thus defeated.
The causal principle is shown to be equally self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في المعارضة الأولى : ( إن جزم العقل بقولنا : الواحد نصف الأثنين ، أقوى من جزمه بأن الممكن لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح . فالجواب الحق : أن هذا التفاوت ممنوع وذلك لأن من أزال عن عقله تعود الجدال والمنازعة في كل شيء ، واستحضر في عقله أن نسبة الوجود اليه كنسبة العدم إليه ، وأنه لا رجحان لأحد الجانبين على الآخر البتة ، جزم جزماً بديهيا : أنه ما دام يبقى (٢) هذا الأستواء فإنه يمتنع حصول الرجحان ، فإن حصل الرجحان فقد زال الاستواء وانضم إلى الطرف الراجح شيء آخر ، فإذا اعتبر العقل هذه القضية على هذا الوجه ، لم يبق بينها وبين القول الواحد نصف الأثنين تفاوت
As for his statement in the first objection: 'The certainty of the mind regarding our saying 'one is half of two' is stronger than its certainty that the possible does not incline to one of its two sides except through a determinant.' The true answer is that this disparity is denied, because one who removes from his mind the habit of disputation and contention in everything, and brings to his mind that the relation of existence to it is like the relation of non-existence, and that there is absolutely no inclination of one side over the other, will judge with self-evident certainty that as long as this equality remains, the occurrence of inclination is impossible; if inclination occurs, then equality has ceased and something else has joined the inclined side. So when the mind considers this proposition in this manner, there remains no disparity between it and the statement 'one is half of two.'
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first objection, which claimed that the principle 'the possible does not occur without a cause' is less certain than the mathematical axiom 'one is half of two'. The author argues that this disparity is denied. If one clears the mind of contentious habits and properly considers that the possible is equally related to existence and non-existence, one will judge self-evidently that inclination (i.e., existence) is impossible as long as equality remains. Once equality is lost, something else (the determinant) must have come into play. Thus, when considered correctly, the certainty of the causal principle equals that of the mathematical axiom.

Key Terms
جزم
بديهي
مرجح
استواء
رجحان
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mathematical axioms are always more certain than metaphysical principles.
Correction: The author argues that the causal principle, when properly understood, is equally self-evident; the disparity is denied.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what removes the disparity between the certainty of the causal principle and the mathematical axiom?
A) Accepting that the possible can occur without a cause
B) Considering the possible's relation to existence and non-existence as equal
C) Adopting a skeptical attitude
D) Relying on authority

Key Takeaway
When properly understood, the causal principle that every possible needs a cause is as self-evident as a mathematical axiom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you truly feel it is just as possible that you might not have existed? Or do you take your existence for granted, ignoring the absolute dependence on a Cause? How would your daily gratitude change if you meditated on this self-evident truth?
?
The author says we must remove 'the habit of disputation and contention in everything' to see the causal principle clearly. In what areas of your life do you resist clear truths because you love to argue or doubt? Will you commit to silencing that habit today and accepting what is self-evident?
?
If the causal principle is as certain as 'one is half of two', then every event in your life has a cause that ultimately traces back to the Necessary Being. Why, then, do you sometimes live as if things happen by chance? What would it look like to live each moment with the certainty that nothing is random and that you are constantly sustained by the First Cause?

Answer to the Objection of Rational Disagreement
Author responds to the claim that rational disagreement shows the principle is not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that some rational thinkers disagree about causality does not disprove the self-evidence of the principle of sufficient reason.

Core Idea
Disagreement does not disprove self-evidence; entailed belief is not the same as committed belief.

The second objection claims that since some rational people have allowed for a possible event to occur without a cause, the principle cannot be self-evident. In this response, the author clarifies that merely being logically entailed by one's doctrines is not equivalent to actually holding that belief. This distinction is crucial for defending the foundational status of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the objector presents a claim, and the author responds with a distinction.
The objection is cited within the passage.
2
Understand the key distinction
Focus on the terms 'ilzam' (entailment) and 'iltizam' (commitment). The author argues that what is entailed by a doctrine is not necessarily what the holder of that doctrine explicitly accepts.
This is the core of the defense.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does this distinction protect the self-evidence of the principle? Consider that self-evident truths are known directly, not through entailment from other beliefs.
Disagreement based on entailment does not challenge self-evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الثانية وهي قوله : ( إن جمعا من العقلاء جوزوا رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، ولو كانت هذه القضية بديهية لما اختلفوا فيها .. فنقول : لا نسلم أنهم يلتزمون رجحان الممكن لا عن مرجح (۳) نعم ربما لزمهم ذلك على بعض مذاهبهم، لكن الفرق بين الإلزام وبين الالتزام معلوم .
As for the second objection—which is his statement: 'A group of rational people have permitted the inclination of one side of the possible over the other without a determinant; and if this proposition were self-evident, they would not have differed about it'—we say: We do not concede that they commit themselves to the inclination of the possible without a determinant. Indeed, perhaps it may be entailed by some of their doctrines, but the difference between entailment and commitment is known.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection, which argues that some rational people have allowed that a possible can occur without a cause, and if the principle were truly self-evident, there would be no disagreement. The author denies that those people actually commit themselves to the proposition that the possible occurs without a cause. It may be that their other doctrines logically entail that consequence, but being logically entailed (ilzam) is not the same as actual commitment (iltizam). The distinction is clear and well-known.

Key Terms
بديهية
مرجح
إلزام
التزام
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any disagreement about a principle proves it is not self-evident.
Correction: Disagreement based on logical entailment from other beliefs is not the same as direct denial of the principle. Self-evident truths can still be accepted by all who understand them; disagreement may arise from confusion or consequences of other views.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the fact that some rational people allow a possible to occur without a cause?
A) He admits they are correct
B) He argues that they are not rational
C) He distinguishes between entailment and actual commitment
D) He says the principle is not self-evident

Key Takeaway
The author defends the self-evidence of causality by distinguishing between what is entailed by a doctrine and what is explicitly committed to.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there truths you reject simply because others disagree, without examining whether their disagreement stems from genuine commitment or mere entailment? How will you change your approach now?
?
What doctrines do you hold that logically entail consequences you have never admitted? Will you search for those entailments and commit to the truth, wherever it leads, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
In your own life, have you ever confused what you are logically committed to (through other beliefs) with what you actually believe? How can you begin to align your explicit commitments with the full entailments of your faith?

The Contingent's Need for a Cause at Continuance
Argument and refutation on causation in continuance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contingent does not need a cause in its state of continuance, and how an objection regarding continuation is refuted.

Core Idea
A cause cannot affect a permanent thing in its continuance without absurdity.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate about whether contingent beings require a cause not only for their origination but also for their continued existence. The author argues that causation in continuance leads to logical impossibilities, and then addresses an objection about the nature of continuation itself. Follow the logical steps carefully to see why the contingent remains independent of a cause once it exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a main claim, a supporting proof, then an objection and its refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that if the contingent needed a cause in origination, it would need it in continuance. But then he argues that the permanent in continuance does not need a cause. Identify the key terms: 'contingent', 'cause', 'permanent', 'continuance'.
3
Follow the proof by contradiction
The proof assumes a cause influencing the permanent in its continuance. Then it examines two possibilities: the cause has an effect or not. If no effect, it's not a cause. If it has an effect, the effect either already existed (absurd) or did not exist (then it is originated, so the cause only affects originated things, not the permanent). Both lead to contradiction.
Trace how each branch leads to impossibility.
4
Analyze the objection and refutation
An objector suggests that the effect could be the continuation itself. The refutation splits continuation into whether it is the thing itself or a separate state. If the same, causing it is obtaining the already obtained (impossible). If separate, the continuation is originated, so again the cause only affects originated things, not the permanent. Thus the objection fails.
This shows the depth of the argument.
5
Summarize the lesson
Now that you've seen the full argument, restate in your own words why the contingent does not need a cause in continuance. Use the key terms.
This reinforces understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيثبت بما ذكرنا : إنه لو افتقر الممكن إلى المؤثر، لافتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ، وإنما قلنا : إن الباقي حال بقائه لا يفتقر إلى المؤثر ، لأنا لو فرضنا مؤثراً يؤثر فيه في حال بقائه ، فذلك المؤثر، إما أن يكون له أثر أو لا يكون له أثر ، فإن لم يكن له أثر ، امتنع وصفه بكونه مؤثراً ، وإن كان له أثر فذلك الأثر إما أن يكون شيئاً يصدق عليه ، أنه كان حاصلا قبل هذا التأثير ، أو يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التأثير ، فإن كان الأول لزم إيجاد الموجود وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك الأثر أثراً يصدق عليه أنه كان موجوداً قبل ذلك الوقت ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث ، فهذا المؤثر لا تأثير له إلا في الحادث فلا يكون له أثر في الباقي ، وقد فرضناه مؤثراً في الباقي هذا خلف

لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال إن أثره هو بقاء ذلك الشيء الذي كان موجوداً ؟ لأنا نقول : بقاء ذلك الشيء إما أن يكون نفسه أو غيره ، فإن كان بقاؤه نفسه ، ونفسه كانت حاصلة قبل هذا الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون تأثير هذا المؤثر في تحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال ، وإن كان بقاؤه غيره فحينئذ يكون المسند إلى المؤثر هو هذه الحالة الزائدة الحادثة ، فحينئذ لم يصدر عن المؤثر إلا ما كان حادثاً ، فحينئذ لا يكون لهذا المؤثر تأثيراً في الباقي . وقد فرضناه كذلك . هذا خلف
It is established by what we mentioned: That if the contingent needed the cause, then the permanent in its state of permanence would need the cause. And we only say: That the permanent in its state of permanence does not need the cause, because if we suppose a cause influencing it in its state of permanence, then that cause either has an effect or does not have an effect. If it has no effect, it is impossible to describe it as a cause. And if it has an effect, then that effect is either something that is true of it that it was obtained before this influence, or it is true of it that it was not obtained before this influence. If the first, then it necessitates producing the existent (ījād al-mawjūd), which is impossible. And if the second, then that effect is an effect that is true of it that it existed before that time, and everything that is like that is originated (ḥādith). So this cause has no influence except in the originated, so it has no effect in the permanent, and we have supposed it to have influence in the permanent. This is a contradiction.

It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that its effect is the continuation (baqā') of that thing which existed? Because we say: The continuation of that thing is either itself or other than itself. If its continuation is itself, and itself was obtained before this time, then the influence of this cause would be in obtaining what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. And if its continuation is other than itself, then what is attributed to the cause is this supervening state that is originated, so then from the cause only what is originated has proceeded, so then this cause has no influence in the permanent. And we have supposed it to be so. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the first shubha (doubt/argument) concerning the need of the contingent for a cause at the moment of its continuance. The author begins by stating that if we claim the contingent needs a cause in its origination, it would also need a cause in its continuance. But the author argues that the permanent in its state of permanence does not need a cause, through a detailed argument: If we suppose a cause affecting it during continuance, then either that cause produces an effect or not. If no effect, it is not a cause. If it produces an effect, either that effect existed before (leading to producing the existent, impossible) or it did not exist before (then the effect is originated, but the thing was already permanent, so the cause only affects originated things, not the permanent). Thus, the supposition leads to contradiction. Then an objection is raised: what if the effect is the continuation itself? The answer distinguishes: if continuation is the thing itself, then causing it is obtaining the already obtained; if continuation is a separate state, then that state is originated, again no effect on the permanent itself. So the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
ممكن (mumkin) - contingent
مؤثر (mu'aththir) - cause/effector
باقي (bāqī) - permanent
حالة بقائه (ḥālat baqā'ih) - state of its continuance
إيجاد الموجود (ījād al-mawjūd) - producing the existent
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) - obtaining the already obtained
حادث (ḥādith) - originated
خلف (khalf) - contradiction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the contingent needs a cause continuously, both in origination and in continuance.
Correction: The argument shows that continuance is a different state; any supposed cause would either produce an already existing thing (absurd) or only affect originated parts (not the permanent itself). Thus, the contingent does not need a cause in continuance.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what happens if a cause influences a permanent thing in its state of continuance and the effect is something that already existed before?
A) It is permissible.
B) It leads to producing the existent, which is impossible.
C) It means the cause has no effect.
D) It means the effect is originated.

Key Takeaway
The contingent's continuance does not require a cause because any supposed effect would either be already existing (impossible) or newly originated (irrelevant to the permanent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your very existence at this moment does not require a new cause from any created thing? How does that free you from dependence on people, wealth, or health, and yet demand a deeper dependence on God?
?
You have likely read that Allah is al-Qayyūm, the Self-Subsisting. But do you live with the awareness that your continuance is not caused by your own effort or any external factor, but is a direct sign of His power? How would your anxiety about tomorrow change if you internalized this?
?
The proof shows that no created cause can affect you in your permanence. When you fear someone's harm or hope for someone's benefit in your ongoing life, are you attributing power to a cause that cannot truly affect you? What would it mean to realign your trust accordingly?

Proof of Free Will from Blame and Praise
Sanusi uses the stone-throwing analogy to prove human free will and deny the necessity of a preponderant cause for the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intuitive distinction between blaming and praising actions demonstrates human free will and why this implies that the possible does not require a preponderant cause.

Core Idea
Blame and praise only make sense if humans are free agents.

Sanusi presents a powerful argument for free will based on a simple everyday observation: we instinctively blame a person who throws a stone, not the stone itself. This distinction would be meaningless if all actions were determined by external causes. In this passage, he argues that this moral intuition proves human choice and even challenges the necessity of a preponderant cause for all possibles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English versions carefully. Notice the contrast introduced between the trembling person (involuntary motion) and the stone thrower (voluntary action).
This contrast is the foundation of the argument.
2
Identify the core analogy
Understand the analogy: the stone is moved involuntarily, while the hand of the thrower moves voluntarily. The intelligent know that praising or blaming the stone is inappropriate, but praising or blaming the thrower is appropriate.
3
Grasp the logical inference
Sanusi argues: if the slave (human) were not free in his action, this distinction between voluntary and involuntary would not hold. Since the distinction does hold, the slave must be free.
This is a proof by contradiction: deny free will, and moral accountability collapses.
4
Connect to the kalam conclusion
The freedom of the slave proves that the possible (a contingent action) does not need a preponderant cause to incline it one way or another. This supports the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb, where humans acquire actions freely while God creates them.
This challenges the deterministic view that every event has a necessitating cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون المرتعش متحركاً بالاضطرار وأيضاً : فمن رمى حجراً إلى وجه إنسان ، فإنه يعرف الفرق بين حركة الحجر وبين يد ذلك الرامي ، فإن العقلاء ببدائه عقولهم يعلمون : أنه لا يجوز مدح ذلك الحجر وذمه في تلك الحركة ، وأنه يحسن مدح ذلك الرامي وذمه في تلك الحركة ، ولو لم يكن العبد مختاراً في فعله ، لما بقيت هذه التفرقة ، فثبت : أنه لو توقف رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر بلا مرجح ، لزم أن لا يكون العبد مختاراً في فعله ، وثبت أنه مختار في فعله ، فوجب أن لا يفتقر الممكن إلى المرجح .
The trembling person is moved involuntarily, and also: whoever throws a stone at a person's face knows the difference between the motion of the stone and the hand of the thrower. For the intelligent, by the primordials of their intellects, know that it is not permissible to praise or blame that stone for that motion, and that it is good to praise or blame that thrower for that motion. If the slave were not free in his action, this distinction would not remain. So it is established: if the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other were to depend on no preponderant cause, it would necessarily follow that the slave is not free in his action. And it is established that he is free in his action. Therefore it is necessary that the possible does not need a preponderant cause.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a proof for human free will and a refutation of the view that every action requires a determinate cause. It begins by contrasting involuntary motion (like trembling) with voluntary action (throwing a stone). People intuitively distinguish between the stone, which is not praised or blamed, and the thrower, who is praised or blamed. This distinction only makes sense if the thrower is a free agent. If all actions were necessitated by a preponderant cause without the agent's choice, then the thrower would be like the stone, and blame would be meaningless. The conclusion: since the slave is free in his action, the possible does not require a preponderant cause (i.e., the agent can choose one of two equal alternatives without an external determinant).

Key Terms
المرتعش (the trembling person)
المختار (the free agent)
المرجح (preponderant cause)
الممكن (the possible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God creates human actions.
Correction: The passage does not deny divine creation; it asserts human responsibility through acquisition (kasb). The distinction between voluntary and involuntary is the basis for moral accountability while still affirming that God is the ultimate creator of all actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a stone not be praised or blamed for its motion?
A) Because it is inanimate and not a moral agent.
B) Because it is thrown by a free agent.
C) Because its motion is caused by another.
D) Because it has no existence.

Key Takeaway
The stone-throwing analogy shows that innate human moral judgment requires free will, and this free will implies that some actions are not necessitated by external causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you blamed yourself sincerely for a sin. Did you truly believe you could have chosen otherwise, or did you secretly excuse yourself as being 'forced' by circumstances? This lesson says your own blame proves your free will. How does that hold you accountable today?
?
If you were to apply Sanusi's stone-throwing analogy to your own life, which actions would you admit are truly your own, and which would you have to stop pretending are forced? Will you take responsibility for one specific choice you have been avoiding?
?
The lesson concludes that the possible does not need a preponderant cause. How does that change the way you approach your choices tonight? Are you willing to act without waiting for a determining 'reason' to make a difficult decision?

Refuting Possibility in the Essence Itself
Refutation of the claim that possibility inheres in the essence itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that possibility cannot be attributed to the essence (mahiyya) itself, regardless of whether the essence is identical to existence or distinct from it.

Core Idea
Possibility cannot be in the essence; essence is either existence (which denies non-existence) or necessary in itself.

In this passage, the author tackles an objection that defines possibility in terms of the essence's capacity to accept existence and non-existence. He systematically examines two scenarios—whether the essence is identical to existence or distinct—and shows that neither yields a coherent subject for possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's definition
Start by reading the opponent's definition of possibility, which begins with 'بمعنى أنه من حيث إنه هو'. Understand that they claim the essence itself, disregarding cause, accepts existence and non-existence.
This definition is the starting point of the refutation.
2
First scenario: essence equals existence
The author considers the possibility that the essence of a thing is identical to its existence. If so, the essence has no identity apart from existence, and existence is incompatible with non-existence, so it cannot accept non-existence.
3
Second scenario: essence is distinct from existence
If the essence is other than existence, the subject of possibility could be the essence itself, existence itself, or the combination of essence and existence. The author refutes each: essence as such is necessary, existence as such is necessary, and the combination leads to an infinite regress if it is existential.
Follow the three sub-cases carefully.
4
Conclusion of the first objection
The author concludes that all possibilities fail, so the first objection is false.
The phrase 'فثبت أن هذا العذر الذي ذكرتموه باطل' marks the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمعنى أنه من حيث إنه هو ، مع قطع النظر عن وجود السبب وعن عدمه ، فإنه يقبل الوجود والعدم أو يقول : إنه صار واجب الوجود في الحال لحضور سبب وجوده ، لكنه ممكن الوجود بالنظر إلى الزمان المستقبل . والجواب : أن نقول : أما الوجه الأول فباطل ، لأن ماهية الشيء إما أن تكون عين وجوده أو غيره، فإن كان الأول امتنع أن يقال : إنه من حيث إنه هو ممكن الوجود ، لأن على هذا القول لا هوية له سوى الوجود ، لكن الوجود من حيث إنه وجود ينافي العدم، والمنافي للشيء لا يكون قابلاً له ، فوجب أن يمتنع كون تلك الماهية قابلة للعدم . وأما الاحتمال الثاني : وهو أن تكون الماهية غير الوجود ، فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير المحكوم عليه بالإمكان ، إما الماهية ، أو الوجود ، أو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، ومحال أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالإمكان هو الماهية لأن الماهية من حيث هي هي، لا تقبل الانتفاء والتبديل ، بل هي واجبة التحقق لعينها ولذاتها فلا تكون ممكنة البتة. ومحال أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالإمكان هو الوجود من حيث إنه وجود ، لأن الوجود من حيث إنه وجود لا يقبل العدم، فيمتنع أن يكون الوجود من حيث إنه وجود قابلاً للعدم . ومحال أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالإمكان هو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود ، لان تلك الموصوفية إن لم تكن أمراً وجودياً فقد بطل الحديث ، وإن كان أمراً وجودياً عاد التقسيم الأول . فثبت أن هذا العذر الذي ذكرتموه باطل . وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو أن الإمكان إنما يتحقق بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل . فنقول : إما أن يكون المراد منه : أن عند حصول الاستقبال ، يحصل هذا الإمكان وإما أن يكون المراد منه : أن الإمكان حصل في الحال بالنسبة إلى الاستقبال ، والأول باطل ، لأن الاستقبال ينقلب حالا عند الحضور ، فيعود السؤال المذكور . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، لأن حصول الشيء في الاستقبال مشروط بحصول الاستقبال ، والاستقبال محال الحصول في الحال، والموقف على المحال محال ، فالشيء بشرط حصوله في الاستقبال مستحيل الحصول في الحال ، وإذا كان هو بهذا الأعتبار ممتنع الحصول، امتنع أن يقال : إنه بهذا الاعتبار يكون ممكن الحصول، لأن الإمكان الخاص والامتناع لا يجتمعان في الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد .
Meaning that it, from the perspective of itself, disregarding the existence of the cause and its non-existence, accepts existence and non-existence; or he says: it has become necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) at the present moment due to the presence of its cause of existence, but it is possible of existence (mumkin al-wujud) with respect to future time. The answer: we say: As for the first aspect, it is false, because the essence (mahiyya) of a thing is either identical to its existence or other than it. If the first, then it is impossible to say that it is, from the perspective of itself, possible of existence, because on this view it has no identity other than existence, but existence as existence is incompatible with non-existence, and what is incompatible with a thing cannot accept it, so it is necessary that that essence cannot be receptive to non-existence. As for the second possibility, that the essence is other than existence, we say: on this assumption, the subject of the judgment of possibility is either the essence, or existence, or the qualification of the essence by existence. It is impossible that the subject of the judgment of possibility is the essence, because the essence as such does not accept extinction and change; rather, it is necessarily realized (wajibat al-tahaqquq) in itself and by its essence, so it is not possible at all. And it is impossible that the subject of the judgment of possibility is existence as existence, because existence as existence does not accept non-existence, so it is impossible that existence as existence is receptive to non-existence. And it is impossible that the subject of the judgment of possibility is the qualification of the essence by existence, because that qualification, if it is not a existential matter, then the discussion is vain; and if it is an existential matter, the first division returns. So it is established that this excuse you mentioned is false. As for the second question, which is that possibility is only realized with respect to future time, we say: either the intended meaning is that when the future arrives, this possibility occurs; or the intended meaning is that possibility occurred at present with respect to the future. The first is false, because the future becomes present at the moment of arrival, so the mentioned question returns. The second is also false, because the realization of something in the future is conditional upon the realization of the future, and the future is impossible to realize at present, and what is suspended on the impossible is impossible. So the thing, on condition of its realization in the future, is impossible to realize at present. And if it is, in this consideration, impossible of realization, it is impossible to say that it is, in this consideration, possible of realization, because specific possibility (al-imkan al-khass) and impossibility (al-Imtina') cannot coexist in one thing under one consideration.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an objection and refutation. The opponent presents a definition of possibility: something that, considered in itself, disregarding cause, accepts existence and non-existence, but he qualifies it by saying that with future time it becomes possible. The author refutes in two parts. First, he addresses the claim that the essence of the thing is either identical to existence or different. If identical, then the essence cannot accept non-existence because existence is incompatible with non-existence. If different, then the subject of possibility could be the essence, existence, or the combination. The essence as such is necessary, existence as such is necessary, and the combination leads to an infinite regress if it is existential. Thus the first excuse is false. Second, regarding the claim that possibility is relative to future time, he examines two interpretations. The first, that possibility occurs when the future arrives, is false because the future becomes present and the same question returns. The second, that possibility is present now with respect to the future, is also false because the future cannot be realized at present, so the condition is impossible, and thus the thing cannot be possible under that condition.

Key Terms
إمكان
ماهية
وجود
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the essence itself can be the subject of possibility, i.e., that something is possible because of its very essence.
Correction: The author shows that the essence as such is either identical to existence (which cannot accept non-existence) or distinct but necessary in itself, so it cannot be possible. Possibility must belong to something else, not the essence alone.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for the essence (mahiyya) to be the subject of the judgment of possibility if the essence is distinct from existence?
A) Because the essence is identical to existence.
B) Because the essence as such is necessarily realized and does not accept change.
C) Because the essence depends on a cause for its existence.
D) Because the essence is possible only with respect to future time.

Key Takeaway
Possibility cannot be attributed to the essence itself, whether the essence is existence or distinct from it, because essence either denies non-existence or is necessary in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that your own essence—your 'self'—has some sort of independent standing, as if you could possibly exist or not exist without reference to Allah? How does this passage's logic challenge that feeling?
?
If possibility is not in your essence itself, then what does that say about every moment of your existence? Are you not, in every instant, entirely dependent on a cause that is Allah? How would your life change if you truly lived with that awareness?
?
The passage shows that your essence is either existence (which denies non-existence) or necessary in itself. Since you are not necessary, your essence must be existence from Allah. Have you ever thanked Allah for the very fact that you exist right now, as if He is creating you anew each moment? Will you start doing that today?

Refuting Possibility Relative to Future Time
Refutation of the claim that possibility is relative to future time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that possibility cannot be relative to future time, as the future cannot be a genuine condition for present possibility.

Core Idea
Future time cannot ground possibility because the future is impossible to realize in the present.

The second objection attempts to escape the first refutation by appealing to future time. The author examines two interpretations of this claim and shows that both lead to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Begin with 'وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو أن الإمكان إنما يتحقق بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل'. Understand that the opponent now claims that possibility only applies when considering future time.
2
First interpretation: possibility occurs when future arrives
The first interpretation is that possibility actually occurs at the moment the future becomes present. The author refutes this because once the future arrives, it becomes present, and the same question arises about the present.
This is a circular argument.
3
Second interpretation: present possibility with respect to future
The second interpretation is that possibility exists now but is directed toward the future. The author refutes this by showing that the future cannot be realized in the present, so the condition for that possibility is impossible, and thus the thing cannot be possible under that condition.
This involves conditional impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the author, why is the second interpretation (that possibility occurred at present with respect to the future) false?
A) Because the future becomes present at the moment of arrival.
B) Because the future is impossible to realize in the present, so the condition is impossible.
C) Because possibility and impossibility can coexist.

?

?

?
This lesson teaches that possibility cannot depend on a future that cannot be realized now. Where in your faith or practice have you relied on 'future time' as a condition for your current certainty? Are you willing to act now based on what is actually possible today?

First Proof: Priority in Existence and Non-Existence
Razi presents the first proof that existence can be prioritized over non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of the present moment and sound to argue that priority can be conceived for existence as it is for non-existence.

Core Idea
Priority is conceivable for existence, just as it is for non-existence.

This lesson introduces Razi's first proof that existence can be prioritized over non-existence. Using the example of the present moment and a part of sound, he argues that since we can conceive priority for non-existence, we can also conceive it for existence. This establishes a key rational judgment in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from observation to analogy.
The argument is built on the empirical example of perishable things.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the present moment, the part of sound, and the two properties: receptivity to existence and priority of non-existence.
These elements are used to establish that non-existence is prioritized.
3
Understand the analogy
Razi asks: if we conceive priority for non-existence, why not for existence? The analogy is the core of the proof.
The proof does not assert actual priority of existence, only conceivability.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Consider that the proof only argues for conceivability, not actuality. This prevents overinterpreting the conclusion.
Refer to the misconception section below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك، أن هذا الآن (١) الحاضر، وهذا الجزء الحاضر من الصوت لاشك أنه قابل للوجود، إذ لو لم يكن قابلاً للوجود لما دخل (۲) في الوجود، ولا شك (٣) أن العدم أولى بها، إذ لو لم تحصل هذه الأولوية لما كان واجب الانقراض والانقضاء، فثبت في هذه الأشياء أنها قابلة للوجود، وقابلة للعدم، ثم إن العدم أولى بها من الوجود، فإذا عقلنا حصول هذه الأولوية في جانب العدم، فلم لا نعقل حصولها في جانب الوجود؟
So we say: The evidence that the matter is so is that this present moment (1) and this present part of sound are undoubtedly receptive to existence, for if they were not receptive to existence they would not have entered into existence, and there is no doubt (3) that non-existence is prioritized for them, for if this priority had not occurred they would not have been necessarily perishing and passing away. So it is established regarding these things that they are receptive to existence and receptive to non-existence, then non-existence is prioritized for them over existence. So if we conceive this priority on the side of non-existence, why do we not conceive it on the side of existence?
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the conceivability of priority (al-awlawiyya) in existence. The author begins by stating the proof: the present moment and the present part of sound are undoubtedly possible (qābil li-l-wujūd) because they exist, but they are also necessarily perishable, which shows that non-existence is prioritized for them. Since we can conceive such priority for non-existence, by analogy we can conceive it for existence as well. The argument relies on empirical observation of priority of non-existence to infer the conceivability of priority of existence.

Key Terms
الأولوية (al-awlawiyya)
الممكن (al-mumkin)
الوجود (al-wujud)
العدم (al-‘adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that existence is actually prioritized for all things.
Correction: The proof only argues that priority for existence is conceivable, not that it actually occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the first proof?
A) The present moment is necessarily existent.
B) Priority can be conceived for existence just as it is for non-existence.
C) Non-existence is always prioritized over existence.
D) Existence cannot be prioritized.

Key Takeaway
Priority for existence is conceivable, just as priority for non-existence is conceivable, based on the example of perishable things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God has priority of existence, but do you live as if anything else—your health, wealth, or relationships—has priority over God? What would change today if you truly internalized that only God's existence is necessary?
?
The proof uses the example of a passing sound. Think of a moment today when you heard something that distracted you from remembrance of God. That sound is gone. How can you use the temporary nature of everything to make your heart cling to the Eternal?
?
If priority of existence can be conceived, does that make you realize that God's choice to create you is not random but a deliberate priority? Does knowing that God prioritized your existence make you more grateful and obedient? If not, what is holding you back?

Third Proof: Philosophers' Division of Possibles into Majority, Minority, and Equal
Razi cites philosophers' consensus on three categories of possibles to establish priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of possibles into three categories (majority, minority, equal) and how this classification supports the concept of priority without implying necessity.

Core Idea
Priority does not mean necessity: possibles can be majority, minority, or equal in tendency.

Razi presents a third proof by appealing to the consensus of philosophers. They divide possibles not based on actual frequency but on conceptual priority. This division shows that priority is a legitimate concept even when non-existence remains possible. The example of earth's natural tendency illustrates this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: classification, definition, and example.
The passage is concise but packs three key ideas.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three divisions: majority, minority, equal. Understand how each is defined in terms of priority and possibility.
Majority means existence is prioritized but non-existence is still possible.
3
Understand the example
Reflect on the example of earth's nature to be at the center on a majority basis. Water can be thrown upward, showing that priority does not mean perpetuity.
This example anchors the abstract definition.
4
Appreciate the philosophical consensus
Note that Razi uses the agreement of philosophers as a premise. This consensus shows that priority is a recognized rational concept.
The consensus is sufficient to establish the validity of the question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن الفلاسفة اتفقوا على أن الممكنات على ثلاثة أقسام : منها أكثرية ومنها أقلية ومنها متساوية ، ولا معنى للأكثري إلا ما يكون الوجود أولى به ، مع أنه لا يمتنع أن يبقى على العدم، ولا معنى للأقلي ، إلا ما يكون العدم أولى به ، مع أنه لا يمتنع أن يصير موجودا ، وإذا كان هذا حكما متفقا عليه بين الفلاسفة كان كافيا في تقرير ذلك السؤال ، ألا ترى أنهم قالوا : طبيعة الأرض الحصول في الوسط على سبيل الأكثرية ، لا على سبيل الدوام ، لأنها قد تصير ممنوعة عن إيجاد ذلك ، على سبيل القسر ، مثل المدرة المرمية إلى فوق .
The third aspect: The philosophers agreed that possibles are of three divisions: some are majority-occurring, some are minority-occurring, and some are equal. The meaning of 'majority' is only that existence is prioritized for it, while it is not impossible for it to remain in non-existence. And the meaning of 'minority' is only that non-existence is prioritized for it, while it is not impossible for it to become existent. So if this is a judgment agreed upon among the philosophers, it is sufficient in establishing that question. Do you not see that they said: The nature of earth is to be in the center on a majority basis, not on a perpetual basis, because it may become prevented from effecting that by force, like a clod thrown upward.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which appeals to the consensus of philosophers. They divide possibles into three categories: majority (akthari), minority (aqalli), and equal (mutasāwiya). For majority, existence is prioritized but non-existence is possible; for minority, non-existence is prioritized but existence is possible. Since philosophers accept this classification, priority is a valid concept. The example of earth's natural tendency to be at the center, but only on a majority basis (it can be forced upward), illustrates that priority does not mean necessity.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة (al-falasifa)
الممكنات (al-mumkinat)
الأكثري (al-akthari)
الأقلي (al-aqalli)
المتساوية (al-mutasawiya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'majority' implies that existence is necessary or always occurs.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that for majority, non-existence remains possible. Priority does not entail necessity.

Checkpoint

C) Existence and non-existence are equal.
D) Non-existence is prioritized.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' consensus on dividing possibles into majority, minority, and equal shows that priority is a valid rational concept even when the opposite outcome remains possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your worship and obedience to Allah as a 'majority' habit—something you do often but can neglect—or as a perpetual necessity that cannot be abandoned? What does your actual record suggest about which category you truly believe your worship belongs to? This week, identify one act of obedience you have been treating as 'optional' and commit to doing it daily for a month, with the awareness that only the One who is Necessary (al-Wājib) deserves unwavering priority.
?
The philosophers' example of a clod thrown upward shows that even a natural 'priority' can be overridden by an external force. What forces in your life—passion, peer pressure, convenience—override your spiritual priorities? Are you aware that behind those forces lies a preponderator (murajjih) you have chosen? Sit alone today and ask: 'Am I the one who chooses my priorities, or am I a clod thrown by my surroundings?' Then resolve to be the murajjih of your own akthari (majority) acts.
?
Razi uses the consensus of philosophers to prove a point. But whose 'consensus' do you rely on when making moral or theological decisions? Is it the consensus of your community, your family, or your own desires? If the philosophers' consensus was enough to establish a rational concept, why is the consensus of revelation (ijma') not enough to guide your life? Write down one area where you follow popular opinion over divine command, and actively seek to realign it this week.

Fourth Proof: Possible Quiddities Necessitate a Specific Side
Razi refutes two possibilities to prove that quiddities necessitate a specific side.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi proves that each possible quiddity has a specific inclination towards either existence or non-existence, establishing the concept of priority (awlawiyya).

Core Idea
Every possible quiddity inherently points to one specific side – existence or non-existence.

In this proof, Razi turns to the very nature of possible things – quiddities that can be or not be. He argues that such quiddities must have a built-in disposition toward either existence or non-existence, not merely being neutral. This leads to the concept of 'priority' which is central to later arguments about causation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three possibilities
Read the passage and identify the three logical options Razi lists for how a possible quiddity relates to existence and non-existence.
The options are: no necessity, unspecified necessity, specific necessity.
2
Refute the first option
See why the quiddity cannot be completely neutral – if it necessitated neither, it would be devoid of both existence and non-existence, which is impossible.
3
Refute the second option
Grasp why 'one without specification' cannot exist: an actual existent must be determinate, not indeterminate.
The passage says 'it is impossible for something to occur in existence while it is in itself not a specific thing'.
4
Conclude the third option
Recognize that the quiddity must necessitate a specific side, establishing priority (awlawiyya).
This priority is the foundation for further arguments about causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إن الماهيات الممكنة لا شك أنها قابلة للوجود والعدم، فهذه الماهيات إما أن لا تكون مقتضية الحصول هذين الأمرين ، وإما أن تكون مقتضية لحصول أحدهما لا بعينه، وإما أن تكون مقتضية لحصول أحدهما بعينه، والأول باطل، وإلا لزم خلو هذه الماهيات عن الوجود والعدم معا وهو محال، والثاني أيضا باطل لأن أحدهما لا بعينه يمتنع دخوله في الوجود، لأن من المحال أن يحصل في الوجود شيء، مع أنه في نفسه لا يكون شيئا معينا، بل يكون هو في نفسه إما هذا وإما ذاك، وما يكون ممتنع الوجود في نفسه، يمتنع أن يكون وجوده معلولا لوجود غيره، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ثبت أن هذه الماهيات الممكنة تقتضي إما الوجود بعينه، وإما العدم بعينه، مع أن الطرف الثاني يكون غير ممتنع الحصول فثبت بهذا البرهان : أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحصول هذه الأولوية
The fourth aspect: The possible quiddities are undoubtedly receptive to existence and non-existence. These quiddities are either not necessitating the occurrence of these two matters, or they necessitate the occurrence of one of them without specification, or they necessitate the occurrence of one of them specifically. The first is false, otherwise it would necessitate the quiddities being devoid of both existence and non-existence together, which is impossible. The second is also false, because one of them without specification cannot enter into existence, for it is impossible for something to occur in existence while it is in itself not a specific thing, but rather it is in itself either this or that; and what is impossible of existence in itself cannot have its existence caused by the existence of another. So since these two divisions are false, it is established that these possible quiddities necessitate either existence specifically or non-existence specifically, while the other side is not impossible of occurrence. So it is established by this proof that it is necessary to acknowledge the occurrence of this priority.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, a logical disjunction regarding possible quiddities. The author considers three possibilities: (1) the quiddity does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, (2) it necessitates one without specification, (3) it necessitates one specifically. The first is impossible because it would imply the quiddity is devoid of both, which is impossible. The second is impossible because an indeterminate existent cannot enter existence; an existent must be determinate. Therefore, the third is true: the quiddity necessitates either existence or non-existence specifically. This means that for each possible quiddity, there is a priority for one side over the other. Thus, priority is established.

الوجود (al-wujud)
العدم (al-‘adam)
المقتضية (al-muqtaḍiya)
الأولوية (al-awlawiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that quiddities are actualized or that existence is predetermined.
Correction: The proof is about the intrinsic nature of quiddities before existence; it establishes a priority or inclination, not actual determination.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, why is the second possibility (necessitating one without specification) considered impossible?
A) Because it would make the quiddity indeterminate.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
C) Because it contradicts the philosophers' division.
D) Because it requires a cause.

Key Takeaway
Possible quiddities inherently possess a specific inclination towards either existence or non-existence, which Razi calls priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you living in accordance with the priority your Creator intended for you – towards obedience and eternal life – or are you inclined toward negligence and spiritual non-existence?
?
Razi argues that an indeterminate 'one without specification' cannot exist. In what areas of your life have you tried to remain neutral or non-committal about truth, avoiding the specific side? How will you now choose to be decisive for what is right?
?
This proof establishes that quiddities necessarily have a specific priority. Reflect: if your soul has an inherent priority toward good or evil, which one do you feed and actualize? What concrete step will you take today to align your actions with the priority that pleases Allah?

Proof of Equal Relation to Existence and Non-Existence
The author presents the first argument that a contingent essence has no inherent preponderance for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that a contingent essence must have an equal relation to existence and non-existence, and why any claim of inherent preponderance leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
A contingent essence has no inherent preference; its existence requires an external cause.

This lesson presents the first proof that an essence capable of existence and non-existence must be equally related to both. The argument shows that if there were an inherent preponderance, it would either make the essence necessary or lead to a preponderance without a preponderant, both of which are impossible. This proof is foundational for establishing that contingent beings need an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: the proof is presented as the first aspect (al-awwal).
2
Understand the two main cases
The proof considers an essence with all factors that could give it a preponderance. Then it asks: is non-existence valid given those factors? If not, the essence would be necessary (contradiction). If yes, then we examine the cause of non-existence.
This is a dichotomous argument (qismat al-taqsīm).
3
Analyze the sub-case of dependence
If non-existence depends on a nullifying cause, then the preponderance for existence only holds when that cause is absent. This means the assumed factors are insufficient for preponderance—a contradiction.
The author calls this a 'khalaf' (contradiction).
4
Analyze the sub-case of independence
If non-existence does not depend on a cause, then the preponderance exists both when the thing exists and when it does not. This makes the relation equal, so specifying existence over non-existence would be a preponderance without a preponderant (impossible).
This is a final reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that any claim of inherent preponderance is impossible. Therefore, a contingent essence must have equal relation to existence and non-existence, which implies it needs an external preponderant to exist.
This is a key step in the argument for a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الجواب على السؤال الأول : إنما يظهر إذا أقمنا البرهان القاطع على أن الماهية ، إذا كانت قابلة للعدم والوجود ، فإنه يجب أن تكون نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى تلك الماهية على السوية ، وأنه يمتنع أن يكون أحد الطرفين أولى بها ، والذي يدل على أن الأمر كما ذكرناه وجوه : - الأول : إنا نقول الماهية إذا اعتبرناها مع جميع الأمور المعتبرة في حصول تلك الأولوية ، فإما أن يكون العدم عليها حال حصول تلك الأولوية صحيحا ، أو لا يكون العدم عليها ، حال حصول تلك الأولوية صحيحا ، فإن لم يصح العدم مع حصول تلك الأولوية الذاتية، كان ذلك واجبا لذاته لا ممكنا لذاته ، وإن صح طريان العدم عليها ، فطريان العدم عليها حال حصول تلك الأمور المعتبرة في حصول تلك الأولوية ، إما أن يتوقف على حصول السبب المعدم المبطل ، أولا يتوقف على حصوله فإن توقف عليه فحينئذ لا تحصل أولوية الوجود إلا مع عدم ذلك السبب المعدم ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالحاصل قبل هذا الفيد ما كان كافيا في حصول تلك الأولوية ، وقد فرضنا أن الأمر كذلك . هذا خلف . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن لا يتوقف عدم ذلك الشيء على حصول السبب المعدم المبطل ، فعلى هذا التقدير تكون تلك الأولوية حاصلة تارة مع الوجود ، وأخرى مع العدم، فنسبة تلك الأولوية إلى الوقتين على السوية ، واختصاص أحد ذينك الوقتين بالوجود والآخر بالعدم ، يكون ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح ، وهو محال .
And know that the answer to the first question only becomes evident when we establish the decisive proof that the essence (al-māhiyya), if it is receptive to non-existence and existence, then the relation of each of the two to that essence must be equal, and it is impossible that one of the two sides is more appropriate to it. That which indicates that the matter is as we have mentioned are aspects: First: We say: The essence, when we consider it together with all the matters considered in the occurrence of that preponderance, then either non-existence is valid for it in the state of the occurrence of that preponderance, or non-existence is not valid for it in the state of the occurrence of that preponderance. If non-existence is not valid with the occurrence of that essential preponderance, then it would be necessary by itself (wājib bi-dhātihī), not possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhātihī). And if the occurrence of non-existence upon it is valid, then the occurrence of non-existence upon it in the state of the occurrence of those matters considered in the occurrence of that preponderance either depends on the occurrence of the cause that nullifies and removes, or does not depend on its occurrence. If it depends on it, then there is no preponderance of existence except with the absence of that nullifying cause, and on this assumption what is obtained before this input is not sufficient for the occurrence of that preponderance, and we have assumed the matter to be so. This is a contradiction (khalaf). As for the second division: that the non-existence of that thing does not depend on the occurrence of the nullifying cause, then on this assumption that preponderance occurs sometimes with existence, and other times with non-existence. So the relation of that preponderance to the two times is equal, and the specification of one of those two times with existence and the other with non-existence would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the equally possible over the other without a preponderance, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the first question by proving that an essence (māhiyya) capable of existence and non-existence must have an equal relation to both; any claim of inherent preponderance leads to absurdity. The author presents the first proof (al-awwal) as follows: Consider the essence along with all factors that could allegedly give it a preponderance for existence. Two cases: (A) If non-existence is not valid for it given that preponderance, then the essence would be necessary by itself (wājib li-dhātihī), contradicting its assumed contingency. (B) If non-existence is valid, then its occurrence either depends on a nullifying cause or not. If it depends, then the preponderance for existence is only present when that cause is absent, meaning the assumed factors alone are insufficient for preponderance—a contradiction (khalaf). If it does not depend, then the preponderance is present both when the thing exists and when it does not, making the relation equal; thus the specification of existence over non-existence would be a preponderance without a preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ), which is impossible. Hence, the claim of an inherent preponderance is false, and the essence must be equally related to existence and non-existence.

العدم
الوجود
أولوية
السبب المعدم
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is directly about God proving His necessity.
Correction: The proof is about the nature of contingent essences. It shows that contingency implies equal possibility, which is a foundation for proving the need for an external preponderant, ultimately leading to the existence of a necessary being.

Checkpoint
In the proof, what happens if non-existence is not valid for an essence given an alleged inherent preponderance?
A) The essence becomes impossible.
B) The essence becomes necessary by itself.
C) The essence remains contingent.
D) The preponderance is confirmed.

Key Takeaway
A contingent essence cannot have an inherent preference for existence or non-existence; its existence depends on an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof forces you to admit that nothing you are or have—your intellect, character, or circumstances—is inherently sufficient for your existence. What part of your life do you secretly rely on as if it were self-sufficient, and how will you uproot that reliance today?
?
If every contingent thing needs an external cause to tip the scale from non-existence to existence, then your very existence at this moment is a gift given by a Preponderant. How does knowing that your essence had no claim to existence change the way you thank God for being alive right now?
?
The argument says that a supposed inherent preponderance would either make a thing necessary or lead to an impossible contradiction. Search your heart: are you living as if you are 'necessary' in some role—indispensable, self-caused? What concrete step will you take tonight to acknowledge your complete contingency before God?

The First Proof: Essence Has No Priority for Existence
Sanusi presents the first proof against priority via a case analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that an essence receptive to both existence and non-existence must relate equally to both, and that any notion of priority leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
Priority would make the essence either necessary or impossible; equal relation is the only consistent option.

Sanusi presents a decisive proof against the idea that an essence can have a natural priority for existence over non-existence. By carefully analyzing the conditions for priority, he derives contradictions that show the relation must be equal. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the goal: refute priority
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states that a decisive proof is needed to show that essence cannot have one side preferred. This sets the target of the argument.
The proof assumes the essence is possible (receptive to both).
2
First branch: non-existence impossible
Follow the first case: if with all the conditions for priority, non-existence is impossible, then the essence would be necessary, but we started with a possible essence. This yields a contradiction.
3
Second branch: non-existence possible
Now assume non-existence is possible despite the priority conditions. Then examine whether its occurrence depends on a destructive cause. Trace both subcases and see why each leads to contradiction.
Pay attention to the two subcases: dependent and independent.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The last subcase shows that if non-existence does not depend on a cause, then priority would hold in both existence and non-existence equally, leading to preponderance without a preponderant. This impossible result completes the proof.
The argument shows that no coherent scenario allows priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذين السؤالين . واعلم أن الجواب على السؤال الأول : إنما يظهر إذا أقمنا البرهان القاطع على أن الماهية ، إذا كانت قابلة للعدم والوجود ، فإنه يجب أن تكون نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى تلك الماهية على السوية وأنه يمتنع أن يكون أحد الطرفين أولى بها ، والذي يدل على أن الأمر كما ذكرناه وجوه : - الأول : إنا نقول الماهية إذا اعتبرناها مع جميع الأمور المعتبرة في حصول تلك الأولوية ، فإما أن يكون العدم عليها حال حصول تلك الأولوية صحيحا ، أو لا يكون العدم عليها ، حال حصول تلك الأولوية صحيحا ، فإن لم يصح العدم مع حصول تلك الأولوية الذاتية، كان ذلك واجبا لذاته لا ممكنا لذاته ، وإن صح طريان العدم عليها ، فطريان العدم عليها حال حصول تلك الأمور المعتبرة في حصول تلك الأولوية ، إما أن يتوقف على حصول السبب المعدم المبطل ، أولا يتوقف على حصوله فإن توقف عليه فحينئذ لا تحصل أولوية الوجود إلا مع عدم ذلك السبب المعدم ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالحاصل قبل هذا الفيد ما كان كافيا في حصول تلك الأولوية ، وقد فرضنا أن الأمر كذلك . هذا خلف وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن لا يتوقف عدم ذلك الشيء على حصول السبب المعدم المبطل ، فعلى هذا التقدير تكون تلك الأولوية حاصلة تارة مع الوجود ، وأخرى مع العدم، فنسبة تلك الأولوية إلى الوقتين على السوية ، واختصاص أحد ذينك الوقتين بالوجود والآخر بالعدم ، يكون ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح ، وهو محال .
This completes the discussion in establishing these two questions. Know that the answer to the first question only becomes clear when we present the decisive proof that the essence, when it is receptive to non-existence and existence, must have the relation of each one of them to that essence on an equal footing, and that it is impossible for one side to have priority over it. That which indicates that the matter is as we have mentioned are aspects: The first: We say: The essence, if we consider it together with all the matters considered in the occurrence of that priority, then either non-existence upon it while that priority occurs is valid, or non-existence upon it while that priority occurs is not valid. If non-existence is not valid with the occurrence of that essential priority, then it would be necessary in itself, not possible in itself. And if the advent of non-existence upon it is valid, then the advent of non-existence upon it while those matters considered in the occurrence of that priority are present is either dependent on the occurrence of the destructive cause or not dependent on its occurrence. If it is dependent on it, then the priority of existence is only obtained with the absence of that destructive cause, and on this assumption, what was obtained before this was not sufficient for the occurrence of that priority, and we have assumed the matter to be so. This is a contradiction. As for the second division: that the non-existence of that thing is not dependent on the occurrence of the destructive cause, then on this assumption that priority is obtained sometimes with existence and sometimes with non-existence. So the relation of that priority to the two times is equal, and the specification of one of those times with existence and the other with non-existence would be a preponderance of one side of the possible equal being over the other without a preponderant, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a proof. The author begins by stating that the answer to the first question (presumably about priority) only becomes clear after establishing a decisive proof that an essence which is receptive to both existence and non-existence must have an equal relation to both. Then he presents the first argument: Consider the essence along with all the conditions that would give it priority. Either non-existence is possible given that priority or not. If non-existence is not possible, then the essence would be necessary in itself, not possible. If non-existence is possible, then its occurrence either depends on a destructive cause or not. If it depends, then the priority of existence only holds in the absence of that cause, meaning the prior conditions were insufficient, a contradiction. If it does not depend, then the priority would be present in both existence and non-existence equally, so the actual assignment of existence at one time and non-existence at another would be a preponderance without a preponderant, which is impossible. Thus the notion of priority is refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes what it tries to prove (that essence has no priority), but it actually starts from the concept of priority and derives contradictions.
Correction: The proof begins by assuming priority conditions and then shows that both possibilities lead to absurdity, so priority is impossible.

Checkpoint

D) The essence becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that any notion of priority for existence or non-existence in a possible essence leads to contradictions; therefore the relation must be equal.

?

Do you truly live as if your existence depends on God every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you internalized that your essence has no inherent priority for existence?
?
The proof shows that any attempt to claim priority for existence leads to contradictions. In your own life, do you ever feel that you 'deserve' existence or blessings as if you have a natural right? How does this proof challenge that assumption?
?
This argument forces you to admit that the existence of everything possible requires an external preponderant. Have you ever really grasped that your own existence at this very moment is a continuous gift from the Necessary Being? Write one concrete way you will remind yourself of this dependence today.

The Second Proof: Priority Entails Preponderance Without a Preponderant
A second proof against priority leading to impossibility and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against priority and why it shows that preponderance without a preponderant is impossible.

Core Idea
Priority without a cause is logically impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a second rigorous proof against the claim that one side of a possible being could be prior without a cause. He builds on the established principle that a possible being with equal sides cannot have one side preponderant without a preponderant. The argument then shows that assuming priority leads to a contradiction, forcing the necessity of the preponderant side.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation carefully. Notice the logical flow and key terms.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'preponderant' (راجح) and 'preponderate' (مرجوح). Understand their meanings in this context.
These are crucial for the proof.
3
Understand the logical flow
Walk through the argument step by step: from the established principle, to the assumption of priority, to the impossibility of the preponderate side, to the necessity of the preponderant side.
Ask yourself why the preponderate side is more impossible.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Think about how this argument refutes the claim of priority. Why does it show that priority cannot exist without a preponderant?
This connects to the broader theme of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : على فساد القول بهذه الأولوية : إنا قد دللنا على أن الممكن المتساوي، يمتنع رجحان أحد طرفيه على الآخر من غير مرجح ، إذا ثبت هذا . فنقول : بتقدير أن يكون أحد الطرفين راجحا كان الطرف الثاني مرجوحا ، ولما كان حصول الرجحان حال الاستواء ممتنعا ، كان حصوله حال حصول المرجوحية أولى بالامتناع ، وإذا كان الطرف المرجوح حال كونه مرجوحاً ممتنع الحصول، كان الطرف الراجح واجب الحصول ، ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن طرف النقيض .
The second proof: against the invalidity of the claim of this priority: We have already demonstrated that the possible equal being cannot have one of its sides preponderant over the other without a preponderant. When this is established, we say: Assuming that one side is preponderant, the second side is preponderate. Since the occurrence of preponderance in a state of equality is impossible, its occurrence in a state of being preponderate is more deserving of impossibility. And when the preponderate side, while being preponderate, is impossible to obtain, then the preponderant side is necessary to obtain, necessarily because there is no exit from the two contradictories.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a second proof. The author first reminds that it has been established that a possible being with equal sides cannot have one side preponderant without a preponderant. Then he argues: If one side is preponderant (rajih), the other side is preponderate (marjuh). Preponderance while in a state of equality is impossible; therefore preponderance while in a state of being preponderate is even more impossible. And if the preponderate side, while being preponderate, is impossible to obtain, then the preponderant side must be necessary to obtain, because the two contradictories (existence and non-existence) exhaust all possibilities. Hence priority is impossible.

Key Terms
ممكن متساوي
راجح
مرجوح
امتناع
وجوب
طرف النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular.
Correction: It builds on an already established principle, so it is not circular. It shows that assuming priority leads to an impossibility, thereby refuting the claim.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, what is the relationship between the preponderant side and the preponderate side?
A) They are both equally possible.
B) If one is preponderant, the other is preponderate.
C) The preponderant side is impossible.
D) The preponderate side becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that assuming priority leads to an impossibility, so the preponderant side must be necessary — thus priority cannot exist without a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that no side can become preponderant without a preponderant. When you look at your own life, do you ever attribute successes or failures to 'luck' or 'chance'—effectively assuming preponderance without a cause? How does this argument expose that assumption as irrational, and what does it demand of your trust in Allah's decree?
?
Consider a situation where you felt that your good deeds were the 'preponderant' factor for a blessing. Does this proof not show that without Allah as the true preponderant, your good deeds are themselves possible and need a cause to be effective? How must you revise your understanding of reliance (tawakkul) in light of this truth?
?
The proof concludes that if one side is impossible, the other is necessary. In moments of despair or indecision, do you live as though multiple outcomes are equally possible without a cause? This proof forces you to accept that whatever occurs is necessitated by its preponderant. Are you ready to embrace that reality with contentment (rida)?
